The One as the Only Efficient Cause
Author argues that only a specified cause can be efficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the only possible efficient cause of contingent beings must be a specific, determinate entity (the One).

Core Idea
Contingency requires a specific, self-existent cause; an unspecified cause cannot exist.

In this passage, the author presents a concise rational argument to establish that no efficient cause exists except the One (Allah). The argument hinges on the impossibility of an indeterminate cause existing in itself. Let's follow the logical steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Let the author's words sink in.
Notice the logical connectors: 'والثاني باطل' etc.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage begins by implying two possibilities for the efficient cause: either it is unspecified (غير معين) or specified (معين). Find where this is stated.
The first alternative is not explicitly stated in this excerpt, but it is implied from the refutation.
3
Understand why the unspecified cause is impossible
The author argues that a cause not specified in its essence cannot exist in itself. Trace the logic: whatever exists is specified; so no specification = no existence.
This is a key step in the proof.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since contingency requires a specified cause, every contingent must be attributed to that cause. Hence only the One can be the efficient cause.
The conclusion is stated twice for emphasis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى مؤثر غير معين. والثاني باطل، لأن المؤثر الذي لا يكون معيناً في ذاته امتنع وجوده في نفسه لأن كل ما كان موجوداً في نفسه فهو معين في ذاته، وما لا يكون معيناً في ذاته، امتنع كونه موجوداً في ذاته، وما لا وجود له في ذاته، امتنع احتياج غيره في الوجود إليه. فثبت: أن الإمكان لا يحوج إلا إلى شيء معين. فوجب إسناد كل ممكن إليه. فثبت: أنه لا مؤثر إلا الواحد. فهذا تقرير هذا القول.
to an unspecified determiner. And the second is false, because the determiner that is not specified in its essence is impossible to exist in itself, because everything that exists in itself is specified in its essence, and that which is not specified in its essence is impossible to exist in itself, and that which has no existence in itself is impossible for something else to need it for existence. So it is established that contingency does not necessitate anything except a specified thing. So it is obligatory to attribute every contingent thing to it. So it is established that there is no determiner except the One. That is the exposition of this statement.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) that the only true efficient cause (mu'aththir) is Allah, the One. The author begins by stating the two possibilities: either the contingent needs an unspecified cause (mu'aththir ghayr mu'ayyan) or a specific one. The second is false because a cause that is not specified in its essence cannot exist in itself; only a specific entity can exist in itself. Therefore, contingency (imkān) can only depend on a specific, determinate cause. Hence, every possible thing is attributed to that specific cause, and thus there is no efficient cause except the One. The author concludes the proof with the remark 'That is the exposition of this statement.'

Key Terms
مؤثر (mu'aththir)
إمكان (imkān)
معين (mu'ayyan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes what it wants to prove.
Correction: The argument is not circular. It starts from the nature of contingency and existence, deducing that a cause must be determinate. It then identifies that determinate cause as the One, not by assumption but by necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why can an unspecified cause not be the efficient cause?
A) Because contingency requires a cause that is also contingent.
B) Because an unspecified cause cannot exist in itself.
C) Because the cause must be the One.
D) Because the cause must be observable.

Key Takeaway
The only efficient cause must be a specific, self-existent entity, which is the One (Allah).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the successes or failures in your life, do you truly see only Allah as the One who determines them? Or are there people, circumstances, or your own efforts that you treat as independent causes? What would it take for you to shift that reliance entirely to Him?
?
The argument shows that an unspecified cause cannot exist. In your daily life, do you sometimes vaguely hope for 'something' to help you without turning specifically to Allah? How can you make your hope and trust specific to the One alone?
?
If every contingent moment of your existence is directly attributed to the One, then your next breath, your next thought, and your next action are entirely from Him. How does that change the way you approach your own responsibilities and choices? Do you feel accountable to Him for even your smallest intentions?

The Opponents' Second Argument Against Immaterial Beings
Presents the opponents' argument limiting existence to extended beings and their attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the opponents' argument that denies the existence of immaterial beings based on sense perception.

Core Idea
The opponents argue that only extended bodies and their attributes are known by sense, so immaterial beings cannot be proven.

Before we engage with the refutation, it is essential to grasp precisely what the opponents are claiming. Their argument rests on limiting knowledge to sense perception and then denying any need for a third category of existence. This lesson presents that argument in its raw form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that the opponents present a chain of reasoning.
2
Identify the two categories accepted by the opponents
The opponents say sense perception only proves the existence of extended bodies and the attributes that inhere in them. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the rule for the third category
They argue that a third category (immaterial beings) can only be known if one of the first two depends upon it. See how they apply this rule.
4
Trace the conclusion
Since God's existence is already accepted and suffices to cause the extended world, they conclude no proof remains for any other immaterial being. Note the final claim that affirming without proof leads to follies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية لهم : قالوا : الحس دل على وجود المتحيز ، وعلى وجود الصفات القائمة به . وأما القسم الثالث ، فلا يمكن إثباته ، إلا لأجل افتقار أحد هذين القسمين إليه . لأن ما لا يكون العلم بوجوده بديهياً ، لم يجز المصير إلى إثباته ، إلا إذا قضى العقل بإحتياج ما علم وجوده إليه . إلا أنا لما اعترفنا بأن الإله تعالى غير متحيز ، ولا حال في المتحيز ، كان وجوده كافياً في وجود هذه المتحيزات ، وفي وجود الأعراض القائمة بها . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلم يبق على وجود موجود [ آخر) ] غير متحيز ، ولا حال في المتحيز : دليل . وما لا دليل عليه أصلاً ، كان القول بإثباته موجباً للجهالات

وأما الحجة الثانية : فجوابها : أن حاصلها ) يرجع إلى المطالبة بالدليل الذي يدل على ثبوت هذا القسم ، لأن المطالبة بدليل الثبوت غير ، وإقامة الدليل على النفي غير . فأين أحد البابين من الآخر ؟
The second argument for them: they said: The senses indicated the existence of the extended (al-mutahayyiz) and the existence of the attributes subsisting in it. As for the third category, it cannot be affirmed except due to the need of one of these two categories for it. For what is not known intuitively, it is not permissible to proceed to affirm it unless reason judges that what is known to exist needs it. However, since we acknowledge that God Most High is neither extended nor in an extended, His existence is sufficient for the existence of these extended beings and for the existence of the accidents subsisting in them. And if that is the case, no proof remains for the existence of another existent that is neither extended nor in an extended. And what has no proof at all, affirming it leads to follies.

As for the second argument: its answer is that its gist reduces to demanding the proof that indicates the existence of this category, because demanding a proof of existence is one thing, and adducing a proof for negation is another. So where is one of the two doors from the other?
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument from the opponents (likely atheists or materialists) and then provides a refutation. The argument begins by asserting that sense perception (al-hiss) only proves the existence of extended bodies (al-mutahayyiz) and the attributes or accidents that inhere in them. They then claim that a third category—immaterial beings—cannot be established except by demonstrating that one of the first two categories depends on it. The author counters by noting that since we already affirm God as non-extended and not inhering in an extended, His existence suffices for the existence of all extended beings and their accidents. Therefore, there is no need to posit another immaterial existent, and further proofs are lacking. The response clarifies that the opponent's demand is actually a request for a proof of existence, not a proof of non-existence, and conflating the two is a fallacy.

Key Terms
الحجة الثانية
المتحيز
القسم الثالث
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the opponents, which two categories does sense perception directly prove?
A) Extended bodies and immaterial beings
B) Extended bodies and their attributes
C) Attributes and immaterial beings
D) Only extended bodies

Key Takeaway
The opponents limit known existence to extended bodies and their attributes, and they argue that immaterial beings are unprovable unless needed by the first two categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the world around you, do you unconsciously limit 'real' existence to what you can see and touch? How does this lesson challenge you to expand your understanding of reality beyond the physical?
?
The opponents argue that God's existence alone is sufficient for everything else—meaning you need no other spiritual beings or intermediaries. Does your heart truly find sufficiency in God alone, or do you rely on other created things as if they were independent causes?
?
If you were to strip away every belief that cannot be proven by your senses or by necessity, what would remain of your faith? This argument from the opponents forces you to ask: what is the foundation of your certainty in the unseen?

Refutation of the Second Argument
Refutes the second argument by distinguishing proof of existence from proof of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key fallacy in the opponents' argument as identified by the author: confusing the demand for a proof of existence with a proof of non-existence.

Core Idea
Demanding proof of existence is not the same as proving non-existence.

Having presented the opponents' argument, the author now delivers a crisp refutation. He does not engage with the content of the argument directly but rather exposes a logical confusion: the opponents are asking for a proof that immaterial beings exist, which is a different task from proving that they do not exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the brevity and the key distinction the author makes.
2
Identify the two different tasks
The author distinguishes between 'demanding a proof of existence' and 'adducing a proof for negation'. Find these phrases in the text.
3
Understand the fallacy
Realize that the opponents' argument actually reduces to a demand for proof of existence, but they presented it as a proof of non-existence. This is the logical error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية لهم : قالوا : الحس دل على وجود المتحيز ، وعلى وجود الصفات القائمة به . وأما القسم الثالث ، فلا يمكن إثباته ، إلا لأجل افتقار أحد هذين القسمين إليه . لأن ما لا يكون العلم بوجوده بديهياً ، لم يجز المصير إلى إثباته ، إلا إذا قضى العقل بإحتياج ما علم وجوده إليه . إلا أنا لما اعترفنا بأن الإله تعالى غير متحيز ، ولا حال في المتحيز ، كان وجوده كافياً في وجود هذه المتحيزات ، وفي وجود الأعراض القائمة بها . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلم يبق على وجود موجود [ آخر) ] غير متحيز ، ولا حال في المتحيز : دليل . وما لا دليل عليه أصلاً ، كان القول بإثباته موجباً للجهالات

وأما الحجة الثانية : فجوابها : أن حاصلها ) يرجع إلى المطالبة بالدليل الذي يدل على ثبوت هذا القسم ، لأن المطالبة بدليل الثبوت غير ، وإقامة الدليل على النفي غير . فأين أحد البابين من الآخر ؟
The second argument for them: they said: The senses indicated the existence of the extended (al-mutahayyiz) and the existence of the attributes subsisting in it. As for the third category, it cannot be affirmed except due to the need of one of these two categories for it. For what is not known intuitively, it is not permissible to proceed to affirm it unless reason judges that what is known to exist needs it. However, since we acknowledge that God Most High is neither extended nor in an extended, His existence is sufficient for the existence of these extended beings and for the existence of the accidents subsisting in them. And if that is the case, no proof remains for the existence of another existent that is neither extended nor in an extended. And what has no proof at all, affirming it leads to follies.

As for the second argument: its answer is that its gist reduces to demanding the proof that indicates the existence of this category, because demanding a proof of existence is one thing, and adducing a proof for negation is another. So where is one of the two doors from the other?
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument from the opponents (likely atheists or materialists) and then provides a refutation. The argument begins by asserting that sense perception (al-hiss) only proves the existence of extended bodies (al-mutahayyiz) and the attributes or accidents that inhere in them. They then claim that a third category—immaterial beings—cannot be established except by demonstrating that one of the first two categories depends on it. The author counters by noting that since we already affirm God as non-extended and not inhering in an extended, His existence suffices for the existence of all extended beings and their accidents. Therefore, there is no need to posit another immaterial existent, and further proofs are lacking. The response clarifies that the opponent's demand is actually a request for a proof of existence, not a proof of non-existence, and conflating the two is a fallacy.

Key Terms

الدليل على النفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absence of proof for something's existence is proof of its non-existence.
Correction: Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence. Demanding a proof of existence is different from proving non-existence.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the main problem with the opponents' argument?
A) They deny the existence of the senses.
B) They demand a proof for existence instead of providing a proof for non-existence.
C) They claim that God is extended.
D) They accept the existence of a third category.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' argument conflates two distinct logical tasks: demanding a proof for existence and providing a proof for non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a belief about the unseen simply because you couldn't find proof for it? How does this lesson challenge that attitude, and what would it mean to take the first step toward accepting what you cannot prove but might be true?
?
In what area of your life—your faith, relationships, or personal struggles—are you currently demanding proof of existence when you should instead be asking whether there is any proof of non-existence? What would change if you shifted your question?
?
Are there truths about Allah that you have rejected or doubted because they seemed unproven? What would it cost you to admit that your lack of evidence does not make them false, and to begin sincerely seeking the proofs that exist?

Objection and Answer on Knowledge of the Self
Razi raises an objection about self-knowledge and resolves it via a key distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi formulates an objection about self-knowledge being self-evident yet disputed, and how he resolves it by distinguishing existential from quidditative knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge of your own existence is obvious; knowledge of your true nature requires demonstration.

This passage presents a classic objection in Islamic philosophy: if knowledge of the self is the most obvious thing, why are there so many disagreements? Razi formulates the objection rigorously and then offers a precise answer by distinguishing between two kinds of knowledge. Understanding this dialectic is key to grasping how kalam deals with foundational epistemological puzzles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then a story about Aristotle, then the author's own formulation, and finally the answer.
Pay attention to the logical flow from objection to answer.
2
Identify the objection's structure
The objection has two parts: an argument that self-knowledge is prior and more evident, and a consequence that any derivation would lead to a vicious circle. Can you find these parts?
The objection uses the concepts of taṣdīq (affirmation) and taṣawwur (conceptualization).
3
Understand the answer's distinction
The answer distinguishes between knowledge of the existence (wujūd) of the self and knowledge of its quiddity (māhiyya). Which one is the object of demonstration?
The distinction breaks the circularity.
4
Evaluate the resolution
Explain in your own words how this distinction resolves the objection. Does it fully address the puzzle?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : أعرف المعارف، وأظهر الظاهرات عند كل أحد : علمه بالشيء المشار إليه بقوله : أنا ، وهو نفسه المعينة ، وذاته المخصوصة ، ولما كان هذا أعرف المعلومات وأظهر المفهومات ، فكيف وقعت فيه هذه الإختلافات العظيمة ، والشبهات الغامضة ؟ ورأيت في الرسالة الموسومة بالتفاحة . وهي الرسالة المشتملة على المباحثات التي جرت عند ه أرسطاطاليس (۳) ، عند قربه من الموت. فقيل له : كيف يعقل أن يسأل الإنسان غيره عن حال نفسه ؟ فأجاب الحكيم : بأنه مثل سؤال المريض : الطبيب عن دائه ، وسؤال الأعمى من حوله : عن لونه . وأقول : : من تذكر تقرير هذا الإشكال كما ينبغي ، ثم نطلب الجواب أما تقرير الإشكال فهو أن نقول : أعرف الأشياء للإنسان نفسه والدليل عليه : أن علمه بغيره يتوقف على علمه بنفسه [ فإني إذا قلت في شيء من الأشياء : إني أعرفه . فهذا حكم على نفسي بكونها عارفة بذلك الشيء (٤) ] وكل تصديق فهو مسبوق بتصوّر الطرفين . ينتج : إني كل ما حكمت على نفسي بأني أعرف شيئاً من الأشياء، فإن معرفة نفسي بنفسي ، سابقة على معرفة ذلك الحكم . فثبت : أن علمي [ بنفسي ، سابق على علمي (١) ] بكل ما يغايرني . وإذا ثبت هذا ، ظهر أن علمي بنفسي ، أظهر من علمي بكل ما سواها. فلو توسلنا بمعرفة شيء آخر إلى معرفة النفس ، كان هذا من باب تعريف الأظهر الأجل، بالأعمق الأخفى . وإنه محال . وأيضاً : لما كان العلم بغير النفس ، مشروطاً بالعلم بالنفس ، فلو كانت معرفة النفس مستفادة من شيء آخر ، لزم الدور . وهو محال . والجواب : إن العلم بوجود النفس من حيث إنها شيء ما غير . والعلم بأنها ما هي على التفصيل والتعيين شيء آخر . والظاهر الجلي هو القسم الأول وهو غير المطلوب بالبرهان البتة ، وأما القسم الثاني فهو المطلوب بالبرهان [ ومعرفة سائر الأشياء (٢) ) غير موقوفة على معرفة النفس ، بحسب هذا الإعتبار (۳) فزال السؤال .
If someone says: The most known of known things and the most apparent of apparent things to everyone is his knowledge of the thing referred to by his saying 'I' (anā), which is his specific self and his particular essence. Since this is the most known of known things and the most clear of conceived things, how then did these great disagreements and obscure doubts occur concerning it? And I saw in the treatise known as 'Al-Tuffāḥa' (The Apple), which is the treatise containing the discussions that took place before Aristotle near his death. It was said to him: How can it be understood that a person asks another about his own state? The philosopher answered: It is like the sick person asking the doctor about his ailment, and the blind person asking those around him about his color. And I say: Whoever recalls the formulation of this objection properly, then seeks the answer. As for the formulation of the objection, it is that we say: The most known of things to a person is his self, and the evidence for it is that his knowledge of others depends on his knowledge of his self. For when I say about something: 'I know it,' this is a judgment about my self that it is knowing that thing. And every affirmation (taṣdīq) is preceded by a conceptualization (taṣawwur) of its two sides. The result is: Whenever I judge about my self that I know something, then my knowledge of my self by my self is prior to my knowledge of that judgment. Thus it is established that my knowledge of my self is prior to my knowledge of everything that is other than me. When this is established, it becomes clear that my knowledge of my self is more evident than my knowledge of everything else. So if we were to seek knowledge of the self through knowledge of something else, this would be like defining what is more evident and clearer by what is deeper and more hidden, and that is impossible. Moreover, since knowledge of what is other than the self is conditioned by knowledge of the self, if knowledge of the self were derived from something else, there would be a vicious circle (dawr), which is impossible. The answer: Knowledge of the existence (wujūd) of the self, inasmuch as it is something, is one thing; and knowledge of what it is in detail and specifically is another thing. The clear and evident is the first part, and it is not the object sought by demonstration (burhān) at all. As for the second part, it is what is sought by demonstration; and knowledge of other things is not dependent on knowledge of the self according to this consideration. Thus the objection falls.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning knowledge of the human self (nafs). The objector claims that the self is the most obvious thing to everyone, so how can there be disagreements about it? He references a story about Aristotle to illustrate that one can be ignorant of one's own state. The author then formulates the objection rigorously: Knowledge of the self is prior to and more evident than knowledge of anything else; therefore, it is impossible to derive knowledge of the self from something else, as that would involve a vicious circle (dawr). The answer distinguishes between two types of knowledge: (1) knowledge of the existence of the self as a bare fact, which is obvious and not in need of proof; and (2) knowledge of the quiddity (māhiyya) of the self in detail, which is what demonstration (burhān) seeks. Since knowledge of other things does not require knowledge of the self in the second sense, the alleged circularity is resolved. The entire exchange is a classic example of kalam dialectic, showing the importance of precise formulation and distinction in addressing philosophical puzzles.

Key Terms
نفس
برهان
وجود
ما هي
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection refutes all knowledge of the self, showing it is impossible.
Correction: The answer clarifies that only detailed quidditative knowledge is problematic; existential knowledge is obvious and not disputed.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction made in the answer to the objection?
A) Knowledge of self vs. knowledge of others
B) Knowledge of existence vs. knowledge of quiddity
C) Knowledge of the soul vs. knowledge of the body
D) Knowledge of particulars vs. knowledge of universals

Key Takeaway
The key to resolving the puzzle of self-knowledge is to distinguish between knowing that you exist (obvious) and knowing what you truly are (requires proof).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know yourself, but do you know what you truly are beyond your fleeting desires and thoughts? How often do you seek to understand your own soul's nature, or do you settle for the mere awareness that you exist?
?
If knowledge of your own essence is not automatic, then what are you actively doing to uncover it? Are you using the demonstration (burhān) that scholars prescribe, or do you rely only on your immediate feelings?
?
The objection shows that confusion arises from mixing two kinds of knowledge. In your own life, where might you be confusing what is obvious (your existence) with what requires deep inquiry (your purpose and reality)? What step will you take today to move from awareness to understanding?

Why the Essence Cannot Be Proven by Demonstration
Razi enumerates two arguments against proving essence through demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's twofold argument that the essence of a thing cannot be known through demonstrative proof because no middle term can connect a thing to itself, and any candidate mediator would be less known than the essence.

Core Idea
A thing's own essence is known directly, not through proof.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi tackles a foundational question in epistemology and metaphysics: can we prove the essence of something using logical demonstration? He argues that it is impossible, and his reasoning reveals deep principles about the structure of proof and what can be known immediately. As you read, pay attention to the two distinct arguments he offers and their shared conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of its flow. Don't worry about understanding every word; just notice the rhythm and key terms like 'حد أوسط' and 'برهان'.
The passage is concise; each sentence carries weight.
2
Understand the first argument
Razi's first argument focuses on the impossibility of a middle term between a thing and its essence. A demonstration requires a middle term that links subject and predicate, but here the subject and predicate are identical (the thing and its essence). No third term can fit between them.
Think of a demonstration like a bridge: you need something to connect two banks, but if the banks are the same point, no bridge is needed or possible.
3
Examine the second argument
Even if a mediator were hypothetically possible, the second argument shows that the mediator would be less known than the essence itself. Since proof must proceed from the better known to the less known, this direction is reversed, making the proof invalid.
This is a powerful point: the essence is primary and self-evident; anything else depends on it.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The two arguments together force a binary: either the essence is utterly unknowable, or it is known necessarily (by immediate intuition). Razi implies the latter is correct, setting the stage for later discussions of self-evident knowledge.
Notice that Razi does not explicitly choose here; he leaves the two options open, but the context suggests necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بينهما ، أو ثالث يتخللهما. وإذا كان حصول الحد الأوسط ههنا ممتنعاً : كان طلب هذا البحث ممتنعاً من لذاته. وأيضاً : فبتقدير أن يحصل بين الشيء وبين عين ذاته متوسطاً ، إلا أنه المحال أن يكون ثبوت ذلك المغاير لذلك الشيء ، أعرف من ثبوت ذاته. فثبت بهذين الوجهين : أنه يمتنع أن يكون هذا المطلوب : مطلوباً بالدليل . فبقي فيه أحد الأمرين. إما أن يكون مأيوساً من إثباته مطلقاً . وإما أن يكون معلوماً بالضرورة.
Between the two, or a third that mediates between them. And if obtaining the middle term here is impossible, then seeking this inquiry is impossible in itself. Also: Even if it were supposed that a mediator occurs between a thing and its very essence, it would still be impossible for the existence of that other (i.e., the mediator) to be more known than the existence of the essence itself. Thus it is established by these two aspects that it is impossible for this object of seeking to be sought through demonstration. So there remain for it one of two possibilities: either it is hopeless of being proven absolutely, or it is known necessarily.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that knowledge of a thing's essence cannot be obtained through demonstrative reasoning (burhān). The argument is twofold. First, the author states that a demonstrative proof requires a middle term (ḥadd al-awsaṭ) that links the subject and predicate. If the object of inquiry is the very essence of a thing, there is no middle term that can mediate between the thing and itself, because that would require a third thing between a thing and its own identity, which is impossible. Second, even if such a mediator were possible, the existence of that mediator would be less known than the existence of the essence itself, since the essence is primary. Therefore, proving the essence through demonstration is impossible. The author concludes that the sought knowledge of the essence must be either completely hopeless and unattainable (ma’yūs) or known by necessity (ḍarūra). The implication is that it is the latter—the essence is known intuitively, not through proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that any unknown truth can be proven through logical demonstration, including the essence of a thing.
Correction: Razi shows that the essence itself cannot be proven because it is the foundation of all knowledge about that thing; any proof would require a middle term that cannot exist between a thing and itself.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that seeking knowledge of a thing's essence through demonstration is impossible?
A) Because the essence is too complex to be understood.
B) Because there is no middle term that can connect a thing to its own essence.
C) Because demonstration only works for material things.
D) Because the essence is known only through scripture.

Key Takeaway
The essence of a thing cannot be proven through demonstration because no middle term can connect a thing to itself; it must be known directly or not at all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If knowledge of His essence is not through proof but through necessity, how does that change the way you seek nearness to Him? Are you relying more on intellectual arguments or on direct consciousness of Him in your daily life?
?
This passage suggests that the essence of a thing is either known necessarily or hopelessly unknown. Do you treat your knowledge of Allah as a certainty that requires no proof, or do you still seek demonstrations? What is the state of your yaqeen (certainty) regarding His essence? How can you cultivate that direct knowledge beyond mere argument?
?
If you cannot prove Allah's essence by demonstration, what does that mean for your worship? Is your devotion based on intellectual certainty or on a direct, unshakable awareness of His reality? How would your prayer change if you fully internalized that Allah's essence is known directly and not through reasoning?

The Self-Evidence of Self-Knowledge
Razi argues from the necessary truth 'I exist' to the intuitive knowledge of the essence, then refutes alternative referents of 'I'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that knowledge of one's own essence is intuitive and cannot be proven by demonstration, based on the self-evident proposition 'I exist'.

Core Idea
Your knowledge of your self is self-evident—you cannot prove what you already know directly.

Razi presents a powerful argument that the simplest piece of self-knowledge—the awareness that 'I exist'—already presupposes a direct conception of the self. He then examines what the word 'I' could possibly refer to, eliminating every alternative except the essence itself. This lesson walks through that argument, showing why the self is known immediately and not through any chain of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting point
Notice that Razi begins with a conceded point: the knowledge that 'I exist' is necessary (ḍarūrī). This is the foundation of the entire argument.
The opponent already accepts that 'I exist' is a necessary truth.
2
Analyze the proposition 'I exist'
Break down the proposition into subject ('I') and predicate ('exists'). Since conception of the subject must precede assent to the proposition, we must already have a conception of what 'I' means.
This uses the principle that taṣawwur precedes taṣdīq.
3
Follow the three possibilities for 'I'
Razi considers three things the word 'I' could refer to: the entire essence, a part of the essence, or something external. Examine why each possibility leads to either absurdity or the conclusion that knowledge of the self is intuitive.
Pay attention to the logical refutations of the second and third options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فإذا سلمتم أن علمي بأني موجود : علم ضروري . فقول القائل : أنا موجود : قضية . موضوعها : قولنا أنا . ومحمولها : قولنا موجود. والتصور سابق على التصديق ، فوجب أن يكون علمي بالمعنى المشار إليه بقولي : ( أنا ) : حاصلاً ، وذلك المعنى ليس إلا الماهية المخصوصة ، التي هي ( أنا ) وإذا كانت هذه الماهية المخصوصة معلومة ، من حيث إنها هي ، امتنع كونها مطلوبة بالبرهان . وأيضاً : فالمشار إليه بقولي : ( أنا ) إما أن يكون تمام تلك الماهية. وإما أن يكون جزء من أجزاء هذه الماهية. وإما أن يكون أمراً مغايراً لها خارجاً عنها . فإن كان الأول ، كان العلم بماهية نفسي ، علماً بديهياً ، فيمتنع كونه مطلوباً بالبرهان . وإن كان الثاني فهو باطل . لأنه يلزمه أن يكون المشار إليه بقولي : ( أنا ) : جزء من أجزاء ماهيتي . فيكون تمام حقيقة الشيء : جزءاً من أجزاء حقيقته ، وهو محال. وبهذا البيان : يظهر أن المشار إليه بقولي : « أنا » : يمتنع أن يكون خارجاً عن الماهية لازماً لها. وأيضاً : فهب أن هذا القسم معقول في الجملة . إلا أني إن علمت كونه من لوازم نفي ، كان العلم بنفسي سابقاً على هذا العلم ، وإن لم أعلم كونه من لوازم نفسي، كان علمي بنفسي ، علماً بشيء مغاير لنفسي ، ولا تعلق له بنفسي . وذلك محال .
Also: If you concede that my knowledge that I exist is necessary knowledge, then the statement 'I exist' is a proposition. Its subject is our saying 'I', and its predicate is our saying 'exists'. Conception (taṣawwur) precedes assent (taṣdīq), so it is necessary that my knowledge of the meaning indicated by my saying 'I' is obtained, and that meaning is nothing but the specific essence which is 'I'. And if this specific essence is known insofar as it is itself, it is impossible for it to be sought through demonstration. Also: The referent of my saying 'I' is either the entirety of that essence, or a part of the parts of this essence, or something other than it external to it. If it is the first, then knowledge of my essence is intuitive knowledge, so it is impossible for it to be sought by demonstration. If it is the second, it is false, because it would imply that the referent of my saying 'I' is a part of the parts of my essence, so the entirety of a thing's reality would be a part of the parts of its reality, which is absurd. By this explanation, it becomes evident that the referent of my saying 'I' cannot be something external to the essence that is a necessary concomitant of it. Also: Grant that this division is conceivable in general. However, if I know it to be a concomitant of myself, then knowledge of myself precedes this knowledge; if I do not know it to be a concomitant of myself, then my knowledge of myself would be knowledge of something other than myself, with no connection to myself, and that is absurd.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage provides a second argument that knowledge of one's essence is necessary, not acquired through demonstration. The author begins with the conceded point that 'I exist' is a necessary truth (ʿilm ḍarūrī). He analyzes this proposition: the subject is 'I' (anā) and the predicate is 'exists'. Since conception (taṣawwur) of the subject must precede assent (taṣdīq) to the proposition, it follows that we already have a conception of what 'I' means. That conception is the specific essence (māhiyya makhṣūṣa) that is the self. Since this essence is already known, it cannot be sought through demonstration. The author then examines possible interpretations of what the word 'I' refers to. He considers three possibilities: (1) it refers to the entire essence, (2) it refers to a part of the essence, or (3) it refers to something external to the essence. The first option yields that knowledge of the essence is intuitive (badīhī) and therefore not provable. The second option is absurd because it would make the whole of a thing a part of itself. The third option is also impossible: if 'I' refers to an external necessary concomitant (lāzim), then either we know it is a concomitant (in which case knowledge of the self precedes) or we do not know it (in which case our knowledge would be of something unrelated to the self). Hence, the only coherent interpretation is that 'I' refers directly to the essence, which is known necessarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the word 'I' refers to a part of the self (like the soul) or an attribute (like consciousness), and that knowledge of the self could be acquired by analyzing these parts.
Correction: Razi shows that if 'I' referred to a part, it would lead to the absurdity that the whole is a part of itself. If it referred to an external attribute, knowledge of the self would either be presupposed or become disconnected. Therefore, 'I' must refer directly to the entire essence, which is known intuitively.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what does the word 'I' refer to in the proposition 'I exist'?
A) A part of the essence
B) The entire essence
C) A necessary concomitant
D) The body

Key Takeaway
The self is known directly through the necessary truth 'I exist', and no demonstration can prove what is already self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your knowledge of your own self is immediate and undeniable, then why do you often live as if you are a stranger to yourself? What would change in your daily life if you truly acted on the certainty that your 'I' is not a mystery but something you already know?
?
Razi argues that you cannot prove what you already know directly. Is there an aspect of your relationship with Allah that you keep trying to 'prove' instead of simply accepting the self-evident truth? What would it look like to stop seeking proof and start living that truth?
?
You just read that the referent of 'I' cannot be a part or attribute, but the whole essence. In your own life, do you identify yourself more with your attributes (your thoughts, feelings, achievements) than with your unchanging essence? How might that shift in perspective change your priorities?

Refuting the Philosophers' Claim on Self-Knowledge
Author refutes the philosophers' claim that self-knowledge is immediate and not demonstrable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the kalam argument distinguishes between immediate self-knowledge of the soul's essence and the demonstrable nature of its negative attributes.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge is immediate; only the soul's negative attributes require demonstration.

This passage presents a robust debate about whether the soul's knowledge of itself is immediate or can be demonstrated. The philosophers argue for immediacy, but the author counters by showing that what is actually demonstrated are the soul's negative qualifications, not its essence. Follow this dialectic to see how kalam upholds both immediate self-awareness and the need for rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument come through before interpreting.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim and dilemma
Find where the philosophers assert that self-knowledge is essential (nafsī) and present their dilemma: either a distinct form or identical to the essence. Understand why they reject the first option.
Pay attention to the terms 'ḥāliyya' and 'maḥalliyya'.
3
Note the author's shift to negative attributes
After the philosophers' argument, the author provides the answer: what is sought by demonstration is not the soul's essence but its negative qualifications. Recognize this as the key move.
This answers why demonstration is still possible.
4
Trace the objections and responses
Follow the subsequent dialectic: the interlocutor objects that negative attributes are necessitated by the essence, then that no middle term can cause them. The author responds to each, demonstrating the robustness of the kalam position.
Consider how the author uses the empirical observation of differences in opinion to counter the final objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : إن مذهب الحكماء : أن علم الشيء بذاته ، هو نفسي . وفي هذه الصورة يقولون : العقل والعاقل والمعقول : واحتجوا عليه : بأنه : لما ثبت أن تعقل الشيء عبارة عن صورة مساوية للمعقول في ذات العاقل . فنقول : علم الشيء بذاته. إما أن يكون صورة مغايرة لذاته ، حاصلة في ذاته. وإما أن يكون عين ذاته. والأول باطل . وإلا لزم اجتماع المثلين . وذلك محال . ولأن على هذا التقدير لم يكن أحدهما بالحالية، والآخر بالمحلية أولى من العكس . ولما بطل هذا القسم ، ثبت أن علم النفس بحقيقتها المخصوصة : عين ذاتها . ولما كانت ذاتها حاضرة في جميع أوقات دوام تلك الذات، وجب أن يكون علمها بذاتها المخصوصة ، دائما بدوام ذاتها . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون هذا العلم مطلوباً بالبرهان . لان الحاصل لا يمكن تحصيله ثانياً . فيثبت بهذه المباحث القوية : أن معرفة جوهر النفس يجب أن تكون معرفة بديهية أولية ، غنية عن التعريف والكسب والطلب . وأنتم قد جعلتموه من المباحث البرهانية ، فكان الإشكال لازماً . والجواب : إن المطلوب بالبرهان : هو أن جوهر النفس : جوهر ليس بمتحيز ، ولا حال في المتحيز. فالمطلوب بالبرهان هو هذا القيد السلبي والقيود السلبية أمور مغايرة للذات المخصوصة . فإن قالوا : هب أن الأمر كذلك ، إلا أن المقتضي لذلك السلب للخصوص هو تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة. فإذا كان العلم بتلك الحقيقة المخصوصة حاصلاً كان العلم بعلة ذلك السلب حاصلاً والعلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول ، فوجب أن يكون العلم بذلك السلب حاصلاً على سبيل الدوام. قلنا : السلب نفي محض ، فلا يمكن جعله من مقتضيات الماهية . فإن قالوا : إذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع كون الحد الأوسط موجباً له ، فوجب أن تتعذر معرفته بواسطة البرهان . فنقول : الجواب الأولى هو أن يقال : إنا نجد بالضرورة اختلاف الناس في هذا المطلوب ونعلم بالضرورة أنه [يحث؟] غامض . فما ذكرتموه من السؤال تشكيك في البديهيات
The second question: Indeed the doctrine of the philosophers (al-ḥukamā') is that knowledge of a thing of itself is essential (nafsī). And in this form they say: the intellect, the intelligent, and the intelligible. They argued for it: because when it is established that the intellection of a thing is an expression of a form equal to the intelligible in the essence of the intelligent. So we say: knowledge of a thing of itself is either a form different from its essence, subsisting in its essence, or it is identical to its essence. The first is false, otherwise the coincidence of two similars would be necessary, and that is impossible. And because on this supposition, neither of the two would be more deserving of being the inherence (al-ḥāliyya) and the other the locus (al-maḥalliyya) than the reverse. When this division becomes false, it is established that the soul's knowledge of its specific reality is identical to its essence. And since its essence is present at all times of the duration of that essence, its knowledge of its specific essence must be perpetual with the perpetuity of its essence. If it is so, it is impossible that this knowledge be sought through demonstration, for what is already attained cannot be attained a second time. So it is established by these strong investigations that knowledge of the essence of the soul must be a priori primary knowledge, independent of definition, acquisition, and seeking. And you have made it among demonstrative investigations, so the difficulty is necessary. The answer: That which is sought by demonstration is that the essence of the soul is a substance not spatially extended (mutaḥayyiz) nor inhering in a spatially extended thing. So that which is sought by demonstration is this negative qualification, and negative qualifications are things different from the specific essence. If they say: Suppose it is so, but the necessitator of that negation specific to it is that specific reality. So if knowledge of that specific reality is obtained, knowledge of the cause of that negation is obtained, and knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, so knowledge of that negation must be obtained perpetually. We say: Negation is pure negation, so it cannot be made among the requirements of the quiddity. If they say: If it is so, it is impossible for the middle term to be a cause of it, so knowledge of it through demonstration must be impossible. We say: The first answer is to say: We find necessarily that people differ regarding this sought matter, and we know necessarily that it is an obscure [matter?]. So what you have mentioned of the question is a casting doubt upon a priori truths.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence concerning whether the soul's knowledge of itself is immediate or can be sought through demonstration. The author presents the philosophers' (al-ḥukamā') claim that self-knowledge is essential and identical to the soul itself, therefore always present and not demonstrable. Their argument rests on a dilemma: either the soul's knowledge of itself is a form distinct from its essence or identical to it. The first is impossible because it would lead to two similar forms or an arbitrary assignment of inherence and locus. Thus self-knowledge is identical to the soul, and since the soul is always present, its self-knowledge is perpetual and cannot be acquired through demonstration. The author then provides the answer: what is sought by demonstration is not the soul's essence, but its negative attributes—that it is not a body nor a location. These negative qualifications are separate from the essence and therefore require demonstration. The interlocutor objects that the negative attribute is necessitated by the essence, so knowledge of the essence should yield knowledge of the negation perpetually. The author responds that negation is pure absence and cannot be a required concomitant of the quiddity. A further objection claims that if the negation is not necessitated, then no middle term can cause it, making demonstration impossible. The author counters by noting that people manifestly differ about this subject and it is recognized as obscure, so the objection itself undermines a priori truths. The passage systematically defends the possibility and necessity of demonstrative argument in kalam for establishing negative attributes of the soul.

حد أوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author denies immediate self-knowledge entirely.
Correction: The author affirms immediate self-knowledge of the soul's essence but argues that negative attributes require demonstration.

Checkpoint
According to the author in this passage, what is it that can be sought through demonstration regarding the soul?

B) The soul's negative qualifications (e.g., not being spatially extended)
C) The soul's knowledge of itself
D) The soul's existence as a substance

Key Takeaway
The kalam position harmonizes immediate self-awareness with the need for demonstration of the soul's negative attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know yourself, but have you ever honestly examined whether your self-knowledge includes the negative attributes of your soul? Are you certain that you are not merely a body, or have you just assumed it? What would it take for you to prove that to someone who doubts?

The author says people differ about the soul's nature and that it is an obscure matter. Do you find yourself confused when trying to articulate what your soul is? Does that confusion reveal that you rely on immediate self-awareness rather than rigorous demonstration? Will you seek a clearer proof?
?
If self-knowledge of the essence is immediate, why do you still struggle with doubts about your own identity and purpose? Perhaps you need to pursue demonstration for the attributes you lack clarity on. What step will you take today to move from vague self-awareness to certain knowledge about your soul?

The First Premise: Knowledge of the Soul's Attributes is Intuitive
First premise with intuitive examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise of the argument: that our knowledge of the attributes of our souls is intuitive, supported by self-evident examples.

Core Idea
You know your own thoughts and actions without deduction—that is intuitive knowledge.

The author begins by stating that knowledge of our essence is intuitive, then presents the first of three premises: our knowledge of the attributes of our souls is intuitive. This lesson explains the first premise and its supporting examples, showing why it is considered self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author starts with a general claim about essence.
The passage includes the general statement and the first premise with examples.
2
Identify the claim and examples
The claim is that knowledge of our soul's attributes (like seeing, hearing) is intuitive. The author lists several actions we know without reflection.
These examples are meant to be undeniable.
3
Understand why it is considered intuitive
The author argues that anyone who disputes this is denying the clearest of intuitive truths. This establishes the first premise as solid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان العلم بالذات بديهياً، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون علمنا بذاتنا المخصوصة علماً بديهياً، وإذا ثبت هذا وجب أن تكون ذاتنا عبارة عن هذا البدن. فهذه الحجة مبنية على مقدمات ثلاث: المقدمة الأولى: إن علمنا بصفات نفوسنا: علم بديهي. والذي يدل عليه: أني أعلم بالضرورة: أني أبصرت وسمعت وقلت وعرفت، وتفكرت، واشتهيت، وغضبت ودخلت الدار وخرجت وسافرت إلى البلدة الفلانية ورجعت منها. ومن نازع في كون هذه العلوم علوماً بديهية، فقد نازع في أجلى العلوم البديهية وأقواها. فثبت: أن علمنا بصفات أنفسنا: علم بديهي. والمقدمة الثانية: وهي قولنا: إن العلم بصفة النفس إذا كان بديهياً، كان العلم بذات النفس يجب أن يكون بديهياً. فالدليل عليه: أن علمي بأني أبصرت وسمعت وعقلت ونظرت: حكم على نفسي بثبوت هذه الصفات لها. والحاكم بشيء على شيء، لا بد وأن يكون قد سبق له تصور الطرفين أولا، فلو كان علمي بوجود نفسي علماً كسبياً، فقبل ذلك الكسب: أكون شاكاً في وجود نفسي. ومن كان شاكاً في وجود النفس يمتنع أن يكون عالماً بحصول الصفات المخصوصة بها. ولما دللنا على أن هذا العلم علم بديهي وجب أن يكون العلم بوجود النفس بديهياً. وهذا هو المسألة المشهورة عند العقلاء بأنه متى كان العلم بصفة الشيء بديهياً، وجب أن يكون العلم بأصل الذات بديهياً. والمقدمة الثالثة: إنه لما ثبت: أن علم كل أحد بنفسه المخصوصة، علم بديهي. وجب أن تكون النفس هو هذا البدن، أو شيء داخل فيه. والدليل عليه: أن إثبات شيء سوى هذا البدن وسوى أجزاء هذا البدن: أمر استدلالي لا ضروري. وذلك مما لا نزاع فيه بين العقلاء. وعند هذا ينعقد قياس من الشكل الثاني. وهو أن يقال: نفس كل واحد: معلوم له بالبديهة. والشيء المجرد الذي لا يكون جسماً ولا جسمانياً: غير معلوم بالبديهة. ينتج: أن النفس يمتنع أن تكون عبارة عن الموجود الذي ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني. وهو المطلوب.
Knowledge of the essence was intuitive, and this requires that our knowledge of our own particular essence be intuitive knowledge. And if this is established, then it is necessary that our essence be this body. So this argument is built on three premises: The first premise: Our knowledge of the attributes of our souls is intuitive knowledge. What indicates it is that I know necessarily that I saw, heard, said, knew, thought, desired, became angry, entered the house, left, traveled to such-and-such town, and returned from it. Whoever disputes that these items of knowledge are intuitive knowledge has disputed the clearest and strongest intuitive knowledge. So it is established that our knowledge of the attributes of ourselves is intuitive knowledge. The second premise: Our saying: If knowledge of an attribute of the soul is intuitive, then knowledge of the essence of the soul must be intuitive. The proof for it is that my knowledge that I saw, heard, reasoned, and reflected is a judgment upon myself of the affirmation of these attributes for it. The one who judges one thing about another must have already had a conception of both sides. If my knowledge of my soul's existence were acquired (kasbī), then before that acquisition I would be doubting my soul's existence. Whoever doubts the soul's existence cannot be knowing the occurrence of attributes specific to it. Since we demonstrated that this knowledge is intuitive, it is necessary that knowledge of the soul's existence be intuitive. This is the well-known issue among the rational people that whenever knowledge of an attribute of a thing is intuitive, knowledge of the essence itself must be intuitive. The third premise: Since it is established that everyone's knowledge of his own particular soul is intuitive, then the soul must be this body or something inside it. The proof for it is that affirming something other than this body and other than parts of this body is a matter of inference (istidlālī), not necessary (ḍarūrī), and that is something about which there is no dispute among the rational people. At this point a syllogism of the second figure is formed. It is said: Every person's soul is known to him intuitively. A non-physical entity that is not a body nor a corporeal entity is not known intuitively. The conclusion: It is impossible for the soul to be something that is not a body nor corporeal. And that is the desired.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the thesis that the human soul is the body. The author begins by stating the conclusion: our knowledge of our own essence is intuitive, implying that our essence must be this body. Then he lays out three premises that support this argument. The first premise is that our knowledge of the attributes of our souls is intuitive. He argues by appealing to self-evident examples: we know without reflection that we have seen, heard, thought, desired, etc. Anyone who denies this intuitiveness is disputing the clearest of intuitive truths. The second premise establishes a principle: if knowledge of an attribute is intuitive, then knowledge of the essence that bears that attribute must also be intuitive. The proof is that judging an attribute of something presupposes knowledge of the thing itself; if knowledge of the self were acquired, we would first have to doubt its existence, which is impossible since we do not doubt. Therefore, knowledge of the soul's existence is intuitive. The third premise argues that since intuitive knowledge of one's own soul is universal, the soul must be either the body or something within it. The reason is that any other non-physical entity would not be known intuitively but only by inference. Then he constructs a second-figure syllogism: the soul is known intuitively; a non-physical entity is not known intuitively; therefore, the soul cannot be a non-physical entity. The conclusion is that the soul is the body.

Key Terms
النفس
البديهة
العلم البديهي
الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first premise, which of the following is an example of intuitive knowledge?
A) That the earth orbits the sun
B) That one saw and heard something
C) That the soul is a separate substance
D) That the body is physical

Key Takeaway
The first premise establishes that our knowledge of our own soul's attributes is immediate and undeniable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you were absolutely certain of your own intention or action. Did you need anyone to tell you or prove it? How does that immediate certainty challenge any doubts you might have about your own soul? Are you willing to accept that this intuitive knowledge is a real starting point for understanding who you are?
?
If your knowledge of your own states like seeing and hearing is intuitive and undeniable, why do you sometimes second-guess your own motives or feelings? What would it take for you to trust that inner awareness as much as you trust your physical senses?
?
The author says disputing this is like disputing the clearest knowledge. Are there areas in your life where you suppress or deny your immediate self-awareness? What would change if you fully embraced that intuitive knowledge as true?

The Second Premise: From Attribute Knowledge to Essence Knowledge
Second premise with logical proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second premise: that if knowledge of the soul's attribute is intuitive, then knowledge of the soul's essence must also be intuitive.

Core Idea
If you know a thing's qualities directly, you must know the thing itself directly.

The second premise builds on the first: since we have intuitive knowledge of our soul's attributes, we must also have intuitive knowledge of the soul's existence. The author proves this by showing that judging attributes presupposes knowledge of the subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise and its proof
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The author states the premise and then gives a logical proof.
Focus on the argument about judgment requiring prior conception.
2
Understand the proof
To know that 'I see' requires knowing 'I' first. If 'I' were known only by inference, we would be in doubt. But we are not in doubt, so knowledge of the soul is intuitive.
3
Grasp the general principle
The author concludes with a general rule: whenever knowledge of an attribute is intuitive, knowledge of the essence is also intuitive.
This principle is used later.

?

If that is true, then why do you often live as if you are uncertain of your own worth or purpose? ?
You know intuitively that you see and hear—yet do you ever stop to recognize that this knowledge points to a deeper reality: the 'I' that does the seeing? If the 'I' is as certain as the seeing, why do you spend so much effort seeking identity and meaning outside yourself, rather than turning inward to the certain self that is already present?
?

Are you willing to face that soul honestly, with all its faults and potential, or do you prefer to remain distracted and self-avoidant? What is one specific change you can commit to today that honors the undeniable, intuitive reality of your own soul?

The Third Premise and Syllogism: The Soul is the Body
Third premise with proof and syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third premise and the second-figure syllogism lead to the conclusion that the soul is the body or something inside it.

Core Idea
What is known intuitively must be physical; a non-physical entity is not known intuitively—so the soul must be physical.

The third premise asserts that since everyone's knowledge of their own soul is intuitive, the soul must be the body or something inside it. The author then constructs a formal syllogism in the second figure to conclude that the soul cannot be a non-physical entity. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the third premise and its proof
Read the Arabic and translation. The premise states that the soul must be the body or part of it because any other entity would require inference.
2
Understand the syllogism
The author forms a second-figure syllogism: major premise (soul is known intuitively), minor premise (incorporeal is not known intuitively), conclusion (soul is not incorporeal).
The second figure has a specific logical structure.
3
See the final conclusion
The conclusion is that the soul cannot be a non-physical entity; therefore, it must be the body or something inside it, which is what the author wanted to prove.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The syllogism is in the first figure (subject-predicate order)
Correction: The passage explicitly states it is of the second figure, where the middle term is predicate of both premises.

Checkpoint
What form of syllogism does the author use to conclude that the soul is not non-physical?
A) First figure
B) Second figure
C) Third figure
D) Fourth figure

Key Takeaway
The soul cannot be a non-physical substance; it must be the body or a part of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Right now, without thinking, do you know your own soul? What does that knowledge feel like—is it attached to your body or separate? If this passage is correct, that knowledge is of something physical. Does that match your experience? What would it mean for your prayer life if your soul is just your body?
?
Imagine you are standing before Allah tomorrow and He asks: 'Did you truly know yourself as I made you?' Would you have to say you thought your soul was something non-physical? Based on this lesson, how will you correct that misunderstanding starting tonight?
?
The passage says rational people agree that affirming anything beyond the body is inferential. Are you willing to accept that your 'self' is limited to your body and its parts? If not, what is your proof—and is it intuitive or inferred? Be honest: has your conviction been based on feeling or on revealed truth?

Second Argument Against a Non-Physical Soul and Refutation of an Objection
The author presents a second argument against a non-physical soul and answers an objection based on metaphorical language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against a non-physical soul and how the author refutes an objection based on metaphorical language.

Core Idea
The soul's actions are known by reason, not metaphor.

This lesson presents a powerful intuitive argument against the notion of a non-physical soul. The author uses ordinary statements like 'I entered the house' to show that we directly know ourselves as agents of physical actions. Then he answers an objection that tries to dismiss these statements as mere figures of speech, clarifying that the argument rests on rational certainty, not linguistic convention.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the argument begins with a conditional and uses everyday examples.
The examples are 'I moved, I became still, I entered the house, I exited it, I went to the market, I returned from it to the mosque'.
2
Identify the intuitive premise
The author claims these statements are known to be true intuitively (bi al-badiha). Why is this premise important for the argument?
Intuitive knowledge is considered the most certain form of knowledge in kalam.
3
Examine the objection about metaphor
The opponent argues that the statements might be metaphorical, referring to the body. How does this objection aim to undermine the argument?
If the statements are metaphorical, then the intuitive premise is not about the soul but about the body.
4
Study the author's response
The author distinguishes between the verbal expression and the rational judgment of the mind. He insists that the argument relies on the latter, not the former.
The author says: 'Shahadat al-aql bi ma'ani hadhih al-alfaz ghayr, wa shahadat al-aql bi dhikr hadhih al-alfaz ghayr.'
5
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider why the author believes this argument is decisive. How does it avoid the pitfall of linguistic ambiguity?
The argument is based on a mental proposition (qadiyya 'aqliyya ma'nawiyya), not on verbal usages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية لمنكري هذا الجوهر المجرد: إنه لو كانت النفس عبارة عن جوهر مجرد، ليس بجسم ولا حال في الجسم، لكان قول القائل: تحركت وسكنت ودخلت الدار وخرجت منها، وذهبت إلى السوق، ورجعت منه إلى المسجد، كل ذلك يوجب أن تكون أقوالاً باطلة. لأن هذه الصفات ممتنعة الثبوت في حق الجوهر المجرد عن الجسمية. إلا أن هذه الأقوال معلومة الصحة بالبديهة. لأن كل عاقل قبل خوضه في هذه المضائق يعلم بالضرورة صدق قوله: دخلت الدار، وخرجت منها. كما يعلم صدق قوله: علمت كذا، وفهمت كذا. فالقدح فيه يكون قدحاً في أظهر البديهيات. فإن قالوا: حاصل هذا الدليل يرجع إلى التمسك بألفاظ الناس وإطلاقهم، والألفاظ تحتمل الحقيقة والمجاز. فلعل مرادهم بقولهم: دخلت الدار وخرجت منها: أن بدني دخل الدار، وخرج منها. وعلى هذا التقدير تسقط هذه الحجة. فنقول في الجواب: إنا لا نمسك باللفظ المحض الذي يحتمل الحقيقة والمجاز، بل نتمسك بشهادة صريح عقل كل أحد: بأنه هو الذي دخل في الدار وخرج منها، فشهادة العقل بمعاني هذه الألفاظ غير، وشهادة العقل بذكر هذه الألفاظ غير. واعتمادنا في هذه الحجة: على القضية العقلية المعنوية، لا على مجرد الإطلاقات اللفظية.
The second argument for the deniers of this non-physical substance: If the soul were a non-physical substance, not a body nor a state in a body, then the statement of the speaker: 'I moved, I became still, I entered the house, I exited it, I went to the market, I returned from it to the mosque' — all that would necessitate that these statements be false. Because these attributes are impossible of affirmation for a non-physical substance devoid of corporeality. Yet these statements are known to be true intuitively. For every rational person before delving into these difficulties knows necessarily the truth of his statement: 'I entered the house, I exited it,' just as he knows the truth of his statement: 'I knew such and such, I understood such and such.' So to impugn it is to impugn the most apparent of intuitive truths. If they say: The upshot of this argument reduces to clinging to people's expressions and their usage, and expressions admit of literal and figurative meaning. Perhaps they mean by their statement 'I entered the house and exited it' that my body entered and exited. On this assumption this argument collapses. We say in answer: We do not cling to the bare expression that admits of literal and figurative meaning; rather we cling to the explicit testimony of the mind of every person that it is he who entered the house and exited it. The testimony of the mind to the meanings of these expressions is one thing, and the testimony of the mind to the utterance of these expressions is another. Our reliance in this argument is on the mental, non-linguistic proposition, not on mere verbal usages.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against the view that the soul is a non-physical substance (jawhar mujarrad). The argument is based on everyday statements: we say 'I moved, I entered the house, I went to the market.' If the soul were a non-physical substance, such actions would be impossible for it, because motion and location are corporeal attributes. Therefore, these statements would be false. But we know intuitively that they are true. Therefore the soul cannot be a non-physical substance. Then an objection is raised: these statements might be metaphorical, meaning the body, not the soul. The author answers that the argument does not rely on the words themselves, but on the clear testimony of reason that it is 'I' who did these actions, not just the body. The mind's judgment about the meaning is distinct from the mere utterance. So the argument stands on rational certainty, not linguistic conventions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection refutes the argument by pointing out that language can be metaphorical.
Correction: The author shows that the argument is based on the rational judgment of the self, not on the literal or figurative meaning of words.

Razi's Second-Figure Syllogism Against the Abstract Soul
Razi presents a second-figure syllogism to disprove the soul as an abstract substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a second-figure syllogism to disprove that the soul is an abstract non-bodily substance.

Core Idea
Razi uses what we know intuitively to disprove what we cannot know.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a logical argument using a second-figure syllogism to challenge the view that the soul is an abstract, non-bodily substance. He begins by noting that the existence of anything beyond the body is a matter of inference, not immediate knowledge. Then he constructs a syllogism that draws a contradiction from the premise that the soul is known intuitively. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own reasoning come through before you interpret.
The Arabic contains a key term: 'قياس من الشكل الثاني' (second-figure syllogism).
2
Identify the two premises
Find the major and minor premises of the syllogism. The major premise states that the soul is known intuitively. The minor premise states that an abstract thing is not known intuitively.
The major premise is: 'نفس كل واحد : معلوم له بالبديهة' (the soul of each person is known intuitively).
3
Understand the conclusion and contradiction
The conclusion follows that the soul cannot be an abstract entity because if it were, it would not be known intuitively—contradicting the major premise.
Razi concludes that the required conclusion (that the soul is abstract) is impossible.
4
Reflect on the argument's significance
Consider why Razi uses a second-figure syllogism. This form is valid but less common; it highlights the incompatibility of the premises with the desired conclusion.
The second figure typically yields a negative conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه : أن إثبات شيء سوى هذا البدن وسوى أجزاء هذا البدن : أمر استدلالي لا ضروري . وذلك مما لا نزاع فيه بين العقلاء. وعند هذا ينعقد قياس من الشكل الثاني . وهو أن يقال : نفس كل واحد : معلوم له بالبديهة . والشيء المجرد الذي لا يكون جسماً ولا جسمانياً : غير معلوم بالبديهة . ينتج : أن أن تكون عبارة عن الموجود الذي ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني . وهو النفس يمتنع المطلوب
Regarding it: that establishing something other than this body and other than the parts of this body is a matter of inference, not necessary. And that is something about which there is no dispute among rational people. And at this point a syllogism of the second figure is formed. And it is to say: the soul of each person is known to him intuitively. And the abstract thing which is neither a body nor corporeal is not known intuitively. It follows that being an expression of the existent which is not a body nor corporeal is the soul—which is impossible—the required conclusion.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an argument in the form of a second-figure syllogism to refute the view that the soul is an abstract non-bodily substance. The author states that establishing the existence of anything beyond the body is a matter of inference, not immediate knowledge. He then constructs the syllogism: major premise – each person's soul is known intuitively; minor premise – an abstract thing that is neither body nor corporeal is not known intuitively; conclusion – therefore, the soul cannot be such an abstract entity. The conclusion is drawn because if the soul were abstract, it would not be known intuitively, but it is known intuitively – a contradiction. Hence, the denial that the soul is an abstract substance.

Key Terms
نفس
جوهر مجرد
قياس الشكل الثاني
بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is proving the existence of the soul.
Correction: Razi is actually disproving that the soul is an abstract substance; he takes the soul's existence as given and known intuitively.

Checkpoint
What type of syllogism does Razi use in the first argument?
A) First figure
B) Second figure
C) Third figure
D) Hypothetical syllogism

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that since the soul is known intuitively, it cannot be an abstract non-bodily substance, which is known only by inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Second Argument: Intuitive Movement vs. Abstract Soul
Razi presents a second argument, objection, and reply about the abstract soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second argument against the abstract soul, including the intuitive evidence and the response to the figurative language objection.

Core Idea
Our own movement is intuitively certain; an abstract soul cannot move, so the soul is not abstract.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a second argument targeting those who deny the soul is a bodily substance. He appeals to our most basic self-awareness: we know with certainty that we enter houses and leave them. If the soul were abstract and non-bodily, such statements would be false. An opponent might claim these are mere metaphors, but Razi insists that reason itself testifies to the reality of our movements, not just words.

Guided Path
1
Read the full argument
Start by reading the entire Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the flow: a conditional statement, then an appeal to intuition, then an objection and reply.
The Arabic is dense; read the translation carefully too.
2
Identify the core conditional
Find the 'if...then' structure: If the soul is abstract, then movement statements are false. Understand why an abstract substance cannot move.
This is the logical backbone of the argument.
3
Analyze the intuitive evidence
Razi says these statements are 'known by intuition'. Ask yourself: Do you know you entered a room without needing proof? That's his point.
4
Examine the objection and response
The opponent says maybe 'I entered' is metaphorical, meaning my body. Razi distinguishes between bare words and the meaning reason grasps. Trace his reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية لمنكري هذا الجوهر المجرد : إنه لو كانت النفس عبارة عن جوهر مجرد ، ليس بجسم ولا حال في الجسم، لكان قول القائل : تحركت وسكنت ودخلت الدار وخرجت منها ، وذهبت إلى السوق ، ورجعت منه إلى المسجد . كل ذلك ( يوجب أن (۱) ] تكون أقوالاً باطلة . لأن هذه الصفات ممتنعة الثبوت في حق الجوهر المجرد عن الجسمية . إلا أن هذه الأقوال معلومة الصحة بالبديهة. لأن كل عاقل قبل خوضه في هذه المضائق يعلم بالضرورة صدق قوله : دخلت الدار ، وخرجت منها . كما يعلم صدق قوله : علمت كذا ، وفهمت كذا . فالقدح فيه يكون قدحاً في أظهر البديهيات. فإن قالوا : حاصل هذا الدليل يرجع إلى التمسك بألفاظ الناس وإطلاقهم ، والألفاظ تحتمل الحقيقة والمجاز. فلعل مرادهم بقولهم : دخلت الدار وخرجت منها : أن ( بدني ) دخل الدار ، وخرج منها . وعلى هذا التقدير تسقط هذه الحجة. فنقول في الجواب : إنا لا نمسك باللفظ المحض الذي يحتمل الحقيقة والمجاز ، بل نتمسك بشهادة صريح عقل كل أحد : بأنه هو الذي دخل في الدار وخرج منها ، [ فشهادة العقل بمعاني هذه الألفاظ غير ، وشهادة العقل بذكر هذه الألفاظ غير (٢) ] واعتمادنا في هذه الحجة : على القضية العقلية المعنوية ، لا على مجرد الإطلاقات اللفظية .
The second proof of the deniers of this abstract substance: If the soul were an abstract substance, not a body nor inherent in the body, then someone's saying 'I moved, I was still, I entered the house, I left it, I went to the market, I returned from it to the mosque'—all of that would necessitate that these statements be false. Because these attributes are impossible to establish for an abstract substance devoid of corporeality. Yet these statements are known to be true intuitively. For every rational person before delving into these straits knows necessarily the truth of his statement 'I entered the house and I left it', just as he knows the truth of his statement 'I knew such-and-such and I understood such-and-such'. So to impugn it would be an impugnment of the most evident intuitive truths. If they say: The gist of this argument returns to clinging to people's words and their usage, and words admit of literal and figurative [meaning]. Perhaps their intention in saying 'I entered the house and left it' is that my body entered the house and left it. On this assumption, this proof falls. We say in answer: We do not cling to the bare word which admits of literal and figurative, but we cling to the explicit testimony of everyone's reason: that it is he who entered the house and left it. For the testimony of reason to the meanings of these words is other than the testimony of reason to the utterance of these words. And our reliance in this proof is on the intellectual meaningful proposition, not on mere verbal usages.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a second argument against the view that the soul is an abstract substance. The author argues that if the soul were abstract, then statements attributing movement and location to oneself would be false, because an abstract entity cannot change location. However, such statements are known to be true intuitively, as every rational person knows immediately that they themselves enter and leave places. An objection is then raised: perhaps such statements are metaphorical, meaning the body does these actions. The author responds by distinguishing between the bare word and the inner testimony of reason. He insists that reason testifies that it is the self that enters and leaves, not just the body. The proof relies on the intellectual understanding of the meaning, not merely on verbal usage.
