The Decisive Proof: Intuitive Distinction Between Self and Body
Razi argues that if the soul were abstract, entering the house would be like a horse entering, but intuition distinguishes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's decisive proof against the view that the soul is an abstract substance by examining the intuitive distinction between self and body in actions.

Core Idea
The intuitive distinction between 'I entered' and 'my horse entered' disproves the abstract soul.

Razi presents a powerful argument that appeals to our everyday awareness. He invites us to notice how we naturally speak about our actions: we say 'I entered the house,' not 'my body entered the house.' If the soul were abstract and separate from the body, that way of speaking would be false — but it feels undeniably true. This leads to a clear conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the analogy
Razi compares the body entering the house to a horse entering. Ask yourself: what does this analogy aim to show?
The analogy highlights the idea that if the soul is abstract, the body is merely a vehicle.
3
Focus on the intuitive distinction
Razi says every rational person perceives an intuitive distinction between 'I entered' and 'my horse entered'. Why is this distinction important?
It is a direct experience that contradicts the abstract soul view.
4
Trace the logical conclusion
Because the distinction is undeniable, the premise that the soul is abstract must be false. Follow the logic step by step.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
5
Apply the argument to daily language
Think about how we speak about actions in daily life. Does our language support the abstract soul view or the embodied view?
This shows the argument's intuitive appeal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل القاطع عليه : أنه إذا كان الإنسان عبارة عن جوهر النفس ، كان البدن مركباً له ، ومحلاً لتصرفه . فكان دخول بدنه في الدار وخروجه منها ، جارياً مجرى دخول فرسه في الدار ، وخروجه منها . لكن كل عاقل يدرك تفرقة بديهية بين قوله : دخلت في الدار وخرجت منها ، وبين قوله : دخل فرسي الدار ، وخرج منها. ولما كان القول بأن النفس جوهر مجرد ، يوجب زوال هذه التفرقة المعلومة بالبديهة ، وجب أن يكون القول بإثبات النفس : باطلاً
And the decisive proof regarding it: If the human being were an expression of the substance of the soul, the body would be a mount for it and a locus for its disposal. Then the entering of his body into the house and its leaving it would run the course of the entering of his horse into the house and its leaving it. But every rational person perceives an intuitive distinction between his statement 'I entered the house and left it' and his statement 'My horse entered the house and left it.' And since the statement that the soul is an abstract substance necessitates the removal of this distinction known intuitively, it is necessary that the statement affirming the soul [as abstract] be false.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a decisive proof against the abstract soul view. The author builds on the previous argument: if the human being is essentially the soul, then the body is merely a vehicle. When the body enters a house, it is like a horse entering; the person (the soul) does not literally enter. However, every rational person instinctively distinguishes between saying 'I entered' and 'my horse entered'. This distinction is intuitive. If the soul were abstract, the distinction would vanish, because 'I entered' would be false (since the soul didn't move). But since the distinction is undeniable, the view that the soul is abstract must be false.

جوهر مجرد
بدن
تفرقة بديهية
مركب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument only applies to physical movement, but it extends to all actions attributed to the self.
Correction: Razi's argument is based on linguistic intuition about self-attribution in actions generally, not just movement. The principle applies to any action we ascribe to ourselves.

Checkpoint

B) A horse and its rider

Key Takeaway
Razi uses our everyday language to show that the soul cannot be an abstract substance, because we instinctively distinguish between self and vehicle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does Razi's argument about the intuitive distinction between self and body make you reconsider who is accountable for your actions? What would change in your repentance if you believed your body was merely a horse you ride?

?
Razi calls the distinction between self and body 'intuitive'—you already know it. But do you live as if you know it? In what area of your life do you still act as if your soul is a rider and your body a horse, blaming the horse instead of yourself? How would your next 24 hours change if you fully accepted that your body's actions are your own?

Razi's Third Argument: The Problem of Identity Continuity
Razi argues that soul-body separability leads to inevitable doubt about identity, contradicting our certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the assumption of an abstract soul separable from the body leads to an inevitable doubt about personal identity, which contradicts our actual certainty.

Core Idea
If souls can swap bodies, identity becomes unknowable — but we know identities, so souls are not abstract.

Razi presents a clever reductio ad absurdum: if the soul were a non-physical manager of the body, it could in principle switch bodies. But then we could never be sure if the person we meet is the same as before. Since we are certain of identities, the assumption must be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing; then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow.
2
Understand the hypothetical premise
Razi assumes the soul is an abstract substance that manages the body. Consider what that implies: it could leave one body and attach to another.
This is the key assumption being attacked.
3
Follow the analogy of the house
Razi compares the body to a house and the soul to its owner. If owners can change, you cannot be sure who owns the house when you see it again. Similarly, if souls can change bodies, you cannot be sure of a person's identity.
The analogy makes the logical consequence vivid.
4
Recognize the contradiction with reality
In real life, we never doubt whether Zayd is the same person when we see him again. But the abstract soul theory would require such doubt. Therefore, the theory must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كانت النفس عبارة عن جوهر مجرد ، له تعلق بهذا البدن بالتصرف والتدبير ، لم يبعد في أول العقل أن ينقطع تعلق هذه النفس بهذا البدن ، وتصير متعلقة بالبدن الثاني ، وبالعكس. وعلى هذا التقدير ، فإذا رأينا بدن زيد ، ثم رأيناه بعد ذلك وجب أن نبقى شاكين في أن هذا الإنسان . هل هو الإنسان الأول ؟ لأن بتقدير أن يتبدل المدبر الأول لهذا البدن ، مدبر آخر ، فقد تبدل ذلك الإنسان بغيره . كما أنا لما رأينا بيتاً وجوزنا أن ملك ذلك البيت قد انتقل إلى مالك آخر ، فحينئذ لا نقطع عند رؤية ذلك البيت في المرة الثانية ، بأن مالكه هو المالك الأول . بل نبقى شاكين فيه . فكذا ههنا وجب أن يبقى الشك في أن هذا الإنسان الذي نراه الآن ، هل هو الإنسان الذي رأيناه أولاً ؟ ولما لم يحصل هذا الشك لأحد من العقلاء ، وثبت : أن على تقدير أن يكون الإنسان شيئاً آخر، غير هذا البدن، وجب حصول هذا الشك ، علمنا : أن القول بأن الإنسان شيء غير هذا البدن ، قول باطل
The third proof: If the soul were an abstract substance, having a connection to this body by disposal and management, it would not be unlikely in the beginning of reason that the connection of this soul to this body be severed, and it become connected to a second body, and vice versa. On this assumption, if we see Zayd's body, then see it afterwards, we must remain in doubt whether this human is the first human. Because on the assumption that the first manager of this body has been replaced by another manager, then that human has been replaced by another. Just as when we see a house and we allow that the owner of that house has transferred to another owner, then at that time we do not decide upon seeing that house the second time that its owner is the first owner. Rather we remain in doubt about it. So likewise here we must remain in doubt whether this human whom we see now is the human whom we saw first. And since this doubt does not occur to any rational person, and it is established that on the assumption that the human is something other than this body, this doubt must occur, we know that the statement that the human is something other than this body is a false statement.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument: if the soul were an abstract substance that manages the body, it could theoretically leave one body and attach to another. If that were possible, then when we see a familiar body after a time, we could never be sure that the same soul is still managing it—just as we cannot be sure a house has the same owner if ownership can change. However, in reality, we never have such doubt about the identity of a person. Therefore, the assumption that the human is something other than the body leads to a consequence that does not occur, so the assumption is false.

Key Terms
نفس
جوهر مجرد
تعلق
مدبر
شك
بدن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument relies on physical continuity of the body to establish identity.
Correction: In fact, Razi's argument is about the conceptual possibility of soul transfer leading to inevitable doubt, not about physical continuity. The doubt arises from the possibility of a different soul managing the same body, not from body change.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Razi use to illustrate the problem of identity?
A) A horse and rider
B) A house and its changing owner
C) A river and its flow
D) A lamp and its flame

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that the abstract soul theory leads to an absurd consequence (universal doubt about personal identity), which contradicts our actual certainty, so the theory must be false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are absolutely certain that you are the same person you were yesterday. But if your soul could have been swapped while you slept, that certainty would be baseless. Why, then, do you take it for granted that you are 'you'? What does this tangible certainty tell you about the reality Allah has placed in your heart? Will you thank Him today for that gift of self-awareness?
?
Razi uses the example of a house and its owner to show that doubt would be unavoidable if identity were not tied to the body. Think of something you own that you are certain is yours—your own body. Has there ever been a moment when you doubted you were the same person? How does this rare doubt highlight the constant certainty Allah grants you? Commit to praising Him for that stability.
?
If the soul could be transferred, then your deeds might belong to someone else. How does this argument reinforce that you alone are accountable for your actions? Will you examine your life today with the conviction that every deed is truly yours, and you will answer for it before Allah?

Negative Attributes and the Human Soul: Razi's Argument and Objection
Razi presents a reductio argument and an objection regarding negative attributes of the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of Razi's reductio ad absurdum argument concerning negative attributes of the human soul and the objection that negative restrictions are not part of the essence.

Core Idea
Negative attributes do not define the essence; knowledge of the essence does not require knowing its negations.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a subtle argument about the impossibility of the human soul having the same negative attributes as God (e.g., neither inside nor outside the world). He shows that if it did, we would have necessary knowledge of it, which we don't. The objection, however, points out that negative attributes are not part of the essence, so the argument fails. This lesson unpacks that logical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then follow the literal translation. Let the author's own argument and objection come through before analyzing.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if ... then ...'.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum. Find the hypothetical premise, the stated consequence, and the conclusion that the antecedent is false.
The premise: the soul is described with negative attributes like God. The consequence: necessary knowledge of that. The denial: we lack such knowledge.
3
Examine the objection
The respondent distinguishes between knowledge of the essence (māhiyya) and knowledge of its negative restrictions. Why does this distinction invalidate the argument?
Negative attributes are external to the essence, so their absence of knowledge does not disprove them.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this logical distinction applies to broader theological discussions about God's attributes and human knowledge.
This passage shows the limits of analogical reasoning from human to divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما أن الباري سبحانه وتعالى موجود لا داخل العالم، ولا خارجاً عنه، ولا متصلاً بالعالم، ولا منفصلا عنه، لكنت أعلم بالضرورة: أني موجود وموصوف بهذه الصفة. ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل. لأن كثيراً من العقلاء زعموا: أن إثبات هذا الموجود بهذه الصفة: محال في العقول، حتى قالوا: إن إله العالم يجب أن يكون متحيزاً، ومختصاً بالحيز والجهة. وأما العقلاء الذين سلموا ثبوت هذا المعنى في حق الله تعالى، فلم يخطر ببال أحد منهم: أنه في نفسه موصوف بهذه الصفة. فثبت: أن نفس الإنسان، لو كان موصوفاً بهذه الصفة، لكان العلم بكونه كذلك، علماً ضرورياً، وثبت: أن التالي باطل، فكان المقدم باطلاً. ولمجيب أن يجيب عنه، فيقول: هذه القيود سلبية، والعلم بالماهية، مغاير للعلم بقيودها السلبية، وغير موجب له.
Just as the Creator, glorified and exalted, is existent—not inside the world, nor outside it, nor connected to the world, nor separate from it—then I would necessarily know that I am existent and described with this attribute. And it is known that this is false. Because many of the rational thinkers claimed that the affirmation of this existent with this attribute is impossible in the intellects, to the point that they said: The God of the world must be occupying space and specific to location and direction. As for the rational thinkers who affirmed the existence of this meaning for God Almighty, it did not occur to any of them that he himself is described with this attribute. So it is established that the human self, if it were described with this attribute, then knowledge of it being so would be necessary knowledge. And it is established that the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. And a respondent may answer him, saying: These are negative restrictions, and knowledge of the essence is different from knowledge of its negative restrictions, and does not entail it.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument and an objection-response. The argument begins by positing that if the human self (nafs) were characterized by the same negative attributes as the Creator (neither inside nor outside the world, neither connected nor separate), then one would necessarily know that one's self is such. But this knowledge does not exist; many thinkers deny such attributes for God, and even those who affirm them do not claim self-knowledge of them. Therefore, the premise that the human self is described with these negative attributes is false. The objection counters that these attributes are negative (salbīya), and knowledge of the essence (māhiyya) is distinct from knowledge of its negative attributes; thus, the inference from the absence of knowledge does not disprove the attribute. The passage thus illustrates a logical argument about the implications of negative theology for the human soul.

Key Terms
الباري
العقلاء
قيود سلبية
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the argument proves the soul does not exist or has no negative attributes at all.
Correction: The argument only targets a specific set of negative attributes (like God's). The objection clarifies that negative restrictions are not essential, so the argument's inference is flawed.

Checkpoint
What is the objection to the argument that if the human soul had negative attributes like God, knowledge of it would be necessary?
A) The premises are false because the soul is immaterial.
B) Negative attributes do not entail knowledge of the essence.
C) The soul is not a substance that can be known.
D) The argument is circular and assumes what it tries to prove.

Key Takeaway
The objection highlights that negative attributes are external to the essence, so knowledge of the essence does not require knowledge of its negations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often define yourself by what you are not—'I am not arrogant, not a liar, not a hypocrite.' But if knowledge of your essence is separate from these negations, how can you be sure you truly know yourself? What positive attributes of your soul have you neglected while focusing on negations?
?
If necessary knowledge of the soul's attributes is absent in this life, how certain are you that your own existence is real? What does this lesson reveal about the limits of self-awareness, and how does that affect your confidence in knowing God by negation?
?
The objection separates essence from its negative restrictions. Consider your relationship with Allah: do you worship a God you define more by what He is not (e.g., 'not like creation') than by what He is (e.g., 'Ar-Rahman')? This week, which positive attribute of Allah will you actively meditate on to deepen your connection, moving beyond mere negations?

The Seventh Argument: The Body as Locus of Perceptions
Al-Razi presents the seventh argument and proves the first premise that the body is the locus of perceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi argues that the body must be the locus of perceptions based on a dichotomy and self-evident experience.

Core Idea
The body cannot be inanimate because we feel pain — perception proves the body is alive.

Al-Razi introduces the seventh argument for identifying the human with the body. He begins by proving a key premise: the body is the locus of perceptions. Through a simple dichotomy and appeal to undeniable sensory experience, he establishes that the body possesses a power of sensation. This passage is foundational for understanding his materialist theory of the human.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument's structure
Read the passage once to see the flow: the seventh argument is stated, then the first premise is introduced, and its proof is given.
The argument is built on premises; this lesson focuses on the first premise.
2
Identify the dichotomy
Find where al-Razi presents two options: either the body has perception or it does not. Notice he rejects the second option.
3
Why the second option is impossible
Understand that a body without perception is pure inanimate (jamād). Al-Razi argues that we know by necessity that living bodies are not inanimate.
4
Reflect on the self-evident proof
Consider the example of touching a hot coal: the pain proves sensation is present in the body. This is presented as undeniable self-evident knowledge.
This ties the abstract argument to concrete experience.
5
Connect to the conclusion
Notice how the passage concludes that animal bodies have a power of sensation, and that this is what is meant by 'the self perceives particulars'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : هذا الجسد محل للإدراكات الجزئية والكلية ، ومحل للقدرة على الإدراكات والتحريكات الإرادية . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يكون هذا الجسد وما فيه هو الإنسان . فهذه الحجة مبنية على مقدمات المقدمة الأولى : وهي إن هذا الجسد محل للإدراكات . فالدليل عليه : أن هذا الجسد . إما أن يقال : حصل فيه قوة شعور وإدراك ، أو لم يحصل فيه البتة قوة الشعور والإدراك . والقسم الثاني : باطل . لأن كل جسم يكون خالياً عن قوة الشعور والإدراك ، يكون جماداً محضاً ، فإنا لا نعقل بين الجماد وبين الحيوان : فرقاً إلا هذا المعنى، وهو أن الجماد لا شعور له البتة ، ولا إدراك له البتة ، فلو كان جسم البدن خالياً عن الشعور والإدراك ، لكان جماداً محضاً . لكنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن أبدان الحيوانات حال كونها أحياء ، ليست جمادات محضة . وكيف لا نقول ذلك ، وإن من وضع إصبعه على الجمر فإنه يحس بالألم في ذلك الموضع ؟ ولو وضع إصبعه على الجمر ، فإنه يحس بنوع آخر من الألم في ذلك الموضع . فالقول بأن البدن جماد محض لا شعور فيه ، ولا إدراك : إنكار لأعظم البديهيات فثبت : أنه حصل في أبدان الحيوانات : قوة شعور وإحساس بالأشياء المخالفة والموافقة ولا نريد من قولنا : إن النفس مدركة للجزئيات إلا ذلك
The seventh argument: This body is a locus for particular and universal perceptions, and a locus for power over perceptions and voluntary movements. And when it is so, then it must be that this body and what is in it is the human. So this argument is built on premises. The first premise: That this body is a locus for perceptions. The proof of it: That this body—either it is said that a power of sensation and perception occurred in it, or that it did not at all occur in it a power of sensation and perception. And the second division is false. Because every body that is devoid of the power of sensation and perception is pure inanimate matter; for we do not conceive between the inanimate and the living any difference except this meaning: that the inanimate has no sensation at all and no perception at all. So if the body of the organism were devoid of sensation and perception, it would be pure inanimate. But we know necessarily that the bodies of animals while they are alive are not pure inanimates. And how can we not say that? Indeed, whoever places his finger on a burning coal feels pain in that spot. And if he were to place his finger on a burn, he would feel another type of pain in that spot. So the statement that the body is pure inanimate with no sensation in it and no perception is a denial of the greatest self-evident truths. So it is established that in the bodies of animals there occurred a power of sensation and feeling for things that are contrary and agreeing; and we do not intend by our saying 'the self perceives particulars' except that.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument (al-ḥujja al-sābiʿa) concerning the identity of the human. The argument states that the body is the locus for both particular and universal perceptions and for voluntary movements, therefore the body and what is in it (i.e., its powers) is the human. The argument is built on premises. The first premise is that the body is a locus for perceptions. The proof is a dichotomy: either the body has sensation/perception or it doesn't. The second option is false because any body devoid of sensation is pure inanimate (jamād), and the only difference between inanimate and living is sensation. Animal bodies while alive are not inanimate—this is known by necessity, as can be seen from the pain felt when touching a hot coal, or different pains from different sources. Denying this would negate the greatest self-evident truths. Thus, animal bodies possess a power of sensation. The passage clarifies that by 'the self (nafs) perceives particulars' they mean just this bodily sensation.

Key Terms
الجسد
الإدراكات الجزئية والكلية
الجماد
البديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument equates the human with the physical body alone, ignoring any spiritual or mental aspect.
Correction: The text specifies 'this body and what is in it' (الجسد وما فيه), including the body's faculties or powers, not just the physical matter. The conclusion is about the composite of body and its inherent powers.

Checkpoint
According to the first premise, why can the body not be devoid of perception?
A) Because every body has perception inherently.
B) Because animals are alive and have perception, and a body without perception is inanimate.
C) Because the soul provides perception to the body.
D) Because perception is a necessary property of all matter.

Key Takeaway
The body's ability to feel pain and other sensations demonstrates it is the locus of perception, proving it is not inanimate but alive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain or pleasure, do you ever pause to thank Allah for the gift of perception? This argument shows that such sensations are proof that you are alive and not inanimate. How can you turn every sensation into a moment of gratitude and mindfulness of your Creator?
?
Al-Razi uses the undeniable experience of pain to prove a profound truth. Are there self-evident signs of Allah's existence and mercy that you have become numb to? What would it take for you to see them again as immediate and compelling as the pain of touching fire?
?
If your body were truly inanimate, you would feel nothing. But you do feel—so you are alive. Do you live as if you are truly alive to the truth, or have you let your senses become dulled to spiritual realities? Will you commit today to using your perceptions as reminders of the One who gave them to you?

The Body's Perception of Particulars: From Pain to Proof
Al-Razi establishes the body's perception of particulars via pain experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that animal bodies are not mere inanimate matter because they possess consciousness and perception of particulars, as evidenced by the experience of pain.

Core Idea
Pain proves the body perceives particulars.

Al-Razi begins by asking whether the body itself has any awareness. He argues that if the body were purely inanimate, it would feel nothing. But our direct experience of pain—distinct types of pain from different stimuli—shows that living bodies have a power of sensation. This foundational point establishes that the body perceives particular sensations.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the first part of the passage where al-Razi contrasts inanimate matter with living bodies.
Notice how he uses the example of touching a hot coal.
2
Identify the key premise
Al-Razi states that we know necessarily that animal bodies are not pure inanimate matter. Identify the evidence he gives for this.
He points to the experience of pain as self-evident.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that there is a power of consciousness and sensation in the body, which is what we mean by the soul perceiving particulars.
This sets up the next lesson on universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا هذا المعنى، وهو أن الجماد لا شعور له البتة، ولا إدراك له البتة، فلو كان جسم البدن خالياً عن الشعور والإدراك، لكان جماداً محضاً. لكنا نعلم بالضرورة: أن أبدان الحيوانات حال كونها أحياء، ليست جمادات محضة. وكيف لا نقول ذلك، وإن من وضع إصبعه على الجمر فإنه يحس بالألم في ذلك الموضع؟ ولو وضع إصبعه على الجمر، فإنه يحس بنوع آخر من الألم في ذلك الموضع. فالقول بأن البدن جماد محض لا شعور فيه، ولا إدراك: إنكار لأعظم البديهيات. فثبت: أنه حصل في أبدان الحيوانات: قوة شعور وإحساس بالأشياء المخالفة والموافقة. ولا نريد من قولنا: إن النفس مدركة للجزئيات إلا ذلك. والمقدمة الثانية: في بيان أن هذا البدن مدرك للكليات. فنقول: الدليل على صحة هذه المقدمة: إنا بينا: أن البدن مدرك للجزئيات، وكل ما كان مدركاً للجزئيات، فإنه يجب أن يكون مدركاً للكليات. والدليل عليه: إن هذا الألم عبارة عن الألم مع قيد كونه هذا، ومدرك المركب، لا بد وأن يكون مدركاً لمفرداته، لما ثبت في المنطق في باب الذاتي المقوم: إن تصور المركب، مسبوق بتصور مفرداته. فثبت: أن مدرك هذا الألم مدرك للألم، والألم ماهية كلية، فثبت: أن مدرك الجزئيات، يجب أن يكون مدركاً للكليات. وأيضاً: قد بينا أن البدن يبصر هذا الإنسان، وهذا الإنسان عبارة عن الإنسان مع قيد كونه هذا. فمبصر هذا الإنسان يجب أن يكون قد أبصر الإنسان. وأبصر كونه هذا. لكن الإنسان من حيث إنه إنسان ماهية كلية. فالقوة الباصرة قد أدركت الماهية الكلية. فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن القوة الباصرة لما أدركت هذا الإنسان، فهي ما أدركت إلا مجرد كونه هذا، فأما الإنسان فهو غير مبصر. وأيضاً: فهب أن القوة الباصرة أدركت الإنسان إلا أن الإنسان من حيث إنه إنسان، لا كلي ولا جزئي، ولكنه إنسان فقط، فلا يلزم منه أن تكون القوة الباصرة مدركة للكليات. فنجيب عن الأول: بأنا إذا أدركنا هذا السواد وهذا البياض. فإما أن يكون المرئي، مجرد كون هذا: هذا. وذاك: ذاك. من غير أن تتعلق القوة الباصرة بماهية السواد والبياض، وإما أن يكون المرئي مجموع كونه هذا. كونه سواداً، أو مجموع كونه ذاك بياضاً. أما الأول فباطل لوجوه: أحدها: إنا قد دللنا على أن التعين قيد عدمي، فيمتنع كونه مرئياً. وثانيها: إن بتقدير أن يكون التعين أمراً ثابتاً، فإن تعين هذا وتعين ذاك. كل واحد منهما يتشاركان في كونه تعينا، فهذا التعين وذلك التعين شخصان داخلان تحت نوع واحد، ولا مخالفة بينهما في الماهية أصلا. فإذا أحسسنا بهذا السواد وهذا البياض، وفرضنا أن المحسوس ليس إلا مجرد كونه هذا وذاك، وثبت أن مجرد كونه هذا وذاك شخصان داخلان تحت نوع واحد وأنه لا مخالفة بينهما في الماهية، فحينئذ يلزم أن لا يحس من طريق الإبصار بكون السواد والبياض متخالفين. ولما كان ذلك باطلاً بالبديهة، علمنا: أنه ليس المحسوس بحس البصر، هو مجرد كونه هذا وذاك، بل كونه سواداً أو بياضاً أيضاً محسوس بحس البصر. وثالثها: إن بتقدير أن يكون التعين أمراً ثابتاً، وأن يكون المحسوس هو التعين. إلا أن التعين أيضاً ماهية كلية فيكون المحسوس بالبصر أيضاً أمراً كلياً. إلا أن يقال: المحسوس ليس هو التعين بل تعين التعين. ثم يعود الكلام الأول فيه، ويلزم التسلسل.
Except this meaning, which is that an inanimate object has no consciousness at all and no perception at all. So if the body were devoid of consciousness and perception, it would be pure inanimate matter. But we know necessarily that the bodies of animals while they are alive are not pure inanimate matter. How can we not say that, when whoever places his finger on a hot coal feels pain in that spot? And if he places his finger on a hot coal, he feels another kind of pain in that spot. So the claim that the body is pure inanimate matter, having no consciousness or perception, is a denial of the greatest self-evident truths. Thus it is established that in the bodies of animals there is a power of consciousness and sensation of things that are contrary and agreeable. And by our statement 'the soul perceives particulars' we mean nothing other than that. The second premise: concerning the clarification that this body perceives universals. We say: the proof for the validity of this premise is that we have shown that the body perceives particulars, and everything that perceives particulars must perceive universals. The proof of this is that this pain is an expression of pain with the qualification of being this, and whoever perceives a compound must perceive its components, because it is established in logic in the chapter on the essential constituent that the conception of a compound is preceded by the conception of its components. So it is established that the perceiver of this pain is a perceiver of pain, and pain is a universal quiddity. So it is established that the perceiver of particulars must perceive universals. Also, we have shown that the body sees this human, and this human is an expression of human with the qualification of being this. So the seer of this human must have seen the human and seen its being this. But human insofar as it is human is a universal quiddity. So the visual faculty has perceived the universal quiddity. If it is said: why is it not permissible to say that when the visual faculty perceived this human, it perceived only the mere being this, while the human is not seen? Also, suppose the visual faculty perceived the human, but the human insofar as it is human is neither universal nor particular, but only human, so it does not follow that the visual faculty perceives universals. We answer the first [objection] as follows: When we perceive this blackness and this whiteness, either the visible is the mere being this being this and that being that, without the visual faculty attaching to the quiddity of blackness and whiteness, or the visible is the composite of its being this and its being blackness, or the composite of its being that and its being whiteness. The first is false for several reasons. First: We have demonstrated that determination (taʿayyun) is a privative condition, so it is impossible for it to be visible. Second: Even if determination is assumed to be something fixed, then this determination and that determination each share in being determination, so this determination and that determination are two individuals falling under one species, with no difference in quiddity at all. So if we sense this blackness and this whiteness, and we assume that the sensed is nothing but the mere being this and that, and it is established that the mere being this and that are two individuals under one species with no difference in quiddity, then it follows that through sight we would not sense that blackness and whiteness are different. Since that is false by intuition, we know that the object of sight is not merely being this and that, but that being blackness or whiteness is also sensed by sight. Third: Even if determination is assumed to be something fixed and the sensed is the determination, yet determination is also a universal quiddity, so the object of sight would also be a universal, unless it is said that the sensed is not determination but the determination of determination. Then the first argument would recur, and an infinite regress would result.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative and refutational passage in kalam epistemology. The author (al-Razi) first establishes that animals' bodies are not mere inanimate matter because they feel pain, thereby showing that bodies have consciousness and perceive particulars. Then he moves to the second premise: the body also perceives universals. The proof: since the body perceives a determinate particular (e.g., this pain), and that particular is a compound of a universal essence (pain) and a determination (this), and perceiving a compound requires perceiving its components (as per logic), the body must perceive the universal essence (pain). Similarly, seeing this human requires seeing the universal 'human'. An objection is raised: perhaps the visual faculty perceives only the individuation ('this-ness'), not the universal. Also, even if it perceives the human, the human as such is neither universal nor particular. The author answers that if we only perceived individuation, then black and white would not be distinguished because their individuations share the same universal nature (being a determination); but we clearly see them as different, so we must perceive their essences (blackness, whiteness). Additionally, if determination itself is a universal (since it is a shared nature), then the objection leads to infinite regress. Thus the body does perceive universals.

Key Terms
جزئيات (particulars)
إدراك (perception)
بديهة (intuition/self-evident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The body is pure inanimate matter without any consciousness or perception.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that our direct experience of pain proves that the body has consciousness and perception of particulars.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what demonstrates that animal bodies are not pure inanimate matter?
A) The fact that animals move voluntarily.
B) The experience of pain when touching a hot coal.
C) The ability to reason about universals.
D) The fact that bodies have shape and size.

Key Takeaway
The body's ability to feel pain demonstrates that it is not mere matter but has perception of particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience physical pain, how does it confirm for you that your body is not just a machine but has a God-given faculty of perception? What difference does this truth make in how you treat your body?
?
Al-Razi points out that denying the evidence of pain is denying a self-evident truth. Are there areas in your spiritual life where you deny obvious signs because they are uncomfortable? Will you commit to facing one such sign this week?
?
If the body's sensation of pain is a proof of a deeper reality, what other physical experiences might be pointing you toward a greater truth about your soul? How can you become more attentive to these pointers?

How Particular Perception Entails Universal Perception
Al-Razi argues that perception of a particular necessitates perception of its universal component.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's logical argument that perceiving a particular (e.g., this pain) necessarily involves perceiving the universal essence (pain) because the particular is a compound of universal and individuation.

Core Idea
Particulars are compounds; perceiving them requires perceiving universals.

Having established that the body perceives particulars, al-Razi now argues that this necessarily implies perception of universals. His key insight is that a determinate particular like 'this pain' is a composite of a universal essence ('pain') and a specific determination ('this-ness'). According to the logical principle that one must know the parts of a compound before knowing the whole, perceiving 'this pain' requires perceiving pain itself, which is a universal quiddity.

Guided Path
1
Read the second premise and its proof
Al-Razi states that the body perceives particulars, and every perceiver of particulars must perceive universals. Read his proof carefully.
He uses the example of 'this pain' to illustrate.
2
Understand the compound analysis
He analyzes 'this pain' as 'pain with the qualification of being this'. This shows that the particular is a compound of a universal (pain) and an individuation (this).
The logical principle: conceiving a compound requires conceiving its parts.
3
Consider the second example
Al-Razi also gives the example of seeing 'this human', which is a compound of 'human' (universal) and 'this' (individuation).
This reinforces the same point.

محضة. وكيف لا نقول ذلك، وإن من وضع إصبعه على الجمر فإنه يحس بالألم في ذلك الموضع؟ ولو وضع إصبعه على الجمر، فإنه يحس بنوع آخر من الألم في ذلك الموضع. فالقول بأن البدن جماد محض لا شعور فيه، ولا إدراك: Commentary

Refuting the Objection: The Individuation Argument and Infinite Regress
Al-Razi refutes the objection that only individuation is perceived and shows the necessity of perceiving universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's refutation of the objection that the body perceives only individuation, not universals, and his argument that this leads to absurdity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
If we only perceived individuation, we couldn't differentiate between qualities like black and white.

Al-Razi considers a plausible objection: perhaps when we see a particular human, we only perceive its 'this-ness' (individuation), not the universal 'human'. He also considers the claim that human as such is neither universal nor particular. In his detailed response, he uses the example of seeing black and white to show that if we only perceived individuation, we could not distinguish them, because all individuations share the same universal nature. Furthermore, he argues that if the sensed object is determination itself, that too is a universal, and denying that leads to an infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Al-Razi presents two objections: (1) the visual faculty perceives only the 'this-ness', not the universal; (2) even if it perceives 'human', that is neither universal nor particular.
Understand the challenge before reading the response.
2
Follow the first answer: a dilemma
Al-Razi poses a dilemma: either we perceive only bare individuation (this-ness) or a composite of individuation and essence. He argues the first is false for three reasons.
Pay attention to the three reasons, especially the second.
3
Study the second and third reasons
The second reason: if we perceived only individuation, all perceptions would be indistinguishable (since all individuations are alike in nature). The third reason: determination itself is a universal; to deny that leads to infinite regress.
These reasons show that universals must be perceived.

Except this meaning, which is that an inanimate object has no consciousness at all and no perception at all. How can we not say that, when whoever places his finger on a hot coal feels pain in that spot? And if he places his finger on a hot coal, he feels another kind of pain in that spot. So the claim that the body is pure inanimate matter, having no consciousness or perception, is a denial of the greatest self-evident truths. Thus it is established that in the bodies of animals there is a power of consciousness and sensation of things that are contrary and agreeable. And by our statement 'the soul perceives particulars' we mean nothing other than that. The second premise: concerning the clarification that this body perceives universals. We say: the proof for the validity of this premise is that we have shown that the body perceives particulars, and everything that perceives particulars must perceive universals. The proof of this is that this pain is an expression of pain with the qualification of being this, and whoever perceives a compound must perceive its components, because it is established in logic in the chapter on the essential constituent that the conception of a compound is preceded by the conception of its components. So it is established that the perceiver of this pain is a perceiver of pain, and pain is a universal quiddity. So it is established that the perceiver of particulars must perceive universals. Also, we have shown that the body sees this human, and this human is an expression of human with the qualification of being this. So the seer of this human must have seen the human and seen its being this. But human insofar as it is human is a universal quiddity. So the visual faculty has perceived the universal quiddity. If it is said: why is it not permissible to say that when the visual faculty perceived this human, it perceived only the mere being this, while the human is not seen? Also, suppose the visual faculty perceived the human, but the human insofar as it is human is neither universal nor particular, but only human, so it does not follow that the visual faculty perceives universals. We answer the first [objection] as follows: When we perceive this blackness and this whiteness, either the visible is the mere being this being this and that being that, without the visual faculty attaching to the quiddity of blackness and whiteness, or the visible is the composite of its being this and its being blackness, or the composite of its being that and its being whiteness. The first is false for several reasons. First: We have demonstrated that determination (taʿayyun) is a privative condition, so it is impossible for it to be visible. Second: Even if determination is assumed to be something fixed, then this determination and that determination each share in being determination, so this determination and that determination are two individuals falling under one species, with no difference in quiddity at all. So if we sense this blackness and this whiteness, and we assume that the sensed is nothing but the mere being this and that, and it is established that the mere being this and that are two individuals under one species with no difference in quiddity, then it follows that through sight we would not sense that blackness and whiteness are different. Since that is false by intuition, we know that the object of sight is not merely being this and that, but that being blackness or whiteness is also sensed by sight. Third: Even if determination is assumed to be something fixed and the sensed is the determination, yet determination is also a universal quiddity, so the object of sight would also be a universal, unless it is said that the sensed is not determination but the determination of determination. Then the first argument would recur, and an infinite regress would result.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative and refutational passage in kalam epistemology. The author (al-Razi) first establishes that animals' bodies are not mere inanimate matter because they feel pain, thereby showing that bodies have consciousness and perceive particulars. Then he moves to the second premise: the body also perceives universals. The proof: since the body perceives a determinate particular (e.g., this pain), and that particular is a compound of a universal essence (pain) and a determination (this), and perceiving a compound requires perceiving its components (as per logic), the body must perceive the universal essence (pain). Similarly, seeing this human requires seeing the universal 'human'. An objection is raised: perhaps the visual faculty perceives only the individuation ('this-ness'), not the universal. Also, even if it perceives the human, the human as such is neither universal nor particular. The author answers that if we only perceived individuation, then black and white would not be distinguished because their individuations share the same universal nature (being a determination); but we clearly see them as different, so we must perceive their essences (blackness, whiteness). Additionally, if determination itself is a universal (since it is a shared nature), then the objection leads to infinite regress. Thus the body does perceive universals.

Key Terms
تعين (determination/individuation)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
ماهية (quiddity/essence)
بديهة (intuition/self-evident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We perceive only the individuation (this-ness) of an object, not any universal essence.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that if we only perceived individuation, we couldn't distinguish between different qualities like black and white; therefore we must perceive their essences.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi's second reason against bare this-ness demonstrate?
A) That determination is a privative condition.
B) That if we perceived only determination, we would not be able to differentiate black from white because all determinations share the same quiddity.
C) That determination is a universal quiddity.
D) That infinite regress is impossible.

Key Takeaway
If we only perceived individuation, we couldn't distinguish between different qualities; moreover, the very concept of determination is universal and leads to infinite regress if rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world, do you only see isolated objects and events, or do you recognize the universal qualities (like justice, mercy, beauty) that give them meaning? How does this affect your ability to see God's signs in creation?
?
Al-Razi shows that denying perception of universals leads to absurdity. Consider a time when you reduced someone to a single action or label, ignoring the universal human dignity they share. What damage did that do, and how can you practice seeing the quiddity (essence) behind every particular?
?
The argument against infinite regress reveals that if you keep pushing back the basis for perception, you end in contradiction. In your own spiritual life, are you avoiding ultimate truths by hiding behind endless details? Commit today to stop the regress and affirm the universal principles of faith.

How We Perceive Universals: The Three Types
The author answers the objection that we do not perceive universals with the sense of sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the logical, intellectual, and natural universal, and how the natural universal is perceived through sensory experience.

Core Idea
The natural universal is directly perceived by the senses, not just abstract thought.

The author has been defending the claim that we perceive universals through sensory sight. Now he answers the objection that sight only perceives particulars. By distinguishing three meanings of 'universal', he clarifies that the natural universal—the essence itself—is what the senses grasp. This lesson unpacks that key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author sets up the objection and then answers it.
2
Identify the three types of universal
The passage lists three: logical universal (universality itself), intellectual universal (essence plus the condition of being universal), natural universal (the essence that can be judged universal). Spot each one.
3
Follow the author's argument
Notice which type the author focuses on (natural universal) and why the others are irrelevant here. Then see the conclusion: perceiving particulars implies perceiving natural universals.
4
Reflect on the objection answered
Ask yourself: how does this distinction resolve the objection that sight only sees particulars? The answer is that the natural universal is inherent in the particular sensed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت بهذه الوجوه : إنا إذا أحسنا بهذا السواد وبهذا البياض ، فقد حصل الإحساس بالسواد والبياض وأما السؤال الثاني ، وهو قوله : ( هب أنا أدركنا السواد بحس البصر ، لكن لم قلتم : إنا أدركنا بحس البصر السواد من حيث إنه كلي ) ؟ فجوابه : إنا قد بينا في المنطق : أن الكلي قد يراد به الكلي المنطقي ، وهو نفس الكلية ، وقد يراد به الكلي العقلي وهو مجموع الماهية مع قيد كونها كلية . وقد يراد به الكلي الطبيعي ، وهي تلك الماهية التي يمكن أن يحكم العقل عليها ، بكونها كلية. وكلامنا في هذا المقام في بيان أن الكلي الطبيعي مدرك بالقوة الحساسة . أما الكلي المنطقي والعقلي فلا حاجة إلى ذكرهما في هذا الباب . ثبت بما ذكرنا : إن النفس مدركة للجزئيات ، وكل ما كان مدركاً للجزيئات ، فإنه يجب أن يكون مدركاً للكليات الطبيعية
So it is established by these aspects: when we sense this blackness and this whiteness, the sensation of blackness and whiteness occurs. As for the second question, which is his saying: 'Assume that we perceived blackness by the sense of sight, but why did you say that we perceived by the sense of sight blackness inasmuch as it is universal?' The answer: we have explained in logic that the universal may refer to the logical universal, which is universality itself, and it may refer to the intellectual universal, which is the totality of the essence with the condition of its being universal, and it may refer to the natural universal, which is that essence that the intellect can judge to be universal. Our discussion in this context is to explain that the natural universal is perceived by the sensitive power. As for the logical and intellectual universal, there is no need to mention them in this chapter. It is established by what we have mentioned: that the soul perceives particulars, and everything that perceives particulars must perceive natural universals.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The author first states a conclusion from previous arguments: sensory perception of blackness and whiteness occurs. Then he addresses a second objection: how can we claim to perceive blackness as a universal through sight? The answer draws from logic, distinguishing three types of universal: (1) the logical universal, which is the concept of universality itself; (2) the intellectual universal, the essence along with the attribute of being universal; (3) the natural universal, the essence that the intellect can judge to be universal. The author clarifies that his concern is only with the natural universal, which is perceived by the sensory faculty. The logical and intellectual universals are not relevant here. The conclusion is that since the soul perceives particulars, it must also perceive natural universals.

The Body's Capacity for Voluntary Movement
Proof that voluntary agency requires perception, so body is agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that the body (or something in it) must be the agent of voluntary movement, based on the necessity of perception for choice.

Core Idea
Voluntary movement requires perception; the body perceives, so it must be the mover.

This passage presents a key premise in a larger argument about the soul and body. The author argues that voluntary movement cannot originate from a non-perceiving entity. Since the body is the seat of perception, it follows that the body—or something within it—must be the voluntary agent. Let's see how the proof unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the conditional structure: choice requires perception.
2
Identify the proof structure
The passage contains a premise, a proof, and a conclusion. Break it into: (1) The third premise stated, (2) The proof: voluntary mover acts by intention/choice, (3) Choice requires perception, (4) Therefore the agent must be perceiving, (5) Since body perceives, agent is body or in it.
3
Understand the necessity of perception
The author argues that choice of a thing is conditioned upon perception of its quiddity (essence). Without conception, intending to bring it about is impossible. This is the critical link.
This step is the heart of the proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the voluntary agent must be the body or something existing in the body. Consider how this fits with the author's overall position on the soul.
This conclusion supports a corporeal or immanent view of the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثالثة : فهي في بيان أن البدن موصوف بالقدرة على التحريكات الإرادية . والدليل عليه : أن المحرك بالإرادة هو الذي يحرك بواسطة القصد والاختيار . واختيار الشيء مشروط بإدراك ماهيته ، فإن ما لا يكون متصوراً ولا مشعوراً به ، فإنه يمتنع القصد إلى تكوينه . فثبت : أن الفاعل المختار يجب أن يكون مدركاً. فلما كان البدن هو المدرك ، وجب أن يكون الفاعل المختار : هو البدن ، أو شيء موجود في البدن
As for the third premise: it is explaining that the body is described as having the power to cause voluntary movements. The proof for it is that the mover by will is one who moves by means of intention and choice. And choice of a thing is conditioned upon perception of its quiddity, for whatever is not conceived nor perceived, it is impossible to intend to bring it about. So it is established that the agent by choice must be perceiving. Since the body is the perceiver, it follows that the agent by choice must be the body, or something existing in the body.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a proof (premise) in a larger argument. The author states the third premise: the body possesses the power for voluntary movements. The proof is: a voluntary mover acts through intention and choice; choice requires perception of the thing's essence; therefore, the voluntary agent must be perceiving. Since it has been established that the body perceives (from previous arguments), the voluntary agent must either be the body itself or something within it. This supports the conclusion that the soul is corporeal or exists in the body.

The Human is the Body or Something Within It
The author argues that since the body has perception and voluntary movement, the human is the body or something within it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth premise in al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which argues that the human is the body or something within it based on the body's possession of perceptions and voluntary movements.

Core Idea
The human is the body itself, not a separate immaterial soul.

This lesson examines the fourth premise of a larger argument. The author seeks to establish what the human truly is. By looking at the body's attributes—its ability to perceive and move voluntarily—he concludes that the human must be the body or something within it. This challenges the common notion of a purely immaterial soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a conditional structure: 'since... then...'.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the three attributes: partial perceptions, universal perceptions, and voluntary movements. Understand that these are what characterize the body.
'Partial perceptions' refer to sensory perceptions of particulars, while 'universal perceptions' refer to intellectual grasp of universals.
3
Follow the logical argument
Trace the reasoning: the body has these attributes → the substance of the body is described by them → the human is nothing but that substance → therefore the human is the body or something within it.
The conclusion is drawn with certainty: 'that necessitates the certainty'.
4
Consider the alternative
The author considers the possibility of a separate immaterial substance. If such existed, it would be something other than the human. This reinforces the identification of the human with the body.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this premise matters for the author's larger project. How does defining the human as the body affect discussions of the soul, resurrection, or human nature?
This premise is foundational for subsequent arguments in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الرابعة : وهي أنه لما ثبت أن البدن موصوف بالإدراكات الجزئية ، والإدراكات الكلية ، وبالحركات الإرادية، كان جوهر البدن موصوفاً بمجموع هذه الصفات. ولا معنى للإنسان إلا الجوهر الموصوف بهذه الصفات وذلك يوجب القطع بأن الإنسان هو البدن أو جسم موجود فيه وإذا ثبت هذا فلو فرضنا جوهراً مجرداً مفارقاً لهذا البدن ، لكان ذلك الشيء موجوداً مغايراً للإنسان . فيكون الإنسان هو هذا البدن الموصوف بهذه الإدراكات الجزئية والكلية والحركات الإرادية وهذا بحث نفيس
As for the fourth premise: that since it is established that the body is described with partial perceptions, universal perceptions, and voluntary movements, the substance of the body is described with the totality of these attributes. And there is no meaning of 'human' except the substance described with these attributes, and that necessitates the certainty that the human is the body or a body existing in it. And when this is established, if we suppose a purely immaterial substance separate from this body, that thing would exist as something other than the human. Thus the human is this body described with these partial and universal perceptions and voluntary movements. And this is a valuable discussion.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a premise that draws a conclusion from previous ones. The author argues: since the body possesses partial perceptions, universal perceptions, and voluntary movements, the body's substance is characterized by all these attributes. The definition of 'human' is nothing other than the substance possessing these attributes. Therefore, it necessarily follows that the human is either the body itself or a body existing within it (i.e., the physical entity). If we suppose a separate immaterial substance (like an incorporeal soul), it would be something other than the human. Hence, the human is this very body with its perceptions and movements. The author calls this a valuable discussion.

The Eighth Proof for the Soul Being a Body
A proof by contradiction for the soul's corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the eighth proof for the corporeality of the soul and why the soul must be a substance occupying space.

Core Idea
If the soul were immaterial, it could move all bodies without touching them—but it cannot, so it must be a body.

This lesson presents a powerful proof by contradiction for the soul's corporeality. The argument shows that if the soul were an immaterial substance, it would have to be able to move any body without physical contact, which is obviously false. Therefore, the soul must be a material body that moves things through contact.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly. Pay attention to the conditional statements and the conclusion.
This proof is structured as a chain of 'if...then' statements.
2
Identify the main assumption
Find where the proof states what would be the case if the soul were an abstract substance (جوهر مجرد). Highlight that assumption.
3
Trace the logical consequences
Follow the argument step by step: from the assumption to the consequence that the soul could move any body without contact, then to the absurd conclusion that we could move distant objects.
Notice the use of analogy with moving our own body.
4
Understand the contradiction
The proof claims the consequence is self-evidently false (باطل بالبديهة). Confirm that indeed we cannot move objects without contact.
5
See the final conclusion
The proof concludes that the soul must be a substance occupying space (متحيز), i.e., a body. Recognize the phrase 'وهو المطلوب' marking the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة للقائلين بأن النفس جسم : لهم أن يقولوا : لو كانت النفس جوهراً مجرداً عن الحجمية والتحيز ، لامتنع أن يتوقف فعلها على مماسة محل الفعل . لأن ما لا يكون متحيزاً ، امتنع أن يصير مماساً للمتحيز . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فحينئذ يكون فعلها على سبيل الاختراع من غير حاجة إلى حصول مماسة وملاقاة بين الفاعل وبين محل الفعل . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن يقدر الواحد منا على تحريك الأجسام، من غير أن يماسها ، ومن غير أن يماس شيئاً يماسها . وذلك لأن جوهر النفس ، لما كانت قادرة على تحريك البدن من غير حصول مماسة بينها وبين ذلك البدن ، علمنا : أن جوهر النفس قادر على تحريك الجسم من غير واسطة المماسة . وسائر الأجسام قابلة للحركة ، ونسبة جوهر النفس إلى كل الأجسام على السوية ، فلما قدرت النفس على تحريك بعضها من غير المماسة وجب حصول قدرتها على تحريك البقية من غير المماسة ، ولما كان ذلك باطلاً بالبديهة علمنا : أن النفس لا تقوى على التحريك ، إلا بشرط أن تماس ما يماسه . وكل ما كان مماساً لشيء من الأجسام ، فهو متحيز . فوجب أن يكون النفس جوهراً متحيزاً . وهو المطلوب
The eighth proof for those who say the soul is a body: they may say: if the soul were a substance abstracted from magnitude and occupying space, it would be impossible for its action to depend on contact with the locus of the action. Because what does not occupy space cannot become in contact with what occupies space. And if that is the case, then its action would be by way of creation without any need for contact and meeting between the agent and the locus of action. And if that were the case, then each of us would be able to move bodies without touching them, and without touching anything that touches them. That is because the substance of the soul, since it is able to move the body without contact between it and that body, we know that the substance of the soul is able to move a body without the intermediary of contact. And other bodies are receptive to movement, and the relation of the substance of the soul to all bodies is uniform. So since the soul is able to move some of them without contact, its ability to move the rest without contact must hold. And since that is false by necessity, we know that the soul cannot cause movement except on condition that it touches what touches it. And everything that touches something of bodies is occupying space. Therefore the soul must be a substance occupying space. And that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a complete proof by contradiction (burhan) for the corporeality of the soul. The argument proceeds as follows: (1) Assume the soul is an immaterial substance (abstracted from magnitude and location). (2) Then its action cannot depend on contact with the object acted upon, because an immaterial thing cannot contact a material thing. (3) Therefore, the soul should be able to move bodies by sheer creation without any contact. (4) If that were so, then each of us could move any body without touching it or anything that touches it. The reason is that since the soul moves its own body without contact (as per the assumption), and other bodies are equally movable, the soul should move them too. (5) But this is self-evidently false (we cannot move distant objects without contact). (6) Therefore, the soul can only move by means of contact. (7) Whatever touches a body is itself extended and occupying space. (8) Hence, the soul is a corporeal substance. The proof is concluded with the formula 'and that is what is sought' (وهو المطلوب). Note: The text on the page continues with an objection, but it is not included here.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think an immaterial soul can move the body without any physical contact.
Correction: This proof shows that if that were true, we could move any object without contact, which is false. Therefore, the soul must be material and require contact.

Checkpoint

A) The soul is immaterial
B) The soul is a substance occupying space
C) The soul can move bodies without contact
D) The soul is distinct from the body

Key Takeaway
The soul must be a body because only a material substance can move objects through contact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the soul were immaterial and could move your body without contact, what would that imply about your ability to influence others at a distance? Have you ever assumed you could change someone without 'touching' their heart or life? This proof challenges you to consider: in what ways do you rely on unspoken presumptions about your own nature?
?
This proof uses logic to force a conclusion against a comfortable assumption. Think of a belief you hold that you have never tested with rigorous reasoning. Are you willing to subject it to the same kind of scrutiny? If the proof leads to absurdity, will you abandon the belief?
?
The argument says that if the soul can move one body without contact, it must be able to move all bodies. Reflect: Is there an area of your life where you grant yourself an exception that you would not grant to others? Does that exception hold up under the light of consistency?

Eighth Proof: The Soul Must Be a Body Because It Requires Contact to Move Bodies
A proof and objection-answer for the soul's materiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth proof for the materiality of the soul, which argues that if the soul were non-bodily, it could move all bodies without contact, and why the objection that the soul might only need contact for other bodies is invalid.

Core Idea
If the soul were non-bodily, it could move any body without touch — but it can't, so it must be a body.

This passage presents a crucial argument in Islamic philosophical theology that the soul must be a body. The argument proceeds by assuming the opposite and showing it leads to an absurd conclusion. After presenting the proof, the author anticipates and refutes an objection. Understanding this argument clarifies the relationship between the soul and physical causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: proof, objection, answer.
Pay attention to logical connectors like 'لو' (if) and 'ف' (then).
2
Identify the conditional chain
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum. Trace the 'if...then' statements: if soul non-bodily → no contact needed → can move any body without contact → but we know we can't → therefore soul is bodily.
This is the core logical structure.
3
Analyze the objection
An opponent suggests the soul might only need contact for bodies other than its own. Ask yourself why someone might propose this.
The objection tries to break the symmetry argument.
4
Study the answer
The author argues that all bodies are equal in receptivity and the soul's relation to all is equal, so special treatment is impossible. The soul's special attachment to its own body is due to 'love', not a change in essence.
This refutes the objection by showing no difference in capability.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof is considered certain (يقين). Reflect on why this argument is compelling for the materiality of the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة للقائلين بأن النفس جسم : لهم أن يقولوا : لو كانت النفس جوهراً مجرداً عن الحجمية والتحيز ، لامتنع أن يتوقف فعلها على مماسة محل الفعل . لأن ما لا يكون متحيزاً ، امتنع أن يصير مماساً للمتحيز . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فحينئذ يكون فعلها على سبيل الاختراع من غير حاجة إلى حصول مماسة وملاقاة بين الفاعل وبين محل الفعل . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن يقدر الواحد منا على تحريك الأجسام، من غير أن يماسها ، ومن غير أن يماس شيئاً يماسها . وذلك لأن جوهر النفس ، لما كانت قادرة على تحريك البدن من غير حصول مماسة بينها وبين ذلك البدن ، علمنا : أن جوهر النفس قادر على تحريك الجسم من غير واسطة المماسة . وسائر الأجسام قابلة للحركة ، ونسبة جوهر النفس إلى كل الأجسام على السوية ، فلما قدرت النفس على تحريك بعضها من غير المماسة وجب حصول قدرتها على تحريك البقية من غير المماسة ، ولما كان ذلك باطلاً بالبديهة علمنا : أن النفس لا تقوى على التحريك ، إلا بشرط أن تماس ما يماسه . وكل ما كان مماساً لشيء من الأجسام ، فهو متحيز . فوجب أن يكون النفس جوهراً متحيزاً . وهو المطلوب
فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : أن تأثير النفس في تحريك بدنها الخاص ، غير مشروط بالمماسة . وتأثيرها في تحريك الأجسام موقوف على حصول المماسة بين بدنها وبين تلك الأجسام ؟ فإنكم ما لم تبطلوا هذا الاحتمال ، لا يتم دليلكم .
والجواب : إنه لما كان قبول البدن لتصرفات النفس ، لا يتوقف على حصول المماسة بين النفس وبين البدن ، وجب أن يكون الحال كذلك في تحريك سائر الأجسام . لأن جميع الأجسام متساوية في قبول الحركة ونسبة النفس : إلى جميعها على السوية. لأن النفس إذا كانت مجردة عن الحجمية وعلائق الحجمية ، كانت نسبة ذاتها إلى الكل على السوية ومتى كانت ذات الفاعل بالنسبة إلى الكل على السوية وذات جميع القوابل بالنسبة إلى ذات الفاعل بالسوية ، وجب أن يكون التأثير بالنسبة إلى الكلى على السوية ، فإذا استغنى الفاعل في الفعل عن مماسة محل الفعل في حق البعض ، وجب أن يكون الحال كذلك في حق الكل وإن أفتقر إلى المماسة في حق البعض ، وجب أن يفتقر إليها في حق الكل .
وأقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : النفس كالعاشقة لهذا البدن ، إلا أن هذا العشق الشديد يقتضي أن يكون تعلقها بهذا البدن أكثر ، وأن يكون تصرفها في هذا البدن أكثر . فأما أن يتغير مقتضى ذاتها بالنسبة إلى هذه الأجسام . فذاك محال . وهذا برهان يفيد اليقين في هذا المطلوب
The eighth proof for those who say that the soul is a body: They may say: If the soul were a substance abstracted from volume and occupation of space, then it would be impossible for its action to depend on contact with the locus of the act. Because what is not occupying space cannot become in contact with what occupies space. If that is the case, then its action would be by way of invention without need for contact and meeting between the agent and the locus of the act. And if that were the case, then it would be necessary that each one of us be able to move bodies without touching them, and without touching something that touches them. This is because the substance of the soul, since it is able to move the body without there being contact between it and that body, we know that the substance of the soul is able to move the body without the intermediary of contact. And other bodies are receptive to motion, and the relation of the substance of the soul to all bodies is equal. So since the soul is able to move some of them without contact, it would be necessary that it has the ability to move the rest without contact. And since that is false by intuition, we know that the soul is not able to move except on condition that it touches what touches it. And everything that touches something of bodies is occupying space. So it is necessary that the soul be a substance occupying space. That is what was sought.
If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that the soul's effect in moving its own body is not conditioned by contact, while its effect in moving other bodies depends on the occurrence of contact between its body and those bodies? For if you do not invalidate this possibility, your proof is not complete.
The answer: Since the body's acceptance of the soul's actions does not depend on the occurrence of contact between the soul and the body, it is necessary that the situation be the same in moving other bodies. Because all bodies are equal in accepting motion, and the relation of the soul to all of them is equal. Because if the soul is abstracted from volume and the attachments of volume, then its essence's relation to the whole is equal. And when the essence of the agent is equal in relation to the whole and the essences of all recipients are equal in relation to the essence of the agent, then the effect must be equal in relation to the whole. So if the agent is independent of contact with the locus of the act in the case of some, then it must be the same for the whole. And if it needs contact in the case of some, then it must need it for the whole.
The utmost that can be said in this matter is that the soul is like a lover of this body, but this intense love necessitates that its attachment to this body is greater, and its action in this body is more. But that the requirement of its essence should change with respect to these bodies—that is impossible. And this is a proof that yields certainty in this sought matter.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof for the materiality of the soul. It is a structured argument: (1) Assume the soul is non-bodily. (2) Then its action would not require physical contact. (3) Then it could move any body without contact. (4) But we know by intuition we cannot move bodies without contact. (5) Therefore the soul must be bodily. Then an objection is raised: perhaps the soul only needs contact for bodies other than its own. The answer refutes this by arguing that all bodies are equal in receptivity and the soul's relation to all is equal, so if it can move one without contact, it can move all. The answer also mentions that the soul's special attachment to its own body is due to a kind of 'love' but does not change the essence's capability. Hence the proof is conclusive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul might be able to move its own body without contact but require contact to move other bodies.
Correction: The soul's relation to all bodies is equal, so if it can move one without contact, it can move all. The special attachment to its own body is due to affinity, not a change in its essential capability.

Checkpoint
In the eighth proof, why must the soul be a body?

B) Because it cannot move bodies without contact, which requires being bodily.
C) Because it is described as a simple substance.
D) Because philosophers define it as such.

Key Takeaway
The soul must be a body because if it were non-bodily, it would be able to move any body without contact, which we know by intuition is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul moves your body every moment. Do you ever pause to consider that this movement requires an intimate connection—a kind of 'contact'—with your physical being? How does that change your awareness of every action you take?
?
If your soul is a body, then your spiritual state is directly linked to your physical habits. What one physical habit, when you change it, would most transform your inner life starting today?
?
The soul's attachment to its own body is compared to love. Reflect: Is your relationship with your own body driven by love that pulls you toward good, or by neglect that keeps you from the 'contact' that true action requires?
