The First Premise: Existence of Indivisible Entities
The author presents the first premise of his proof for the soul's immateriality, with four arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise of al-Matalib al-‘Aliyah’s proof for the soul’s immateriality and the four arguments that establish the existence of indivisible entities.

Core Idea
The existence of indivisible entities is proven by four rational arguments.

In this passage, the author lays out the foundation of his argument for the soul’s immateriality. Before proving that the soul is indivisible, he first establishes that there exist indivisible entities in the world. He offers four distinct proofs—from the Necessary Existent, from the geometric point, from the concept of unity, and from the nature of essences—to demonstrate that division is not universal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the overall proof structure
Notice that the author begins by stating the conclusion: indivisible existents imply indivisible knowledge, which implies an indivisible knower (the soul). He then announces that this depends on four premises, but only details the first.
The first premise is the focus of this lesson.
3
Examine the four arguments individually
Go through each of the four proofs (Necessary Existent, point, unity, essences) and understand how each shows the existence of something indivisible.
Each argument is independent; they provide cumulative evidence.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why the author needs to establish indivisible entities before discussing the soul. The commentary will help connect this to the overall proof.
This premise is crucial for the later steps in the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في حكاية الحجة التي هي أنيس المجوء في اثبات تجرد النفس نقول : إنه حصل في الوجود موجودات غير قابلة للقسمة ، فوجب أن يكون العلم بها ، غير قابل للقسمة ، فوجب أن يكون الموصوف بذلك العلم : غير قابل للقسمة . وكل متحيز ، وكل حال في المتحيز : منقسم . فوجب أن يكون الموصوف بتلك العلوم : غير متحيز ولا حال في المتحيز . واعلم : أن هذا الدليل مبني على أربع مقدمات : المقدمة الأولى : في بيان أنه حصل في الوجود موجودات ، لا تقبل القسمة . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن واجب الوجود لذاته، غير قابل للقسمة بوجه من الوجوه ، لا القسمة المقدارية ، ولا القسمة العقلية ، الحاصلة من الجنس والفصل ، ولا القسمة [ العقلية (۱) ] الحاصلة من المادة والصورة ، لأن كل منقسم فهو يحتاج إلى جزئه ، [ وجزؤه غيره . فكل منقسم فهو محتاج إلى غيره (۲) ] وكل محتاج إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته [ فكل منقسم فهو ممكن لذاته (۳) ] فيلزم أن كل ما يكون ممكناً لذاته ، فإنه يكون منزهاً عن التأليف والتركيب ، فيكون فرداً محضاً الثاني : إن النقطة يمتنع كونها منقسمة . وقد ذكرنا في المسألة المشتملة على إثبات الجوهر الفرد : وجوهاً كثيرة في تقرير هذا المطلوب . ومن جملتها : أن النقطة نهاية للخط ، فلو كانت النقطة منقسمة ، لم يكن كل واحد من نصفها نهاية للخط . وهذا يلزمه أن كل ما كان نهاية للخط ، يمتنع أن يكون منقسماً . الثالث : إن الوحدة أبعد الأشياء عن طبائع الكثرة . فالوحدة إن كانت موجودة فهو المطلوب ، وإن لم تكن موجودة كانت الكثرة موجودة [ لكن الكثرة عبارة عن مجموع الوحدات فمتى كانت الكثرة موجودة كانت الوحدة موجودة (۱) ] فثبت : أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود الوحدة على كل حال . الرابع : لا شك أن في الوجود ماهيات موجودة ، فهذه الماهيات إما أن تكون بسيطة أو مركبة . فإن كانت بسيطة مفردة فهو المطلوب وإن كانت مركبة وكل مركب فإنما يتركب عن البسائط . فعلى جميع التقديرات لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود ماهية بسيطة مفردة عن جميع جهات التركيب . فثبت بهذه الوجوه الأربعة : أنه قد حصل في الوجود موجودات لا تقبل القسمة .
Chapter Three: On the narrative of the proof that is the familiar [argument] in establishing the immateriality of the soul. We say: There have come into existence existents that are not divisible, so it necessarily follows that knowledge of them is not divisible, so it necessarily follows that the one described by that knowledge is not divisible. And every locus (mutaḥayyiz) and every state in a locus is divisible. So it necessarily follows that the one described by those sciences is neither a locus nor a state in a locus. Know that this proof is based on four premises: The first premise: An explanation that there have come into existence existents that do not accept division. And it is indicated by several proofs: The first: The Necessary Existence (wājib al-wujūd) by itself is not divisible in any way—neither quantitative division, nor conceptual division arising from genus and species, nor conceptual division arising from matter and form—because everything divided needs its part, and its part is other than it. So everything divided is in need of something other than itself, and everything in need of something other than itself is possible by itself. So everything divided is possible by itself. It follows that everything that is possible by itself is free from composition and combination, thus it is pure individual. The second: The point (nuqṭa) is impossible to be divided. We have already mentioned in the question concerning the establishment of the indivisible substance (al-jawhar al-fard) many proofs for this claim. Among them: The point is the endpoint of a line, so if the point were divided, each of its halves would not be the endpoint of the line. This entails that everything that is an endpoint of a line is impossible to be divided. The third: Unity (al-waḥda) is the furthest thing from the natures of multiplicity. So if unity exists, that is what is sought; and if it does not exist, then multiplicity exists. But multiplicity is a collection of unities, so whenever multiplicity exists, unity exists. It is thus established that it is necessary to acknowledge the existence of unity in any case. The fourth: There is no doubt that in existence there are existing essences (māhiyāt). These essences are either simple (basīṭa) or composite (murakkaba). If they are simple and individual, that is what is sought; and if they are composite, every composite is composed from simples. So in all cases it is necessary to acknowledge the existence of a simple essence free from all aspects of composition. So it is established by these four proofs that there have come into existence existents that do not accept division.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the overall proof for the soul's immateriality and then details the first of its four premises. The author begins by stating the conclusion: there are indivisible existents, so knowledge of them is indivisible, so the knower is indivisible and neither a locus nor a state in a locus. He then announces that this proof rests on four premises and proceeds to the first premise, which aims to show that indivisible existents do exist. Four arguments are given:
1) The Necessary Existent (God) is indivisible in every sense—quantitatively, conceptually (by genus and species), and by matter-form composition—because division implies need for parts, and need implies contingency, but the Necessary Existent is not contingent. Hence it is a pure individual.
2) The geometric point cannot be divided because if it were, its halves would not be endpoints of a line; thus endpoints are indivisible.
3) Unity (oneness) is an indivisible concept; it either exists or not, but if it does not, multiplicity (which depends on unity) could not exist, so unity must exist.
4) Essences in reality are either simple or composite; composites reduce to simples, so simple (indivisible) essences must exist.
These four proofs together establish the existence of indivisible entities, which is the first premise.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These proofs claim that only God is indivisible, so the soul cannot be like God.
Correction: The proofs show multiple types of indivisible existents (God, points, unity, simple essences). The soul is argued to be similarly indivisible, but not divine.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four proofs for the existence of indivisible entities?
A) The Necessary Existent is indivisible because division implies composition and need.
B) The point is indivisible because its halves would not be endpoints.
C) Unity must exist because multiplicity depends on it.
D) The soul is known to be indivisible through introspection.

Key Takeaway
The first premise asserts the existence of indivisible entities, proven by four rational arguments from theology, geometry, metaphysics, and ontology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Necessary Existent is indivisible and self-sufficient, what does that imply about your constant need for God? Do you live each moment aware that your very existence is contingent and dependent on Him, or do you act as if you are independent? What would it take for you to truly feel that need in your heart today?
?
The point is indivisible and serves as an endpoint. Your life has an endpoint – death. Are you living with the reality that your soul is not divisible by matter, and that your ultimate end is a return to the Indivisible? How should that change your priorities right now?
?
Unity is the basis of multiplicity. Your daily life is filled with multiple distractions. Have you let the 'multiplicity' of your affairs (work, family, hobbies) fragment your focus on the One who is truly simple and unique? What is one step you can take this week to simplify your worship and intention?

The Second Premise: Knowledge of the Indivisible is Indivisible
The author argues that knowledge of an indivisible object must be indivisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that knowledge of an indivisible object must itself be indivisible.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a simple object cannot be divided.

This lesson presents the second premise of a larger argument. Having established that there exist indivisible entities (the first premise), the author now argues that our knowledge of such an entity cannot be composite. The proof uses a division of possibilities, showing that all possible ways to divide knowledge of an indivisible object are impossible. Let's follow the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: premise, proof by division, and the start of refuting the first possibility.
The passage cuts off, but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the three divisions
The author lists three possibilities if knowledge of an indivisible object were divisible. Write them down.
These are: knowledge of the whole, knowledge of a part, or knowledge of neither.
3
Understand why each division fails
The author states all three are false. Think about why: can a part of knowledge know the whole? Can it know a part when the object has no parts? If it knows neither, it's irrelevant.
The text begins explaining the first impossibility but is cut off.
4
Connect to the overall argument
See how this premise supports the broader discussion about divine attributes or other kalam topics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية : فهي في بيان أنه إذا كان المعلوم غير منقسم ، كان العلم المتعلق به غير قابل للقسمة . فنقول : الدليل عليه : هو أن ذلك العلم ، لو انقسم، لكان كل واحد من جزئياته ، إما أن يكون علماً بذلك المعلوم أو يكون علماً بجزء ذلك المعلوم ، أو لا يكون علماً بذلك المعلوم ولا بشيء من أجزائه ، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة ، فبطل القول بكون ذلك العلم قابلاً للقسمة . يمتنع أن يكون جزء ذلك العلم : علماً بذلك المعلوم . فبيانه من وجهين :
As for the second premise: it is an explanation that if the known (al-maʿlūm) is indivisible, then the knowledge (al-ʿilm) related to it is not divisible. We say: The proof for it is that that knowledge, if it were divided, each one of its parts would either be knowledge of that known, or knowledge of a part of that known, or not knowledge of that known nor of any of its parts. The three divisions are false, so the claim that that knowledge is divisible is invalidated. It is impossible that a part of that knowledge be knowledge of that known. Its explanation is from two aspects:
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the second premise of the overall argument. Having established that there are indivisible existents (first premise), the author now argues that knowledge of such an indivisible object must itself be indivisible. The proof proceeds by division (taqsīm): if knowledge were divisible, each part would have three possibilities: (1) it is knowledge of the entire known object, (2) it is knowledge of a part of the known object, or (3) it is knowledge of neither the whole nor any part. All three are impossible: (1) because the part of knowledge would then equal the whole knowledge of the object, which is absurd; (2) because the known object has no parts (being indivisible), so knowledge of a part is meaningless; (3) because if the part knows nothing of the object, it is not knowledge of that object at all. Since these are the only options and all are false, knowledge must be indivisible. The text begins to elaborate why the first option is impossible but cuts off.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that knowledge, like a physical object, can be divided into parts that each contain a piece of information about the object.
Correction: The author is using logical analysis, not physical division. He shows that the concept of divisible knowledge is incoherent when the known object is simple and indivisible.

Checkpoint
According to the second premise, how many possible divisions does the author consider for knowledge of an indivisible object?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The proof by division shows that knowledge of an indivisible object must be indivisible, because any division leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Have you ever tried to 'compartmentalize' your faith, accepting some Islamic teachings while doubting others? What does the indivisibility of knowledge of a simple being teach you about the integrity your īmān must have?
?
This proof shows that knowledge of an indivisible object cannot be divided. As you read the Qur'anic descriptions of Allah, do you treat your understanding of Him as a single, coherent whole, or do you sometimes pick and choose? What step can you take today to integrate your knowledge of your Lord so that it becomes indivisible?

Proof: Part Equals Whole – Objection and Answer
Sanusi presents the first proof, objection, and answer regarding knowledge of an indivisible object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that knowledge of an indivisible object cannot be composite, including the objection and its answer.

Core Idea
The essence of knowledge is solely being knowledge of something; sharing that essence makes part equal to whole, which is impossible.

Sanusi begins the first proof by assuming the opposite: that knowledge of an indivisible object is composite. He shows this leads to an impossible conclusion—the part being equal to the whole. An objection tries to salvage the assumption, but Sanusi clarifies that the essence of knowledge is singular, so part and whole cannot differ in any relevant aspect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get familiar with the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the structure: proof, objection, answer.
2
Identify the three parts
Break down the passage into the initial statement and reasoning (proof), the objection, and the answer.
Notice how Sanusi uses conditional reasoning (if... then...).
3
Understand the key insight
Focus on Sanusi's claim that knowledge has no essence other than being knowledge of the object. This is why the objection fails.
This essence is shared entirely by both the whole and any part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنه يلزم أن يكون الجزء مساوياً للكل ، وذلك محال . لأنه إذا كان كل ذلك العلم علماً بذلك المعلوم وأحد أجزاء ذلك العلم يكون أيضاً علماً بذلك المعلوم ، فحينئذ يلزم كون الجزء مساوياً للكل من كل الوجوه ، وهو محال . فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون الكل مساوياً للجزء في بعض الأحوال ، وإن كان مخالفاً له من سائر الوجوه ؟ قلنا : العلم بالشيء لا حقيقة له ولا ماهية له ، سوى كونه علماً بذلك المعلوم، وكل ما سوى هذا الاعتبار ، فإنه يكون خارجاً عن ماهية كونه علماً ، فإذا كان لا ماهية للعلم إلا هذا القدر. ثم قلنا : إن هذا القدر وقع الاشتراك فيه بين كل العلم ، وبين جزئه، فحينئذ يلزم كون الجزء مساوياً للكل من كل الوجوه ، وهو محال
First: It follows that the part would be equal to the whole, and that is impossible. Because if all of that knowledge is knowledge of that known thing, and one part of that knowledge is also knowledge of that known thing, then it follows that the part is equal to the whole from every aspect, and that is impossible. If they say: Why is it not permissible that the whole be equal to the part in some states, even though it differs from it in other respects? We say: Knowledge of a thing has no reality and no essence except that it is knowledge of that known thing, and everything apart from this consideration is outside the essence of being knowledge. Since knowledge has no essence except this measure, then we say: This measure is shared between the whole knowledge and its part, so then it follows that the part is equal to the whole from every aspect, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the argument that knowledge of an indivisible object must itself be indivisible. The proof begins by assuming the contrary: that such knowledge is composite (has parts). If each part is also knowledge of the same indivisible object, then each part is exactly like the whole in being knowledge of that object. The objection raises the possibility that the whole might be equal to the part in some respects but not all, i.e., they share the aspect of being knowledge but differ in other attributes. The answer clarifies that the essence of knowledge is solely the relation of being knowledge of something; no other attributes are part of its essence. Since the whole and the part share this exact essence, they are equal in every respect relevant to knowledge, which is impossible for a part and whole. Thus the assumption of compositeness is false.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that knowledge could have additional attributes beyond being knowledge of an object, so a part and whole could differ in those attributes.
Correction: Sanusi insists the essence of knowledge is solely the relation to the known; any other attributes are external to that essence, so they do not affect the equality in essence.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of an indivisible object cannot be composite because its essence is solely the relation to the object, making any part identical to the whole in that respect, which is impossible.

Second Proof: Redundant Knowledge Would Occur
Sanusi gives the second proof: each part being knowledge results in redundant knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's second proof that knowledge of an indivisible object cannot be composite, because it would lead to redundant instances of the same knowledge.

Core Idea
If each part were full knowledge, the knower would know the same thing multiple times — which is absurd.

This proof is a simple but powerful argument: if knowledge of a single object were made of parts, and each part were itself knowledge of that object, then the person would have multiple identical cognitions simultaneously. That is impossible. Sanusi uses this to show that knowledge of an indivisible object must be simple.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the concise logical structure.
The bracketed portion is reconstructed from footnotes but essential for the proof.
2
Identify the logical flow
The proof is a conditional: if all parts are knowledge of the same known, then the knower knows it many times. The conclusion is that this is false, so the original assumption (that knowledge is composite) is false.
Focus on the reductio ad absurdum structure.
3
Understand why redundancy is absurd
Having multiple identical cognitions of the same object at the same time serves no purpose and is not observed in reality. It violates the principle of parsimony.
4
Reflect on the implication
This proof supports the doctrine that divine knowledge (and all simple knowledge) cannot be composite, maintaining the simplicity of Allah's attributes.
This connects to broader themes in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : وهو أنه إذا كان كل ذلك العلم علماً بذلك المعلوم [ وكان كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك العلم أيضاً، علماً بذلك المعلوم ، فحينئذ يكون الموصوف بذلك عالماً بذلك المعلوم لا مرة واحدة ، بل مراراً كثيرة . وذلك باطل ]
Second: It is that if all of that knowledge is knowledge of that known thing, [and each one of the parts of that knowledge is also knowledge of that known thing, then the one described by that knowledge would be knowing that known thing not once, but many times. And that is false.]
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This is a concise second proof: if knowledge of an indivisible object were composite and each part were itself complete knowledge of that object, then the person possessing that knowledge would have multiple identical cognitions simultaneously. This is impossible because a single act of knowledge is sufficient; redundancy serves no purpose and is not observed. The conclusion is that knowledge of an indivisible object cannot be composite.

Key Terms

مراراً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge cannot be composite because it would lead to infinite regress.
B) Knowledge cannot be composite because it would result in redundant instances of the same knowledge.
C) Knowledge cannot be composite because the part would be equal to the whole.
D) Knowledge cannot be composite because the known is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of an indivisible object must be simple; otherwise, redundancy occurs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Proof: Indivisible Part or Infinite Regress
Sanusi presents a dilemma proof for the existence of indivisible knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that knowledge of an indivisible object must contain an indivisible unit, using a logical dilemma that forces the conclusion.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the indivisible must itself be indivisible — or else an impossible regress.

Sanusi now presents a third proof using a powerful dilemma: either the parts of knowledge terminate in an indivisible part, or they go on infinitely. Both paths lead to the same conclusion — that indivisible units of knowledge must exist. This passage shows how a logical dilemma forces acceptance of the desired conclusion, even when one tries to avoid it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the dilemma emerge.
Notice the conditional: 'if we assume each part is complete knowledge...'
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
Find the two alternatives: either the parts end at an indivisible part (first branch) or they do not (second branch, infinite regress). Understand the conclusion for each branch.
The first branch directly yields indivisible knowledge. The second leads to an impossible infinite regress.
3
Understand the concession argument
Even if someone were to concede infinite parts, the author argues that multiplicity itself requires units. So the existence of an indivisible unit is unavoidable.
This is a subtle but powerful point: any 'many' presupposes a 'one'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : وهو أنـا إذا فرضنا أن كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك العلم ، كان علماً بذلك المعلوم ، فهل تنتهي تلك الأجزاء إلى جزء لا يقبل القسمة ، أو لا ينتهي إلى ذلك ؟ فإن كان الأول فذلك الجزء من العلم ، علم بذلك المعلوم ، مع أنه لا يقبل القسمة . فقد وجدنا علماً لا يقبل القسمة . وهو المطلوب . وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ يلزم أن يحصل لذلك العلم أجزاء لا نهاية لها ، لا مرة واحدة بل مراراً لا نهاية لها. وهو محال . وعلى تقدير تسليمه، فالمقصود حاصل. لأن الكثرة عبارة عن اجتماع الوحدات ، فما لم تحصل الوحدة لم يحصل اجتماعها ، وحينئذ يحصل المقصود .
Third: If we assume that each one of the parts of that knowledge is knowledge of that known thing, then do those parts terminate at a part that does not accept division, or do they not terminate at that? If the first, then that part of knowledge is knowledge of that known thing, while it does not accept division. So we have found knowledge that does not accept division. That is the desired. And if the second, then it follows that that knowledge has infinite parts, not once but infinitely many times. That is impossible. And on the assumption of its concession, the purpose is attained. Because multiplicity is the aggregation of units, and unless the unit is obtained, its aggregation is not obtained, and then the purpose is attained.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This third proof presents a dilemma. Assuming each part of the supposed composite knowledge is itself complete knowledge of the indivisible object, either the parts eventually reach an indivisible part or they go on infinitely. If they reach an indivisible part, that part is knowledge but is indivisible—proving the desired conclusion that there can be indivisible knowledge. If they go on infinitely, that is impossible (infinite regress). Even if one were to concede infinite parts, the purpose is still attained because multiplicity presupposes units; the existence of units (indivisible parts) is necessary for any multiplicity. Hence, an indivisible unit of knowledge must exist.

Key Terms
جزء لا يقبل القسمة
لا نهاية لها
كثرة
وحدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress might be a valid possibility for the composition of knowledge.
Correction: The author argues infinite regress is impossible, and even if conceded, the concept of multiplicity necessitates units, so indivisible units must exist.

Checkpoint

A) They must all be divisible
B) They must end at an indivisible part, or if infinite, still require an indivisible unit
C) They can be infinite without any problem
D) They are all equal to the whole

Key Takeaway
The third proof establishes that knowledge of an indivisible object must contain an indivisible unit, either directly or through the impossibility of an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument assumes every part of knowledge is whole. Is there any area of your life where you compartmentalize your faith, acting as if part of you is ignorant of Allah? Which compartment will you surrender to Him this week?

Refuting the Second and Third Possibilities about Compound Knowledge
Sanusi refutes the second and third divisions about compound knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi rejects the idea that knowledge of an indivisible known can be composed of parts that either know parts of the known or are not knowledge individually.

Core Idea
Knowledge of an indivisible reality cannot be composed of parts.

Sanusi has already presented the first division (that the whole composite knows the whole known). Now he turns to the remaining two possibilities: that each part knows a part, or that the parts individually are not knowledge but their combination produces knowledge. Through careful logical analysis, he shows both possibilities are impossible when the known is indivisible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second division
Read the refutation of the second division. Notice why Sanusi says it is false: it requires the known to be divisible, but we are discussing an indivisible known.
This is a straightforward dismissal based on the nature of the known.
2
Examine the third division premise
Consider the third division: parts individually are not knowledge of the known. The question is: does their combination produce something new?
This is the key point of the argument.
3
Follow the two-branch analysis
Trace the two branches: if no new thing arises, the aggregate is just a collection of non-knowledge items—contradiction. If a new thing arises, it must be either divisible or indivisible. Divisible leads back to first division; indivisible is the desired result.
Use the guided breakdown to visualize the logic.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
Learn the universal conclusion: whenever the known is indivisible, knowledge of it must be indivisible. This establishes the existence of simple, indivisible knowledge.
This conclusion is crucial for subsequent arguments about God's knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني من الأقسام المذكورة في أول الدليل : وهو أن كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك العلم يكون علماً بجزء من أجزاء ذلك المعلوم. فهذا أيضاً : باطل . لأن هذا إنما يعقل : إذا كان المعلوم منقسماً . وكلامنا في المعلوم الذي لا يقبل القسمة وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك العلم ، لا يكون [ علماً بنفس ] ذلك المعلوم ولا شيء من أجـزائـه [ علماً بذلك المعلوم ] فنقول : إن تلك الأجزاء إذا اجتمعت . فهل حدث عن اجتماعها أمر زائد على تلك الأجزاء ، أو لم يحدث ؟ فإن لم يحدث أمر زائد ، كان الحاصل لم هناك أموراً ليست [ علماً بذلك المعلوم ولا بشيء من أجزائه . فيلزم أن يقال : العلم بالشيء : ليس ] علماً به . هذا خلف ، وإن حدث أمر زائد . فذلك الزائد إن قبل القسمة ، عاد التقسيم الأول فيه . وإن لم يقبل القسمة فهو علم لا يقبل القسمة . وذلك هو المطلوب [ فثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه متى كان المعلوم غير منقسم ، وجب أن يكون العلم به غير منقسم . وهو المطلوب ]
As for the second division of the divisions mentioned at the beginning of the proof: that each one of the parts of that knowledge is knowledge of a part of the parts of that known thing. This is also false. Because this is only conceivable if the known is divisible. But our discussion is about the known that does not accept division. As for the third division: that it is said: each one of the parts of that knowledge is not [knowledge of] that known thing nor any of its parts [knowledge of that known thing]. Then we say: when those parts are combined, does there arise from their combination something additional over those parts, or does it not arise? If nothing additional arises, then what results are things that are not [knowledge of that known thing nor of any of its parts. So it follows that it is said: knowledge of a thing is not] knowledge of it. This is a contradiction. And if something additional arises, then that additional thing, if it accepts division, the first division applies to it; and if it does not accept division, then it is knowledge that does not accept division. And that is the desired. [So it is established by what we have mentioned: whenever the known is indivisible, knowledge of it must be indivisible. And that is the desired.]
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage addresses the remaining two possibilities from the initial enumeration. The second possibility was that each part of the composite knowledge knows a part of the known. This is rejected because it presupposes that the known object is divisible, but we are considering an indivisible known. The third possibility is that the parts individually are not knowledge at all, but their combination produces knowledge. The author then analyzes this: either combination produces something new or not. If nothing new arises, then the aggregate is just a collection of non-knowledge items, so there is no knowledge—contradicting the assumption that the composite is knowledge. If something new arises, that new thing is either divisible (leading back to the earlier argument) or indivisible—thus proving the desired conclusion that indivisible knowledge exists. The passage concludes that when the known is indivisible, knowledge must also be indivisible.

Key Terms
قسم
معلوم منقسم
اجتماع
أمر زائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge can emerge by simply combining parts that individually lack knowledge of the object.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if no new reality emerges, the aggregate is just a heap of non-knowledge; if something new emerges, it must either be divisible (leading to regress) or indivisible (proving the point). True knowledge of an indivisible object cannot be composed.

Checkpoint

A) It leads to infinite regress
B) It assumes the known is divisible, but we are discussing an indivisible known
C) It makes the part equal to the whole
D) It violates the essence of knowledge

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of an indivisible known must itself be indivisible, because any composite structure leads to contradiction or regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Premise: Subject of Knowledge Must Be Indivisible
Sanusi proves if knowledge is indivisible, its subject must be indivisible via three-case proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that if knowledge is indivisible, the subject possessing it must also be indivisible, and why the contrary leads to contradictions.

Core Idea
Indivisible knowledge requires an indivisible subject.

This lesson is the third premise in Sanusi's broader argument. He demonstrates a crucial logical connection: the nature of knowledge determines the nature of its bearer. By assuming the opposite and showing its absurdity, he establishes that an indivisible accident can only reside in an indivisible locus. The proof is a clear case-by-case refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the three possibilities presented.
2
Identify the three possibilities
When the subject is assumed divisible, Sanusi lists three cases for each part: possessing all knowledge, a part, or none. Find these in the text.
3
Understand why each possibility fails
The first leads to one accident in multiple loci; the second implies knowledge is divisible; the third negates the subject's knowledge. Work through each refutation.
The commentary elaborates on these.
4
Conclude the necessity of indivisibility
Since all three possibilities are impossible, the subject cannot be divisible. Therefore, indivisible knowledge necessitates an indivisible subject.
This conclusion is the premise itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة في بيان أنه متى كان العلم غير منقسم كان الموصوف بذلك العلم غير منقسم : فنقول : الذي يدل عليه : أن ذلك الموصوف ، إذا كان منقسما ، افترض فيه أجزاء وأقسام . وكل واحد من تلك الأجسام ، إما أن يكون موصوفا بكل ذلك العلم ، أو بجزء من أجزائه ، أو لا بكله ولا ببعضه والأول : محال ، وإلا لزم حلول العرض الواحد في المحال الكثيرة . والثاني : محال ، وإلا لزم أن يكون ذلك العلم منقسماً . وقد فرضناه غير منقسم ، خلف . والثالث : محال ، لأنه إذا كان كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المحل : خالياً عن ذلك العلم [ وعن جميع أجزائه يلزم كون ذلك المحل خالياً عن كل ذلك العلم ] مع أنا فرضناه موصوفاً به . هذا خلف
The third premise: explaining that whenever knowledge is indivisible, the subject described by that knowledge is indivisible. We say: What indicates it is that that subject, if it is divisible, parts and sections are assumed in it. Each one of those parts is either described by all of that knowledge, or by a part of its parts, or not by all of it nor by any part of it. The first is impossible, otherwise it would entail the inherence of one accident in multiple loci. The second is impossible, otherwise it would entail that knowledge is divisible, but we have assumed it indivisible—a contradiction. The third is impossible, because if each one of the parts of that place is devoid of that knowledge [and of all its parts, it follows that that place is devoid of all that knowledge], despite the fact that we assumed it described by it. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is the third premise of the larger argument. It aims to show that if knowledge is indivisible, then the subject that possesses that knowledge must also be indivisible. The proof considers the contrary: suppose the subject is divisible into parts. Then each part either (1) possesses the entire knowledge, (2) possesses a part of it, or (3) possesses none of it. Option (1) is impossible because it would mean one accident (the knowledge) inheres in multiple independent loci, which violates the principle that an accident cannot be in two substrates simultaneously without division. Option (2) would require the knowledge itself to be divisible, contradicting the assumption that it is indivisible. Option (3) would mean no part has any knowledge, so the whole subject cannot have knowledge either—contradicting the assumption that it does. All three possibilities are absurd, so the subject cannot be divisible; it must be indivisible.

Key Terms
موصوف
محل
عرض
حلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think a divisible subject could hold indivisible knowledge by each part containing the whole knowledge.
Correction: This is impossible because it would require a single accident to be present in multiple independent substrates, which violates the principle that an accident cannot inhere in more than one locus at once.

Checkpoint
Why is the first possibility (each part of a divisible subject has the whole knowledge) rejected?
A) It requires the knowledge to be divisible
B) It would mean one accident inheres in multiple loci, which is impossible
C) It leads to infinite regress
D) It makes the part equal to the whole

Key Takeaway
An indivisible accident cannot inhere in a divisible substrate; the subject must share the indivisibility of the knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to have knowledge of Tawhid, the oneness of Allah — but do you live as if that knowledge is divided, applying it in some areas of life and ignoring it in others? The proof says the subject must be indivisible if the knowledge is indivisible. Is your heart (the subject) whole in its devotion, or fragmented?
?
The proof shows that assuming otherwise leads to contradiction. In your own life, have you ever held a belief about Allah that contradicted another belief you also held? That is a similar contradiction. Are you willing to examine your beliefs for consistency, even if it means letting go of a comfortable but contradictory idea?

The Second Objection: Parts Devoid of Attribute
Objection about parts lacking the attribute and its answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the argument about attributes inhering in parts and the author's response based on self-evident knowledge.

Core Idea
If every part lacks the attribute, the whole cannot have it—this is self-evident.

This lesson continues the discussion of objections. The objector questions why each part cannot be devoid of knowledge while the whole still has it. The author's answer appeals to self-evident rational insight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dialogue structure: objection, reply, counter-objection, final answer.
2
Identify the objector's question
The objector asks: why can each part not be devoid of the attribute entirely or partially?
This challenges the link between parts and whole.
3
Understand the opponent's initial reply
The opponent (the one asserting the proof) says if so, the whole cannot be described by that attribute. The objector challenges the proof for this.
The objector argues the whole is different from its parts.
4
Examine the author's final answer
The author claims self-evident knowledge: if all parts lack the attribute, the whole necessarily lacks it. This is presented as a basic rational insight.
Self-evident knowledge (al-ilm al-badihi) is used as a foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : هب أن ذلك الجسم ، حصلت له أبعاض وأقسام . فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : كل واحد من أبعاضه خالي عن كل ذلك العلم وعن بعضه ؟ قوله : و لو كان كذلك ، لامتنع أن يكون كل ذلك الجسم موصوفاً بذلك العلم ، قلنا : ما الدليل على أنه لما خلا كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك الموصوف عن كل تلك الصفة وعن بعضها ، وجب خلو ذلك الموصوف عن كل تلك الصفة ؟ وذلك لأن الكل من حيث هو كل ، مغاير لكل واحد من أجزائه خلا عن كل تلك الصفة وعن بعضها ، فلا يلزم من هذا ، وجوب خلو [ كل ] تلك الذات عن كل تلك الصفة ؟ وأما السؤال الثاني . فجوابه : إنا ندعي العلم البديهي بأنه متى كان جميع أجزاء الذات خالياً عن كل الصفة، وعن كل واحد من أجزاء تلك الصفة ، امتنع بعد كون تلك الذات موصوفة بتلك الصفة .
The second question: Suppose that that body has acquired parts and divisions. Why is it not permissible to say: Each of its parts is devoid of all of that knowledge and of part of it? His statement: 'And if it were so, it would be impossible for the entire body to be described by that knowledge.' We say: What is the proof that when each of the parts of that described thing is devoid of all of that attribute and of part of it, the described thing is necessarily devoid of all of that attribute? That is because the whole, insofar as it is a whole, is different from each of its parts that are devoid of all of that attribute and of part of it, so it does not follow from this that [every] essence is necessarily devoid of all of that attribute? As for the second question, its answer is: We claim self-evident knowledge that whenever all the parts of the essence are devoid of the whole attribute and of each one of the parts of that attribute, it is impossible for that essence to be described by that attribute.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objector grants the body has parts and asks why it cannot be that each part is entirely devoid of knowledge or any part of it. The opponent (the one asserting the proof) replies that if so, the whole body could not be described by that knowledge. The objector then challenges the proof for this, arguing that the whole is different from its parts, so the whole could still have the attribute even if parts lack it. The author's answer appeals to self-evident knowledge (al-ilm al-badihi): it is intuitively obvious that if every part lacks the attribute entirely, the whole cannot possess it. This refutes the objection by grounding the inference in immediate rational insight.

Key Terms
أبعاض
أقسام
العلم البديهي
الذات
الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector believes that the whole can possess an attribute even if all its parts are devoid of it, because the whole is different from its parts.
Correction: Self-evident knowledge shows that if every part lacks the attribute, the whole necessarily lacks it; the whole's relationship to its parts is such that the attribute cannot 'appear' from nothing.

Checkpoint

A) Logical deduction
B) Self-evident knowledge
C) Scriptural proof
D) Empirical observation

Key Takeaway
The absence of an attribute from all parts of a whole necessarily implies the absence of that attribute from the whole itself, as known by self-evident knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author argues that self-evident knowledge tells us a whole cannot have what all its parts lack. Consider your relationship with the Quran: if each verse you recite is devoid of reflection, can your recitation as a whole be considered worship? Which verse will you ponder deeply tonight?
?
This passage reveals a fundamental principle: the whole is determined by its parts. Look at your last week—if each day lacked a sincere moment of gratitude to Allah, can your week be called a week of gratitude? What is one concrete action you will take in the next hour to break this pattern?

Second Proof: Unity Entails the Indivisible Atom
Sanusi presents a logical proof from the existence of unity to the existence of the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of unity (al-wahda) logically forces the acceptance of an indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), and how the objection that unity is a privation is refuted.

Core Idea
Unity proves the atom: whatever exists is either substance or accident, and indivisibility follows.

In this lesson, we encounter a dense but elegant proof from the mutakallimūn. The argument begins with the simple observation that unity exists in the world. From that premise, it constructs a dilemma that leaves no escape for the opponent: either unity is itself the indivisible atom, or it forces its locus to be indivisible. The passage also anticipates and refutes a key objection about the nature of unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow come through before diving into commentary.
Notice the compact style; each phrase carries heavy logical weight.
2
Map the dilemma
Identify the two main branches: unity as substance, or unity as accident. Under the accident branch, note the sub-dilemma about the locus being divisible or indivisible.
This dilemma covers all possibilities, leaving no loophole.
3
Understand the objection and refutation
The opponent claims unity is a privation. Follow how the author refutes this by analyzing multiplicity: if unity is privative, multiplicity becomes impossible; if multiplicity is privative, unity becomes existent.
This refutation uses pure logic without empirical assumptions.
4
Reflect on the overall logic
Ask yourself: how does this proof relate to the larger project of proving atoms? Why is it considered a strong argument in kalām?
This proof is one of several; it shows internal consistency in the atomist framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في بيان أنه لا يلزم من انقسام المحل انقسام الحال : هو أن الوحدة حاصلة . فهذه الوحدة إما أن تكون جوهراً أو عرضاً ، فإن كانت [ جوهراً ] فقد ثبت الجوهر [ الفرد ] وإن كانت عرضاً وجب أن يكون له محل . فمحلها إن كان منقسماً فقد جوزتم قيام غير المنقسم بالمنقسم ، وإن كان غير منقسم فهو الجوهر الفرد ، وعلى جميع التقديرات ، يبطل دليلكم . فإن قالوا : الوحدة طبيعة عدمية ، وهي عبارة عن عدم الكثرة . فنقول : [ هذا باطل ] لأن الكثرة إما أن تكون طبيعة وجودية ، أو عدمية . لا جائز أن تكون وجودية ، لأن الكثرة عبارة عن مجموع الوحدات . فإذا كانت الوحدة عدمية ، كانت الكثرة عبارة عن مجموع العـدمات ومجموع العدمات لا يكون موجوداً . وإن كانت الكثرة عدماً والوحدة عبارة عن عدم الكثرة ، فحينئذ تكون الوحدة عدماً للعدم ، فتكون أمراً موجوداً . فسقط ما قالوه
The second proof demonstrating that division of the locus does not necessitate division of the accident: that unity exists. This unity is either a substance or an accident. If it is [a substance], then the [indivisible] atom is established. If it is an accident, it must have a locus. If its locus is divisible, then you have permitted the subsistence of the non-divisible in the divisible; if it is indivisible, then it is the indivisible atom. On all accounts, your argument is invalid. If they say: 'Unity is a privative nature, an expression for the absence of multiplicity'—we say: [This is false] because multiplicity is either a positive nature or a privative nature. It cannot be positive, because multiplicity is an expression for the sum of unities. So if unity is privative, multiplicity would be a sum of privations, and a sum of privations cannot exist. And if multiplicity is a privation and unity is an expression for the absence of multiplicity, then unity would be a privation of privation, so it would be an existent thing. Thus what they said falls.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained proof. It begins with the premise that unity (al-wahda) exists. Then it presents a dilemma: unity is either substance or accident. If substance, the indivisible atom is proven. If accident, its locus must be either divisible or indivisible. If divisible, then a non-divisible accident (unity) would inhere in a divisible locus, which is exactly what the opponent denies, so their position contradicts itself. If indivisible, that is the atom itself. The opponent might object that unity is a privation (nonexistent). The proof refutes this by analyzing multiplicity: if unity were privative, multiplicity (the sum of unities) would be a sum of privations, which is impossible. If multiplicity is privation, then unity becomes a privation of privation, which is existent. Therefore, unity is real and the argument holds.

Key Terms
وحدة (unity)
جوهر فرد (indivisible atom)
عرض (accident)
عدم (privation)
كثرة (multiplicity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity is a privation (nothing real).
Correction: If unity were a privation, multiplicity as sum of unities would be impossible, or if multiplicity is privation, unity becomes a privation of privation and thus existent.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that unity cannot be a privation?
A) Because privation cannot exist.
B) Because it would lead to an infinite regress.
C) Because if unity is privation, multiplicity becomes a sum of privations which cannot exist.
D) Because unity is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The existence of unity forces the existence of an indivisible atom, and the objection that unity is privative is self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that unity must be a real, positive attribute, not just the absence of division. In your life, do you treat the oneness of Allah (tawheed) as a mere negation of other gods, or as a positive, living reality that demands your entire being? What would change if you let this truth permeate your daily thoughts and actions?
?
Sanusi uses rigorous logical reasoning to force a conclusion. When you face doubts about your faith, do you engage with them with the same intellectual honesty, or do you rely on emotional comfort? This week, challenge yourself to identify one belief you hold without evidence and examine it using the tools of reason—not to undermine faith, but to strengthen it.
?
The refutation of the privative view of unity teaches that mere denial or negation is not enough; affirmation is required. In your spiritual life, are you only trying to avoid sins (negation) without actively cultivating virtues (positive existence)? What is one virtue you can actively work on today, not just by avoiding its opposite, but by deliberately practicing it?

Refutation of the Privation of Unity
The author refutes the privation theory of unity via a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a two-pronged logical argument refutes the claim that unity is merely the privation of plurality.

Core Idea
Unity cannot be purely privative without leading to absurdity.

This lesson presents a sharp logical refutation of the claim that unity (al-wahda) is nothing but the absence of plurality. The author examines the consequences of this position on both possible interpretations of plurality, showing that each leads to a contradiction. By working through this argument, you will see how careful conceptual analysis is used to defend the reality of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the objection: unity is merely the absence of plurality. Then read the author's initial response that this is false.
Notice that the opponent treats unity as a privative nature, i.e., a lack of something.
2
Follow the two possible cases
The author considers two possibilities: either plurality is existential or it is privative. Trace the logical consequences for each case.
Case 1 leads to plurality being a sum of privations (impossible); Case 2 leads to unity being a double negation (hence existential).
3
Conclude the refutation
Observe that in both scenarios the opponent's position collapses. The author concludes that the objection is refuted.
The argument is a dilemma: whichever side you take, you reach an absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : الوحدة طبيعة عدمية ، وهي عبارة عن عدم الكثرة . فنقول : هذا باطل لأن الكثرة إما أن تكون طبيعة وجودية ، أو عدمية . لا جائز أن تكون وجودية ، لأن الكثرة عبارة عن مجموع الوحدات . فإذا كانت الوحدة عدمية ، كانت الكثرة عبارة عن مجموع العدمات ومجموع العدمات لا يكون موجوداً . وإن كانت الكثرة عدماً والوحدة عبارة عن عدم الكثرة ، فحينئذ تكون الوحدة عدماً للعدم ، فتكون أمراً موجوداً . فسقط ما قالوه
They said: 'Unity is a privative nature, and it is an expression of the absence of plurality.' We say: 'This is false, because plurality is either an existential nature or a privative one. It cannot be existential, because plurality is an expression of the sum of unities. So if unity is privative, then plurality would be an expression of the sum of privations, and the sum of privations cannot exist. And if plurality is non-existence and unity is an expression of the absence of plurality, then unity would be the non-existence of non-existence, and thus it would be an existent thing.' So what they said is refuted.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The opponent claims that unity (al-wahda) is not a real attribute but merely the privation of plurality. The author argues by analyzing the nature of plurality. If plurality is the sum of unities, and if unity is privative, then plurality would be a sum of privations, which cannot exist. Alternatively, if plurality itself is non-existence, then unity as the absence of plurality would be the non-existence of non-existence, which is a positive thing. Thus the claim that unity is purely privative leads to a contradiction.

Key Terms
وحدة
كثرة
عدمية
وجودية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity is merely the privation of plurality, and this is a coherent and sustainable position.
Correction: The author shows that this position leads to absurdities: either plurality becomes impossible to exist, or unity becomes a positive entity. Thus the privation theory is self-defeating.

Checkpoint
According to the author, if unity is privative and plurality is existential, what does plurality become?
A) A collection of existents
B) A collection of privations
C) Neither existent nor privative
D) A single privation

Key Takeaway
The claim that unity is privative leads to a logical dilemma, ultimately refuting itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is one,' do you merely mean there is no other god, or do you affirm a positive, real oneness that demands exclusive devotion? How does your understanding match the author's conclusion that unity must be existent, not just absence?

If unity were only a lack of plurality, then God's oneness would be dependent on the existence of other gods to be 'lacked'. Does your heart ever treat tawhid as a theoretical or negative belief rather than a positive reality that shapes your every action?
?
The author shows that a seemingly harmless philosophical claim leads to absurdity. Are there beliefs or attitudes in your life that you accept without examining their logical consequences—especially about Allah's attributes? What would it take for you to submit to rigorous truth like this argument demands?

Indivisibility of Relational Attributes
Third proof: indivisibility of relational attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why relational attributes cannot be divided and how this supports the existence of indivisible accidents.

Core Idea
Relational attributes cannot be divided because fractions of relations are rationally impossible.

Sanusi now presents a third proof that focuses on relational attributes like 'above' and 'below'. These attributes are accidents of bodies, and if they were divisible, we could speak of half of 'above-ness'—an absurdity. This lesson unpacks that argument and explains its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise style of the proof.
The proof is short but packed with logical structure.
2
Understand relational attributes
Relational attributes (إضافات) are properties like above-ness, below-ness, ownership. They depend on a relation between two things.
These are accidents, not substances.
3
Follow the conditional argument
The argument says: if the accident (relational attribute) divides when its locus divides, then the attribute itself would be divisible. This leads to an absurdity.
Think: a body can be divided, so its accident would also have parts.
4
Grasp the absurd consequence
If a relational attribute were divisible, you could have half of 'above-ness' or a third of 'ownership'. The intellect rejects such fractional relations.
This shows that relational attributes are indivisible.
5
Connect to the broader proof
This proof supports the existence of indivisible accidents, which in turn supports the existence of indivisible substances (atoms).
It is one of several proofs in the kalam tradition for atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن الإضافات عارضة للأجسام . مثل الفوقية والتحتية والمالكية والمملوكية . فلو انقسم الحال لإنقسام محله ، لزم كون الإضافات منقسمة ، وذلك محال . لأنه يلزم أن يحصل للفوقية والتحتية نصف وثلث وربع . وذلك مما لا يقبله العقل .
The third proof: Relational attributes are accidents of bodies, such as 'above-ness' and 'below-ness' and 'ownership' and 'being owned'. If the accident were to divide due to the division of its locus, then the relational attributes would be divisible, and that is impossible. Because it would necessitate that 'above-ness' and 'below-ness' have a half, a third, and a quarter. And that is something the intellect does not accept.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This is a proof against the divisibility of accidents, specifically relational attributes. The author points out that relations like being above or below are accidents inhering in bodies. If these accidents were divisible along with their loci (the bodies), then we could speak of half of 'above-ness' or a third of 'ownership'. Such fractional parts of a relation are conceptually impossible; for instance, 'above-ness' cannot be half above and half something else. Thus, the accident must be indivisible, supporting the existence of indivisible accidents and, by extension, indivisible substance.

Key Terms
إضافات
فوقية
تحتية
مالكية
مملوكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies that relational attributes are accidents.
Correction: The proof does not deny that relations are accidents; it only denies that they are divisible.

Checkpoint
What does the author use as examples of relational attributes?
A) Above-ness and below-ness
B) Existence and non-existence
C) Unity and plurality
D) Substance and accident

Key Takeaway
Relational attributes are accidents that cannot be divided because fractions of relations are rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If relationships like 'above-ness' cannot be divided, what does that say about the integrity of the relationships you hold—like being a parent, spouse, or friend? Are you treating any of these as if they can be split into fractions, or do you honor them as whole and indivisible? Allow this lesson to press you: in what relationship have you been 'half-present' recently, and what will you change to restore its wholeness?
?
The intellect rejects a half of 'above-ness'. Yet we often think of our connections to Allah as something we can divide—giving part of our attention to Him and part to the world. Does this lesson expose the irrationality of such a division? How can you ensure your devotion to Allah remains undivided, especially in moments when you are tempted to compartmentalize?

The Indivisibility of Imaginative and Thinking Forces
The fourth proof shows that the imaginative and thinking faculties cannot be divided.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the imaginative and thinking faculties cannot be divided, and why this supports the atomist position.

Core Idea
Imagination is a judgment, and judgments cannot be divided.

In this passage, the author presents a tight logical proof that the imaginative and thinking forces are indivisible. He considers what would happen if they were divided, and shows that every possibility leads to an absurdity. This argument reinforces the atomist view that certain mental faculties are not composed of parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Notice the logical structure: the author states a conditional and then examines its consequences.
The proof is about two specific forces: imaginative (wahmiyya) and thinking (mufakkira).
2
Identify the three possibilities after division
The author says that if the forces were divided, each part would be either (1) a complete imagination, (2) a part of imagination, or (3) neither. Underline these three options in your mind.
3
Understand why each option is impossible
Option 1 would make the part equal to the whole, which is absurd. Option 2 fails because imagination is essentially a judgment (e.g., 'this is a friend') and such judgments cannot be split. Option 3 is refuted by the same reasoning used for the rational force.
The key point is that judgment (hukm) is indivisible.
4
Conclude the proof
Since all possibilities are impossible, the original assumption (divisibility) must be false. Therefore the imaginative and thinking forces are indivisible.
This proof is used to support atomism, as it shows that certain mental faculties are not composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن القوة الوهمية والقوة المفكرة : قوى جسمانية ، عند الشيخ ، فيجب أن تحصل للقوة الوهمية والفكرية أجزاء وأبعاض، وذلك محال لأنها لو انقسمت لكان كل واحد من أبعاضها، إما أن يكون وهماً ، وحينئذ يكون الجزء مساوياً للكل ، وهو محال . أو يكون بعض الوهم . لكن الوهم لا معنى له إلا الحكم. يكون هذا صديقاً ، وكون ذاك عدواً . وهذا الحكم لا يقبل القسمة البنة . أو لا يكون وهماً لكل ذلك الشيء ولا لبعضه، وحينئذ يبطل هذا القسم بعين ما أبطلتم مثله في القوة العقلية
The fourth proof: The imaginative force and the thinking force are physical faculties, according to the Shaykh, so it would be necessary for the imaginative and thinking force to have parts and pieces. That is impossible because if they were divided, each one of their parts would either be imagination (wahm), in which case the part would equal the whole, and that is impossible; or it would be a part of imagination. But imagination has no meaning except judgment: that this is a friend and that is an enemy. And this judgment does not admit division at all. Or it would not be imagination of the whole thing nor of a part of it, and then this division is invalidated by the very same thing you invalidated in the case of the rational force.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This proof concerns the faculties of imagination (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya) and thinking (al-quwwa al-mufakkira), which are considered physical faculties according to 'the Sheikh' (likely Ibn Sina). The author argues that if these faculties were divisible, then each part would have to be either a complete act of imagination or a partial one. If a part is a complete imagination, then that part is equal to the whole, which is absurd (the part cannot be as comprehensive as the whole). If it is a partial imagination, then imagination is essentially a judgment (e.g., judging someone as a friend or enemy), and such a judgment is indivisible (a judgment of 'friend' cannot be half-friend). The third possibility is that the part is neither imagination of the whole nor of a part, which is also untenable because it would negate the nature of the force. Thus, divisibility leads to impossibility, so these faculties must be indivisible.

Key Terms

قوة مفكرة
وهم
حكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof denies the existence of imagination or thinking.
Correction: The proof does not deny these faculties; it argues they are indivisible, thus supporting their non-composite nature.

Checkpoint
What does the author say imagination (wahm) reduces to?
A) A physical part
B) A judgment
C) An image
D) A memory

Key Takeaway
The imaginative and thinking forces are indivisible because division leads to absurdities, especially since imagination is essentially an indivisible judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If imagination is an indivisible judgment, what does that reveal about the way you form opinions of others? Are your judgments ever fragmented—seeing someone as both friend and enemy at once? How can you train yourself to hold unified, truthful judgments?
?
The proof argues that a part cannot equal the whole. In your own life, do you ever try to satisfy your spiritual commitments with 'part' of your attention or effort, expecting the same result? What would it mean to give the whole of yourself to your beliefs?
?
This argument shows that division of a judgment is impossible. Is there a judgment about yourself or your Lord that you have allowed to become divided—believing two contradictory things? How will you resolve that division starting today?

The Sixth Proof: Indivisibility of the Nature of Numbers
The sixth proof demonstrates indivisibility of the nature of numbers via the example of ten.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the nature of a number like 'ten' is a single, indivisible accident, and why it cannot be divided among its units.

Core Idea
The concept of 'ten' is one essence; it cannot be split into parts per unit.

In this sixth proof, the author examines the nature of numbers, specifically the number ten. He argues that the quality of being ten is a single accident that attaches to a collection, not to each individual unit. This leads to a crucial point about indivisibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: first definitions, then a dilemma.
2
Identify the two options
The author presents two possibilities for how the single essence of ten relates to its units. Find both options and note which one is explicitly ruled out.
3
Understand the impossibility of the first option
The first option is that the essence attaches to each unit. The author explains why this is impossible: each unit would then be ten, and one accident would subsist in many loci.
4
Conclude the indivisibility
Since the first option is impossible, the only remaining possibility is that the essence of ten is indivisible and attaches to the collection as a whole.
This conclusion supports the broader argument for the existence of an indivisible entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن طباع الأعداد ماهيات مختلفة . فكون الشيء : عشرة : عرض ، وكونه تسعة : عرض آخر . وهذان العرضان : نوعان مختلفان بالماهية ، داخلان تحت جنس الكم المنفصل . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : المفهوم من كون العشرة : عشرة : مفهوم واحد ، وماهيـة واحدة . فتلك الماهية الواحدة ، إما أن تكون عارضة لكل واحد من تلك الأحاد ، وإما أن تنقسم بحسب انقسام تلك الآحاد . والأول محال . وإلا لزم أن يكون كل واحد من تلك الأحاد ، موصوفاً بأنه عشرة . وذلك محال ، وأنه يلزم قيام العرض الواحد بالمحال الكثيرة . وهو محال
The sixth proof: The natures of numbers (tiba' al-a'dad) are different essences. So a thing being ten is an accident, and it being nine is another accident. These two accidents are two types differing in essence, falling under the genus of discrete quantity. If you know this, we say: The concept of 'tenness' is a single concept and a single essence. That single essence either attaches to each one of those units (the ones), or it divides according to the division of those units. The first is impossible; otherwise, each one of those units would be described as being ten, and that is impossible, and it would necessitate one accident subsisting in many loci, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This proof concerns the nature of numbers such as 'ten'. The number 'ten' is an accident that characterizes a collection of ten units. The author argues that the essence of 'ten' (the concept of being ten) is a single, indivisible meaning. If it were divisible, then each unit would have a part of 'ten', but each unit is only one, not ten. Alternatively, if the essence of 'ten' were to attach to each unit individually, then each unit would be 'ten', which is false. Thus, the accident 'ten' cannot be divided among its units; it must be a single accident inhering in the collection as a whole, implying indivisibility.

Key Terms
طباع الأعداد
ماهية
عدد عشرة
كم منفصل
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that numbers themselves are divisible substances, or that this proof is about the substance of numbers.
Correction: This proof does not claim numbers themselves are substances; it uses numerical accidents to show indivisibility. Numbers like 'ten' are accidents, not substances.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the concept of 'ten' is a single essence. What would happen if it were to attach to each unit?
A) Each unit would be ten
B) Each unit would be one
C) Ten would be divisible
D) The units would become ten

Key Takeaway
The nature of a number like ten is a single, indivisible accident that cannot be split among its units.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the accident of 'tenness' cannot be divided among units, what does that say about the unity of your intention in acts of worship? Is your devotion a single, indivisible essence directed solely to Allah, or is it fragmented among worldly motives? How will you test this in your next prayer?
?
The proof eliminates false possibilities before affirming the truth. In your own understanding of tawheed, have you genuinely eliminated all false conceptions of Allah? What false ideas about His attributes have you not yet critically examined?
?
The impossibility of one accident subsisting in many loci mirrors the impossibility of sincere faith coexisting with hypocrisy. Where in your life are you trying to serve two masters? What practical step can you take today to unify your heart's allegiance?

Proof That Universal Knowledge Is Indivisible
Fakhr al-Din al-Razi argues that universal knowledge cannot be divided into similar parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Fakhr al-Din al-Razi argues that universal knowledge cannot be divided into two similar parts, and how this preserves its universality and abstractness.

Core Idea
Universal knowledge is indivisible into similar parts because that would make it particular.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a rigorous rational proof to show that universal conceptual knowledge (like the concept of 'man') cannot be divided into parts. He argues that if division were possible, it would either be into similar or dissimilar parts. He focuses on the impossibility of division into similar parts, offering two sophisticated reasons that highlight the abstract and shared nature of universals. This lesson walks through these arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall structure of the proof
The passage begins by stating that if universal knowledge were divisible, it would be into similar or dissimilar parts. Both are shown impossible. Focus on the first branch (similar parts).
The text only completes the argument for similar parts; the second branch is not fully covered.
2
Examine the first reason: material accidents
Read how each part must differ from the whole in material accidents like quantity, which would make the form non-abstract and hence not universal.
Material accidents (al-awarid al-madiyya) are key here.
3
Examine the second reason: division as a condition
Analyze whether division is a condition for intelligibility. Follow the three sub-proofs showing that if it is a condition, absurdities follow.
The second sub-branch (division not a condition) is incomplete in the text.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Realize that both reasons show universal knowledge cannot be divided into similar parts, thus upholding its universal, shared nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو انقسم هذا العلم، لكان إما ينقسم إلى جزءين متشابهين أو مختلفين والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بكونه منقسماً. وإنما قلنا: إنه يستحيل أن ينقسم إلى جزءين متشابهين لوجهين: الأول: إن كل واحد من ذيك الجزءين، لا بد وأن يكون مخالفاً للكل، لامتناع أن يكون الكل مثل الجزء، وتلك المخالفة ليست بالحقيقة ولوازمها، وإلا لم يكن ذلك الانقسام: انقساماً إلى جزءين متشابهين. بل لا بد وأن يكون بالعوارض المادية كالمقدار والشكل، فحينئذ يلزم أن لا يكون ذلك العلم مجرداً عن جميع اللواحق الزائدة، لأنا بينا: أنه حصل معه المقدار الذي به يخالف الجزء كله في المقدار، وإذا لم تكن تلك الصورة مجردة، امتنع كونها مشتركاً فيها بين المختلفات. لأن الموصوف بصفة معينة يستحيل أن يصدق هو على ما لا يكون موصوفاً بها، لكن العلوم الكلية صور مشترك فيها بين الأشخاص. فإن المعقول من الإنسان، لا شك أنه مشترك فيه بين جميع الأشخاص. الوجه الثاني في إبطال هذا القسم: وهو أن ذلك الانقسام إما أن يكون شرطاً لكون تلك الصورة معقولة، أو لا يكون. والأول باطل. أما أولا: فلانه يلزم كون كل واحد من الجزءين مخالفاً بالماهية لذلك الكل، لوجوب مباينة الشرط للمشروط. وأما ثانياً: فقبل حصول الانقسام، وجب أن لا تكون الصورة معقولة، لفقدان الشرط، وأما ثالثاً: فالشيء الذي يكون هذا حاله، وجب أن يكون منقسماً، وليس كل معقول كذلك. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: أن تلك الصورة منقسمة ليس شرطاً لكونها معقولة. فهذا يقتضي أن تكون تلك الصورة العقلية عند
If this knowledge (ilm) were divisible, it would either divide into two similar parts or two different parts, and both divisions are invalid; thus the statement that it is divisible is false. We say: It is impossible for it to divide into two similar parts for two reasons. First: Each of those two parts must necessarily be different from the whole, because it is impossible for the whole to be like the part. That difference is not in essence (haqiqa) and its necessary concomitants, otherwise that division would not be a division into two similar parts. Rather it must be in material accidents (al-awarid al-madiyya) such as quantity (miqdar) and shape (shakl). Then it would follow that that knowledge is not abstract (mujarrad) from all superadded adjuncts, for we have explained that quantity accompanies it, by which the part differs from the whole in quantity; and if that form (sura) is not abstract, it is impossible for it to be shared among different individuals. Because that which is described by a specific attribute cannot be truly predicated of what is not described by it. But universal knowledges (al-ulum al-kulliyya) are forms shared among individuals. For the intelligible of man (al-ma'qul min al-insan) is undoubtedly shared among all individuals. Second proof for refuting this division: That division either is a condition (shart) for that form being intelligible, or not. The first is false. First [sub-proof]: Because it would necessitate each of the two parts being different in essence (mukhalifan bi al-mahiyya) from the whole, due to the necessity of the condition being distinct from the conditioned. Second: Before the division occurs, the form must not be intelligible, due to the absence of the condition. Third: That which is in this state must be divisible, but not every intelligible is like that. As for the second division: That one says that that form is divisible [but] it is not a condition for its being intelligible. This would require that that intellectual form be...
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof (burhan) for the indivisibility of universal knowledge ('ilm kulli). It begins by stating that if this knowledge were divisible, it would be divisible into either two similar parts or two different parts. Both are shown to be impossible, therefore the claim of divisibility is false. The text then focuses on proving the impossibility of division into similar parts, giving two reasons (wajhan). The first reason argues that if divided, each part must differ from the whole in material accidents like quantity and shape; thus the form would not be abstract and could not be shared among different individuals, but universal knowledges are precisely shared forms. The second reason examines whether division is a condition for the form's being intelligible. If it is a condition, then the form would be different in essence from the whole, would not be intelligible before division, and would require that the whole itself be divisible (which is not always the case). If division is not a condition, then the form would still be subject to divisibility, which contradicts its universality. The argument is incomplete in the text but clearly aims to defend the abstract and universal nature of conceptual knowledge against materialistic division.

صور مشتركة
عوارض مادية
مجرد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that universal concepts can be split into parts like material objects, but the author shows that would make them particular and non-universal.
Correction: Universal knowledge is abstract and shared; division would introduce material accidents or conditions that destroy universality.

Checkpoint
