A) Because the two parts would be identical to the whole.
B) Because they would differ in material accidents like quantity, losing universality.
C) Because division is impossible in any case.
D) Because the whole would cease to exist.

Key Takeaway
Fakhr al-Din al-Razi proves that universal knowledge cannot be divided into similar parts because division would introduce material accidents or conditions that render the form particular and non-universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi argues that universal knowledge must be abstract and shared. In your own intellectual journey, do you cling to truths as if they are private possessions? How might acknowledging the shared nature of knowledge change the way you engage with others who hold different views?
?
This proof shows that division would require material accidents, making knowledge particular. Is there an area of your faith where you have 'divided' a core belief into manageable parts, only to lose its unifying essence? What would it take to embrace the whole again?
?
The second wajh examines whether division is a condition for intelligibility. Consider your own learning: have you ever conditioned your understanding of a concept on having it broken down into pieces, missing its simple unity? Will you trust the unadorned truth as it is?

First Argument Against Division into Similar Parts
Presents the first argument refuting division of the universal into similar parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dividing the universal mental form into similar parts is impossible, as it would destroy its universality.

Core Idea
Division of a universal forces material accidents, making it particular, not universal.

This lesson introduces the first argument from the author against the possibility of dividing the universal mental form into two similar parts. The argument shows that such division would necessarily involve material accidents, which contradicts the very nature of universality. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the nature of mental concepts and their role in knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument starts with the fact that each part must differ from the whole. Why is that? Because the whole cannot be identical to a part.
3
Follow the reasoning on difference
The difference cannot be in the essence or its implications, otherwise the division would not be into similar parts. So it must be through material accidents like quantity and shape.
Material accidents are attached to matter.
4
Understand the consequence
If material accidents attach, the universal form is not abstracted from them. Then it cannot be common among different individuals, because a thing with a specific attribute cannot apply to something lacking it.
Universals like 'human' must be common.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Thus, division into similar parts is impossible for a universal. The example of 'human' shows why: it is common to all individuals, so it cannot have material accidents.
This argument defends the abstraction of universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن كل واحد من ذيك الجزءين لا بد وأن يكون مخالفاً للكل لامتناع أن يكون الكل مثل الجزء وتلك المخالفة ليست بالحقيقة ولوازمها وإلا لم يكن ذلك الانقسام انقساماً إلى جزءين متشابهين بل لا بد وأن يكون بالعوارض المادية كالمقدار والشكل فحينئذ يلزم أن لا يكون ذلك العلم مجرداً عن جميع اللواحق الزائدة لأنا بينا أنه حصل معه المقدار الذي به يخالف الجزء كله في المقدار وإذا لم تكن تلك الصورة مجردة امتنع كونها مشتركاً فيها بين المختلفات لأن الموصوف بصفة معينة يستحيل أن يصدق هو على ما لا يكون موصوفاً بها لكن العلوم الكلية صور مشترك فيها بين الأشخاص فإن المعقول من الإنسان لا شك أنه مشترك فيه بين جميع الأشخاص
First: Indeed each one of those two parts must necessarily be different from the whole, because it is impossible for the whole to be like the part. And that difference is not in essence and its implications, otherwise that division would not be a division into two similar parts. Rather, it must be through material accidents such as quantity and shape. Then it necessarily follows that that knowledge [universal] is not abstracted from all supervenient attachments, because we have explained that quantity occurs with it, by which the part differs from the whole in quantity. And if that form is not abstracted, it is impossible for it to be common among different things, because a thing described by a specific attribute cannot truthfully apply to what is not described by it. But universal sciences are forms common among individuals, for the intellectually conceived 'human' is undoubtedly common among all individuals.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (al-wajh al-awwal) against the claim that the universal mental form (al-surah al-'aqliyyah) can be divided into two similar parts. The author begins by stating that each of the two parts must be different from the whole, since the whole cannot be identical to a part. This difference cannot be in the essence (al-haqiqah) or its necessary implications, because then the division would not be into similar parts. Therefore, the difference must be through material accidents like quantity and shape. Consequently, the universal form would not be abstracted from such attachments. But we know that universals are common among different individuals—for example, the concept 'human' applies to all humans equally. If the form were not abstract and carried material accidents, it could not be common, because a thing with a specific attribute cannot apply to something lacking that attribute. Thus, the universal cannot be divided into similar parts.

انقسام
عوارض مادية
مخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dividing a universal into similar parts is possible without affecting its universality.
Correction: Division would introduce material accidents, making it particular, not universal.

Checkpoint

B) The form would become material and lose its universality.
C) The division would be into different essences.
D) The form would remain abstract.

Key Takeaway
The universal mental form cannot be divided into similar parts because division would require material differences, destroying its commonality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting Division as a Condition for Intelligibility
The author refutes the first alternative of the dichotomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments that disprove the idea that division is a necessary condition for a mental form to be intelligible.

Core Idea
If division were a condition, nothing would be intelligible before division—an absurdity.

The second argument against the first division begins by setting up a clear dichotomy: either the division of the mental form is a condition for its being intelligible, or it is not. The author first refutes the possibility that it is a condition, offering three distinct reasons. This lesson walks through those reasons, showing the logical inconsistencies that arise if division were required for understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the three arguments presented.
The passage begins with the dichotomy and then lists three points starting with 'أما أولا', 'أما ثانيا', 'أما ثالثا'.
2
Identify the three arguments
The passage gives three reasons why division cannot be a condition. Find each one and understand its logical force.
The first argument: condition must be distinct from conditioned. Second: absence of condition before division. Third: the form would have to be divisible, but not all intelligibles are.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why each argument is a problem. Think about what it means for a mental form to require division in order to be conceived.
This connects to the learning objective and shows the absurd consequences.
4
Summarize the refutation
Write a short summary of the three arguments in your own words, showing why the first alternative fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في إبطال هذا القسم وهو أن ذلك الانقسام إما أن يكون شرطاً لكون تلك الصورة معقولة أو لا يكون والأول باطل أما أولا فلانه يلزم كون كل واحد من الجزءين مخالفاً بالماهية لذلك الكل لوجوب مباينة الشرط للمشروط وأما ثانياً فقبل حصول الانقسام وجب أن لا تكون الصورة معقولة لفقدان الشرط وأما ثالثاً فالشيء الذي يكون هذا حاله وجب أن يكون منقسماً وليس كل معقول كذلك وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال أن تلك الصورة منقسمة ليس شرطاً لكونها معقولة فهذا يقتضي أن تكون تلك الصورة العقلية عند انقسامها مغشاة بعوارض غريبة من جمع وتفريق ويكون في أقل بلاغ هذا لفظ الإشارات وتفسيره إن كل واحد من جزئها مكتفي بجزء من ذلك المحل وذلك الجزء من تلك الصورة مساوي لكلها في تمام الماهية وحكم الشيء حكم مثله فوجب أن يكون حلول كل تلك الصورة في جزء من ذلك المحل ممكناً وإذا كان كذلك كان حلول كل تلك الصورة في كل ذلك المحل عارضاً غريباً وفي أقل منه بلاغ
The second aspect in refuting this division: that division is either a condition for that form being intelligible, or not. The first is invalid: First, because it necessitates each one of the two parts to be different in essence from that whole, due to the necessity of the condition being distinct from the conditioned. Second, before the division occurs, the form must not be intelligible, due to the absence of the condition. Third, a thing in this state must be divisible, but not every intelligible is like that. As for the second division: that that form being divided is not a condition for it being intelligible—this requires that that mental form, when divided, be covered with strange accidents of composition and separation, and it would be in less than [a certain amount]. This is the text of al-Isharat and its interpretation: 'Indeed each one of its parts suffices with a part of that locus, and that part of that form is equal to the whole in complete essence, and the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like. So it must be that the existing of all that form in a part of that locus is possible. And if so, then the existing of all that form in all that locus is a strange accident, and in less than it suffices.'
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-thani) against the first division. The author sets up a dichotomy: either the division of the universal form is a condition (shart) for it being intelligible (ma'qulah), or it is not. He first refutes the possibility that division is a condition: (a) if division is a condition, then each part must be different in essence from the whole, because the condition must be distinct from the conditioned; but this contradicts the claim that the parts are similar. (b) Before division, the form would not be intelligible at all, since the condition is absent. (c) Moreover, a thing that requires division in order to be intelligible would itself have to be divisible, but not every intelligible is divisible (e.g., simple concepts). Next, he considers the second possibility: that division is not a condition. In that case, when the form is divided, it becomes covered with extraneous accidents like composition and separation, and would occupy less than a certain magnitude, implying materiality. The author then quotes from Avicenna's al-Isharat wa al-Tanbihat and its interpretation: if each part of the form suffices for a part of the locus (mahall), then that part is equal to the whole form in complete essence; therefore, the entire form existing in a part of the locus is possible, and its existence in the whole locus would be an accident. This further shows the absurdity of dividing a universal into similar parts.

Key Terms
شرط
مشروط
معقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Division as a condition for intelligibility seems plausible because many concepts require analysis.
Correction: The three arguments show that if division were a condition, it would lead to contradictions such as the parts differing from the whole and the form being unintelligible before division.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three arguments against division being a condition for intelligibility?
A) The parts would be different in essence from the whole.
B) The form would be unintelligible before division occurs.
C) The form would have to be non-physical.
D) The form would have to be divisible.

Key Takeaway
The three arguments conclusively show that division cannot be a condition for intelligibility without producing absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes postpone accepting a clear truth because you feel you haven't 'dissected' it enough? How does this lesson challenge that habit, and what simple truth can you commit to accepting today without overanalysis?
?
The lesson argues that before division, a form would be unintelligible if division were a condition. Reflect on a time when you trusted your immediate understanding of something simple without needing to break it apart. How can you cultivate that direct trust in your everyday spiritual and intellectual life?
?
If you act as if 'division is a condition' in your own thinking, you treat analysis as necessary for understanding. But the author shows this leads to absurdity. In what area of your belief or practice might you be demanding too much analysis before acting? What step can you take this week to rely on clear, simple comprehension without unnecessary dissection?

Refuting the Alternative: Division Not a Condition
The author refutes the second alternative and quotes al-Isharat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument against the second alternative and how the al-Isharat quote reinforces the absurdity of dividing a universal into similar parts.

Core Idea
If division is not a condition, the divided form becomes subject to accidental attributes, implying materiality.

Having refuted the first alternative, the author now examines the possibility that division is not a condition for intelligibility. He argues that if division occurs, the mental form becomes covered with strange accidents like composition and separation, and then cites Avicenna's al-Isharat to further illustrate the problem. This lesson unpacks that argument and the implications of the quote.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic for the second alternative and its refutation, including the al-Isharat quote. Notice the structure: claim, consequence, citation.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the first part (consequence)
The author states that if division is not a condition, then when the form is divided, it becomes covered with strange accidents like composition and separation, and would exist in less than a certain magnitude.
This suggests materiality, which is problematic for a mental form.
3
Analyze the al-Isharat quote
The quote argues that if each part of the form suffices for a part of the locus, then that part equals the whole form in essence, so the whole form could exist in a part of the locus, making its existence in the whole locus accidental.
This supports the idea that division leads to absurd consequences.
4
Synthesize the refutation
Combine the two parts: the initial consequence and the quote show that the second alternative is also impossible.
Thus, both alternatives are refuted, so the first division in question is invalid.

Refutation of Division into Parts Differing in Essence
Refutes the claim that the universal can be divided into parts differing in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dividing a universal mental form into parts differing in essence leads to an infinite regress and is therefore impossible.

Core Idea
A universal cannot be divided into essentially different parts without infinite regress.

This passage tackles a specific objection: that the universal mental form splits into parts differing in essence. The author argues that even if we suppose the form corporeal, the divisions are infinite, leading to absurd consequences. Let's see the two reasons given.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
Read the passage and identify the claim: that the universal form divides into two parts differing in essence. This is the second division mentioned earlier.
The claim is presented as an objection to the main argument.
2
Follow the conditional argument
The author says: if the mental form were corporeal, it would have parts corresponding to possible divisions in a body. But those divisions are infinite, so the parts would be infinite.
This sets up the impossibility.
3
Examine the first reason
First reason: if each of these infinite parts exists in a different part of the body (locus), then each body part would have a distinct accident, resulting in a body composed of actually infinite parts—absurd.
This shows a contradiction with the nature of a body.
4
Examine the second reason
Second reason: any multiplicity requires a unifying element. If the form has infinite parts, then a single part has occurred, and the original division would recur, leading to infinite regress.
This shows the argument is self-defeating.
5
Conclude the impossibility
Both reasons show the claim leads to absurdity, so the assumption that the universal can be divided into essentially different parts is invalid.
The refutation is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني من القسمين المذكورين في أول الدليل وهو أن يقال إن تلك الصورة تنقسم إلى جزءين مختلفين في الماهية فنقول هذا محال لأن الصورة العقلية لو كانت جسمانية الحصلت لها أجزاء بحسب الانقسامات الممكنة في الجسم لكن تلك الانقسامات غير متناهية فوجب أن تكون الأجزاء المختلفة الحاصلة لتلك الصورة العقلية غير متناهية وهذا محال لوجوه الأول إنه إذا كان كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء التي هي غير متناهية حالاً في جزء آخر من ذلك المحل فقد اختص كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك الجسم بعرض مخالف للعرض الحال في الجزء الآخر وذلك يوجب تركب الجسم من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل والثاني وهو أن تلك الكثرة سواء كانت متناهية أو غير متناهية فلا بد فيها من واحد فتلك الصورة العقلية إذا كانت مركبة من أجزاء غير متناهية فقد حصل فيها جزء واحد وحينئذ يعود التقسيم المذكور
As for the second division of the two divisions mentioned at the beginning of the proof—that it is said that that form divides into two parts differing in essence—we say: this is impossible, because if the mental form were corporeal, it would have parts according to the possible divisions in the body, but those divisions are infinite. Therefore, the different parts resulting for that mental form must be infinite. And this is impossible for reasons: First, if each one of those parts, which are infinite, exists in another part of that locus, then each part of that body is characterized by an accident different from the accident existing in the other part, and that necessitates the body being composed of actually infinite parts. Second, that multiplicity, whether finite or infinite, must have a unity in it; so if that mental form is composed of infinite parts, then a single part has occurred in it, and then the mentioned division returns.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage refutes the second division (al-qism al-thani) of the universal mental form—namely, that it divides into two parts that differ in essence (mukhtalifayn fi al-mahiyyah). The author states that this is impossible. He argues: if the mental form were corporeal (jismaniyyah), it would have parts corresponding to the possible divisions of a body. But those divisions are infinite; therefore, the parts of the form would be infinite. This is impossible for two reasons. First, if each of these infinite parts exists in a different part of the body (i.e., the locus), then each part of the body would have a different accident, resulting in a body composed of actually infinite parts—which is absurd. Second, any multiplicity, whether finite or infinite, must have a unifying element; if the form is composed of infinite parts, then a single part has occurred within it, and the original division (into two different parts) would recur, leading to an infinite regress. Thus, the assumption that the universal can be divided into essentially different parts is invalid.

جسمانية
انقسامات غير متناهية
أجزاء غير متناهية
تركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that dividing a universal into essentially different parts avoids materiality, but it still leads to infinite regress.
Correction: The passage shows that even if the universal is considered non-corporeal, the claim of division into essentially different parts implies infinite regress, making it impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason given against the infinite parts of the mental form?
A) The body would have infinite accidents.
B) The form would not be universal.
C) The parts would be similar.
D) The division would stop.

Key Takeaway
Dividing a universal mental form into essentially different parts leads to infinite regress and is therefore impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you reconsider your assumptions today?

Are you willing to let go of them for the sake of a simpler, more unified truth?

The Universal Form is Abstract from Shape and Position
Al-Razi presents Ibn Sina's second argument for the immateriality of universal intellectual forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the immateriality of the universal intellectual form: because it is abstract from shape and position, it cannot be bodily.

Core Idea
Universality cannot reside in a body because bodies always have specific shapes and positions.

Al-Razi presents a succinct argument for the non-bodily nature of the universal intellectual form. The argument hinges on two premises: universality means commonality across different individuals, and this commonality cannot be tied to any specific shape or position. Since bodies are necessarily shape-and-position-bound, the universal form must be non-bodily. In this lesson, we will unpack both premises and see how they lead to the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument statement
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that al-Razi begins by stating the conclusion: the universal form is abstract from shape and position, therefore non-bodily.
The argument is attributed to 'the Shaykh' (Ibn Sina).
2
Identify the two premises
Break the argument into two claims: (1) the universal form is abstract from shape and position; (2) anything abstract from shape and position cannot be bodily. See how they connect.
The passage then focuses on explaining the first premise.
3
Understand the explanation of the first premise
Read the explanation carefully. It defines the universal form as the commonality among individuals with different shapes, positions, and quantities. This commonality cannot have a specific shape or quantity.
This shows why the universal form is abstract from particular shapes.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if something cannot have a specific shape, can it be a body? The second premise says no. Thus, the universal form is non-bodily.
The argument is valid; we need to accept both premises to accept the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية من الوجوه التي ذكرها ( الشيخ ) : أن قال : ( الصورة العقلية الكلية مجردة عن الشكل والوضع ، وكل ما كان كذلك ، امتنع كونه جسمانياً . . بيان الأول : إن الصورة الكلية ، معناها : القدر المشترك بين الأشخاص ، ذوات الأشكال المختلفة ، والأوضاع المختلفة [ والمقادير المختلفة ] وما كان كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون موصوفاً بشكل معين ، وقدر معين .
The second argument among the points mentioned by the Shaykh: He said: 'The universal intellectual form is abstract from shape and position, and everything that is abstract from shape and position is impossible for it to be bodily.' Explanation of the first [premise]: The universal form means the commonality among individuals possessing different shapes, different positions, [and different quantities], and what is like that is impossible for it to be described with a specific shape and a specific quantity.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the immateriality of the intellect, attributed to 'the Shaykh'. The argument consists of two premises: first, the universal intellectual form is abstract from shape (al-shakl) and position (al-wad'), and second, anything that is abstract from shape and position cannot be bodily. The author then explains the first premise: the universal form is the common aspect (al-qadr al-mushtarak) shared among individuals that have different shapes, positions, and quantities. Because this commonality is not restricted to any particular shape or quantity, it cannot be described by a specific shape or quantity, implying it cannot be a bodily entity. The passage ends mid-argument; the reader expects further elaboration of the second premise or a conclusion.

Key Terms
الصورة العقلية الكلية
الشكل
الوضع
القدر المشترك
مجردة
جسمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a universal concept can exist in a bodily substrate, but the argument here emphasizes that universality itself precludes being tied to a specific shape or position.
Correction: The universal form is a common aspect among many individuals; therefore it cannot be limited to one particular shape or position, which would be required if it were bodily.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why can the universal form not be described with a specific shape?
A) Because it is a commonality among many individuals with different shapes.
B) Because it exists only in the mind.
C) Because shapes are accidental to forms.
D) Because the argument is still incomplete.

Key Takeaway
The universal intellectual form is non-bodily because it abstracts from shape and position, which are necessary attributes of any physical body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the concept 'humanity,' do you unconsciously attach a specific shape or face to it? How does this tendency tie you to bodily limitations, and what would it mean for your intellectual pursuit to truly abstract from all particular shapes?
?
This argument claims that the object of your intellect—the universal—is non-bodily. Have you ever acted as if your mind's grasp of truth is bound by physical constraints (like fatigue or bias), thereby denying the immaterial nature of your intellect? What would change if you consistently treated your understanding as rooted in an immaterial faculty?
?
Ibn Sina's argument forces you to see that a common concept cannot be pinned down to any specific quantity. Is there a 'shape' or 'position' in your life—a particular role, status, or possession—that you mistakenly identify as essential to who you are? How can you detach your identity from it and embrace your universal human dignity?

The Third Proof: Rational Soul's Capacity for Infinite Acts
A syllogistic proof for the immateriality of the rational soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the rational soul is immaterial because it can perform infinite acts while bodily powers are limited to finite acts.

Core Idea
The rational soul's capacity for infinite acts proves it is not a bodily power.

This lesson presents a clear syllogistic argument from Islamic theologians for the immateriality of the rational soul. The proof hinges on a key difference: the rational faculty can engage in infinite perceptions and acts, whereas any bodily power is inherently limited by its divisibility. Let's examine the structure of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism structure
Read the passage and identify the two premises and the conclusion of the syllogism.
Note that the passage first states the syllogism, then explains each premise separately.
2
Examine the first premise
How does the rational faculty's capacity for infinite perceptions support the claim of infinite acts? Understand that perception is a type of act, so infinite perception implies infinite acts.
The proof relies on the idea that the rational form can have infinite perceptions, e.g., of truths or numbers.
3
Examine the second premise
Why does the divisibility of bodily powers lead to finitude? Because the capacity of a part is less than the whole, and the whole's capacity is a finite multiple of a finite part, hence finite.
The key principle is that a part cannot equal the whole, and anything less than something else is finite.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion follows necessarily: the rational faculty is not a bodily power. Reflect on what this implies about the nature of the soul.
This is a foundational proof in Islamic theology for the immateriality of the soul.

الحجة الثالثة : قالوا : القوة العاقلة تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية ، ولا شيء من القوى الجسمانية يقوى على أفعال غير متناهية ، ينتج : أن القوة العاقلة ليست من القوى الجسمانية . أما بيان المقدمة الأولى : فهو أن الصورة العاقلة تقوى على إدراكات غير متناهية . وذلك يدل على أنها تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية . وأما بيان المقدمة الثانية : فهو أن كل قوة جسمانية ، فإنها تنقسم بانقسام محلها . فالذي تقوى عليه بعض القوة ، يجب أن يكون أقل من الذي تقوى عليه كل القوة . وإلا لزم أن يكون البعض مثلاً للكل . وهو محال . وكل ما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه ، فالذي تقوى عليه بعض القوة ، يجب أن يكون متناهياً ، والذي يقوى عليه الكل، أضعاف ما يقوى عليه البعض مراراً متناهية . وضعف المتناهي مراراً متناهية ، يجب أن يكون متناهياً ، فيلزم أن يقال : إن كل ما تقوى عليه القوة الجسمانية ، يجب أن يكون متناهياً .
The third proof: They said: The rational faculty has power over infinite acts, and no bodily power has power over infinite acts; conclusion: The rational faculty is not among the bodily powers. As for the explanation of the first premise: The rational form has power over infinite perceptions, and that indicates that it has power over infinite acts. As for the explanation of the second premise: Every bodily power is divisible by the division of its subject. So what some of the power is capable of must be less than what the whole power is capable of; otherwise the part would be equal to the whole, which is absurd. And everything less than something else is finite. So what some of the power is capable of must be finite. And what the whole is capable of is a finite multiple of what the part is capable of. And a finite multiplied finitely must be finite. Therefore, it must be said: Everything that a bodily power is capable of must be finite.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (ḥujja) for the immateriality of the rational soul. It is structured as a syllogism with two premises. The first premise states that the rational faculty can perform infinite acts, evidenced by its ability to have infinite perceptions. The second premise argues that no bodily power can perform infinite acts because bodily powers are divisible with their subject; the capacity of a part is less than that of the whole, and since less is finite, the whole's capacity is a finite multiple of a finite, hence finite. The conclusion follows that the rational faculty is not a bodily power. The proof relies on the logical principle that part cannot equal whole.

Key Terms

غير متناه
متناه
انقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodily powers can perform infinite acts through accumulation over time or across generations.
Correction: The proof shows that even the whole capacity of a bodily power is a finite multiple of a finite part, so it remains finite. Accumulation of finite acts does not yield an infinite capacity because the power itself is bounded.

Checkpoint
Why can't a bodily power perform infinite acts according to the proof?
A) Because it is divisible and its part's capacity is finite
B) Because it is weak
C) Because it depends on the body
D) Because it is temporary

Key Takeaway
The rational soul's ability to engage in infinite acts sets it apart from any bodily power, confirming its immaterial nature.

What one area of your thinking have you been confining to the finite, and how will you begin to exercise your soul's infinite capacity starting today?

The proof shows that your soul can engage in infinite perceptions of truth. Yet you spend so much time on finite distractions. What is one infinite truth (about Allah, yourself, or the purpose of life) that you have been avoiding contemplating? How will you change that this week?

Consider that your bodily powers are finite and will decay, but your rational soul is not. How does this change the way you prioritize your time between caring for your body and nurturing your soul? Name one concrete step you will take to shift the balance towards the soul.

Objection and Response to the Third Proof
Objection and response: rational soul's limit and the reductio on bodily powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the third proof and the author's counter-argument that turns the reasoning against itself.

Core Idea
The objection backfires: if the soul can reach a limit, so can bodily powers—invalidating the original proof.

Having presented the third proof that the rational soul is capable of infinite acts, the author now anticipates an objection. The objector questions the soul's infinite capacity and proposes a limit. The author responds by showing that the very logic used against the soul would equally apply to bodily powers, creating a contradiction that undermines the proof. This exchange deepens our understanding of essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) and its role in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow of the objection and response. Notice the back-and-forth structure.
Pay attention to the repeated phrase 'change from essential possibility to essential impossibility'—it is the crux of the argument.
2
Identify the initial objection
The objector denies that the rational soul can have infinite perceptions. Find the reasoning behind this denial and the alternative proposal of a limit.
The objector says: 'Why may it not be said that it reaches a limit...?'
3
Understand the opponent's counter-argument
The opponent (proponent of the proof) responds by saying that a limit would change essential possibility to essential impossibility, which is absurd. Grasp why they consider this a refutation.
This assumes that the soul's capacity for perception is essentially possible, so it cannot become impossible without a change in essence.
4
Follow the author's counter-response
The author turns the tables: if that reasoning is sound, it would also apply to bodily powers. Since a bodily power can exist forever (by possibility), it must also be capable of infinite acts. See how this leads to the collapse of the proof.
The author uses the same principle of essential possibility to draw the opposite conclusion.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with the conclusion that the opponent's argument destroys their own proof. Consider how this strengthens the author's position.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض : لا نسلم أن القوة العاقلة ، تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية وأما قوله : ( إنها تقوى على إدراكات غير متناهية، والإدراكات أفعال ، ينتج : أنها تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية وقلنا : لا نسلم أنها تقوى على إدراكات غير متناهية . ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنها تنتهي في الإدراكات والمعارف إلى حيث لا يمكنها أن تزيد عليه شيئاً ؟ فإن قالوا : هذا محال ، وذلك لأن جوهر النفس ، لما أمكنها تحصيل الإدراك والمعرفة ، فلو انتهت إلى حد يمتنع عليها تحصيل الزائد لزم أن ينقلب الشيء من الإمكان الذاتي ، إلى الامتناع الذاتي وهو محال . فنقول : إن صح هذا الكلام . فهذا بعينه يدل على أن القوة الجسمانية تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية . وذلك لأن تلك القوة الجسمانية ، وجب القول بصحة بقائها أبداً . وإلا لزم انتهاؤها إلى وقت يمتنع بقاؤها فيه . وحينئذ يلزم الانتقال من الإمكان الذاتي، إلى الامتناع الذاتي وهو محال . وإذا ثبت إمكان بقائها أبداً ، وجب إمكان بقاء كونها مؤثرة أبداً . لو انتهت في صحة التأثير والفعل . إلى حيث أن يمتنع ذلك بعده ، لزم انتقالها من الإمكان الذاتي . إلى الامتناع الذاتي . وهو محال ؟ فثبت : إن هذا الكلام الذي ذكروه في بيان أن القوة العاقلة تقوى على إدراكات غير متناهية إن صح ، وجب القطع بأن القوة الجسمانية تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية وذلك يوجب سقوط دليلهم بالكلية
The objection: We do not grant that the rational faculty has power over infinite acts. As for his statement: 'It has power over infinite perceptions, and perceptions are acts, so it has power over infinite acts' – we say: We do not grant that it has power over infinite perceptions. Why may it not be said that it reaches a limit in perceptions and cognitions beyond which it cannot increase? If they say: This is impossible, because the substance of the soul, since it is possible for it to acquire perception and cognition, if it reaches a limit where it is impossible for it to acquire more, then it would change from essential possibility to essential impossibility, which is absurd. We say: If this statement is correct, then this very same thing indicates that the bodily power has power over infinite acts. That is because that bodily power must be said to be capable of existing forever; otherwise it would reach a time when its existence is impossible, and then it would change from essential possibility to essential impossibility, which is absurd. And if the possibility of its existing forever is established, then the possibility of its being effective forever is established. If it reached a limit in the soundness of effect and act such that it is impossible after that, then it would change from essential possibility to essential impossibility, which is absurd. So it is established: If this statement they mentioned in explaining that the rational faculty has power over infinite perceptions is sound, then it must be concluded that the bodily power has power over infinite acts, and that causes the collapse of their proof entirely.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the third proof and a detailed response. The objector denies the first premise that the rational faculty can perceive infinitely, suggesting it might reach a limit. The opponent argues that such a limit would change essential possibility to impossibility, which is absurd. The author responds by turning the argument around: if that reasoning is valid, it would also apply to bodily powers. Since a bodily power can potentially exist forever, it cannot reach a limit of effectiveness, otherwise the same absurdity would follow. Therefore, the bodily power would also have infinite acts, which contradicts the original proof. Thus, the objection is refuted by showing its own consequence undermines the proof.

Correction: The author demonstrates that accepting the objection's logic leads to an absurdity: bodily powers would also be infinite, which undermines the entire proof.

B) Bodily powers can also perform infinite acts

Key Takeaway
The same logical principle used against the soul's infinity can be turned back to show the absurdity of denying infinite capacity to bodily powers.

Objection and Reply on Bodily Powers
Objection and reply on finitude of bodily powers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a parity argument to rebut an objection about the finitude of bodily powers.

Core Idea
No rational difference between bodily and rational powers forces consistency.

This passage is a critical exchange in the debate over whether bodily powers (imagination, thought, memory) can be infinite. The opponent argues they must eventually become unable to act, but the author cleverly turns the objection back on the opponent by demanding the same logic be applied to the rational power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's statement followed by the author's reply.
The passage uses a dialectical style common in kalam.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent asserts that bodily powers like imagination and memory cannot perform acts without end; they must reach a limit.
3
Understand the author's rebuttal
The author asks why the same limit cannot be applied to the rational power. He claims there is no rational difference between the two cases, thus exposing inconsistency.
This is a parity-of-reasoning argument, not a direct proof.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider the broader principle: one cannot accept a limitation for one type of power while rejecting it for another without a rational distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التخيلات والتفكرات والتذكرات إلى غير النهاية . مع أنها عندكم قوى جسمانية .
فإن قالوا : لا نسلم أن هذه القوى تقوى على أفعالها إلى غير النهاية ، بل لا بد من الانتهاء إلى حيث لا تقوى على الفعل بعد ذلك
فتقول : فإذا جاز لكم أن تلتزموا ذلك ، فلم لا يجوز لغيركم أن يلتزم مثله في القوة العاقلة. وهو أن يقال : إنها تنتهي إلى حيث تقوى على الزيادة ؟ وما الفرق بين البابين ؟ فإنا لا نجد في العقل : فرقا بين الموضعين
Imaginations, thoughts, and memories extend without end, despite being bodily powers according to you. If they say: We do not grant that these powers are capable of their acts without end, but rather they must eventually reach a point where they are not capable of action after that. Then you say: If it is permissible for you to commit to that, why is it not permissible for someone else to commit to the same regarding the rational power? That is, it is said that it ends at a point where it is capable of increase? And what is the difference between the two cases? For we find no rational difference between the two situations.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair within a larger argument about the soul's capacity for infinite acts. The opponent begins by stating that imagination, thinking, and remembering are unlimited, even though they consider these powers bodily. The opponent then objects: they do not accept that these bodily powers can perform infinite acts; rather, they must eventually become unable to act. The author replies by turning the objection back: if you can accept that bodily powers have a limit, why can't someone else accept that the rational power has a limit? Specifically, one could say that the rational power terminates at a point where it is capable of increase? The author asks what differentiates the two cases, asserting that reason finds no distinction. Thus, the author forces consistency on the opponent.

Are Perceptions Actions or Passions?
Dialectical argument: question, objection, counter, author's reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether perceptions are actions or passions, and the author's argument that both concepts and assents are passions.

Core Idea
Perceptions are not actions but passions: the soul receives rather than produces them.

This lesson examines the third question in a series: if the soul can have infinite perceptions, why are they considered actions? The debate revolves around the nature of concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat). The author argues that even assents are passions because they consist of the soul receiving a belief.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question and initial objection
Read the opening: the questioner grants infinite perceptions but asks why they are called actions. The objection: perception is reception of form, so the soul is passive.
Note the comparison to prime matter (hayula) receiving infinite forms.
2
Grasp the opponent's distinction
The opponent splits perceptions into concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat). Concepts are passions; assents are actions because they involve judgment/attribution.
This aims to save the claim that the soul can perform infinite actions.
3
See the author's rejection
The author denies that judgment/attribution is an action. He defines it as the obtaining of a belief that one essence is described by another.
This redefines assent as a passive reception, not an act.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Since assent is just the obtaining of a belief, the soul is receptive, not active. Hence all perceptions are passions, and no infinite actions are implied.
The original argument that the soul can have infinite passions (not actions) stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : هب أن النفس تقوى على إدراكات لا نهاية لها. فلم قلتم : إن الإدراكات فعل ؟ وتقريره : أن إدراك الشيء عندكم : عبارة عن حصول صورة المعقول في العاقل ، وإذا ثبت هذا فيكون الجوهر العاقل قابلا لتلك الصورة العقلية ، لا فاعلا لها . وعندكم : الممتنع على الأجسام أفعال لا نهاية لها . فإنها انفعالات لا نهاية لها، فهذا غير ممتنع عندكم على الجسمانيات. ألا تروا أن الهيولى أزلية . وقد حصل فيها صور متعاقبة لا نهاية لها ؟ .
فإن قالوا : الإدراكات على قسمين : التصورات والتصديقات أما التصورات ، فلا نزاع في كونها انفعالات . وأما التصديقات ، فهي أفعال ، لأنها عبارة عن حكم الذهن بإسناد أحد التصورين إلى الثاني ، وهذا الإستاد فعل . فثبت : أن النفس لما كانت قادرة على التصديقات التي لا نهاية لها ، كانت قادرة على أفعال لا نهاية لها.
فنقول : لا نسلم أن الإسناد والحكم : فعل . وذلك لأنا لا نعقل من هذا الإسناد والحكم ، إلا حصول الاعتقاد بأن إحدى الماهيتين موصوفة بالثانية ، أو مسلوب عنها تلك الثانية . فإذا حصل في الذهن اعتقاد أن كذا موصوف بكذا ، أو غير موصوف به . فهذا هو (٣) الحكم والإسناد. والتصديق هو في الحقيقة ليس إلا عبارة عن حصول هذا الاعتقاد في جوهر النفس فتكون جوهر النفس(۱) مهنا قابلة [ لا فاعلة (٢) ] ويعود التقرير المذكور .
The third question: Suppose that the soul is capable of perceptions without end. Then why do you say that perceptions are an action? Its exposition: The perception of a thing, according to you, is the obtaining of the form of the intelligible in the intellect. And if this is established, then the intelligent substance is receptive of that intelligible form, not an agent of it. And according to you, what is impossible for bodies is actions without end. But passions without end are not impossible according to you for bodies. Do you not see that prime matter (hayula) is eternal, and there have occurred in it successive forms without end? If they say: Perceptions are of two types: concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat). As for concepts, there is no dispute that they are passions. As for assents, they are actions, because they are the mind's judgment of attributing one concept to another, and this attribution is an action. So it is established that when the soul is capable of assents without end, it is capable of actions without end. Then we say: We do not grant that attribution and judgment are an action. That is because we do not conceive of this attribution and judgment except as the obtaining of a belief that one of the two essences is described by the second, or is negated of that second. So when there obtains in the mind a belief that such-and-such is described by such-and-such, or is not described by it, this is (3) the judgment and attribution. And assent in reality is nothing but the obtaining of this belief in the substance of the soul. So the substance of the soul (1) here is receptive, not an agent (2). And the aforementioned exposition returns.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents the third question in a series. The questioner grants that the soul can perform infinite perceptions, but asks why perceptions are considered actions rather than passions. The questioner's exposition: perception is the obtaining of an intelligible form in the intellect, making the intellect receptive, not active. Since bodies can receive infinite passions (like prime matter receiving forms), the soul's infinite perceptions would be passions, not actions, so no contradiction. The opponent then replies that perceptions are of two kinds: concepts (tasawwurat) which are passions, and assents (tasdiqat) which are actions because they involve the mind's judgment and attribution. Thus, if the soul can perform infinite assents, it can perform infinite actions. The author rejects that attribution and judgment are actions: they are merely the obtaining of a belief. Assent is nothing but the obtaining of this belief in the soul's substance, so the soul is receptive, not active. Hence the original argument stands.

الحكم والإسناد

Correction: The author argues that judgment and attribution are merely the obtaining of a belief in the soul, making the soul receptive, not active.

The soul's perceptions, both concepts and assents, are passions because they involve receiving forms or beliefs, not producing them.

Refuting the Divisibility Argument for Finite Bodily Powers
Refutation of the divisibility argument for finitude of bodily powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that bodily powers cannot be infinite because extended things and their states are divisible, and how the author refutes it by challenging the necessity of cessation.

Core Idea
Divisibility does not force finitude; difference in strength can be due to slowness, not finality.

In this passage, the author tackles the fourth question in a series of refutations. The opponent claims that bodily powers cannot perform infinite actions because they reside in divisible bodies. The author systematically deconstructs the argument, accepting some premises but strongly rejecting the conclusion that difference necessarily implies finitude. This lesson will walk you through the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. The opponent argues that bodily powers are finite because every extended thing is divisible, and the state in a divisible thing is divisible, leading to the conclusion that the power of a part is less than the whole, and thus finite.
Notice the compact logical structure.
2
Identify the three premises
The author explicitly lists three premises in the opponent's argument. Find them in the text and understand what each claims.
The first premise relies on denying atomism (al-jawhar al-fard).
3
Understand the author's response to the first two premises
The author mentions that the first premise depends on atomism, which has been discussed earlier. The second premise has already been refuted by eight counterexamples. The author does not need to refute them again here.
These premises are not the main focus of this refutation.
4
Analyze the refutation of the third premise
The author grants that there is a difference in strength between part and whole, but questions why that difference must be due to cessation. The author suggests an alternative: the difference could be due to slowness, even if both powers last forever.
This is the key move: the opponent cannot prove that cessation is the only cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : هب أن النفس تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية ، فلم قلتم : إن القوة الجسمانية لا تقوى على أفعال غير متناهية ؟
قوله : لأن كل متحيز فهو منقسم، والحال في المنقسم منقسم وإذا كان كذلك ، كان مقوى بعض تلك القوة أقل من مقوى كلها ، والأقل من الغير : الامتناعورة من متناه . فمقوي بعض القوة : متناه . فيجب أن يكون مقوي كلها : متناهياً )
فنقول : هذه الحجة تشتمل على مقدمات :
أولها : قوله : ( كل متحيز منقسم ، وهو بناء على نفي الجوهر الفرد . وقد سبق الكلام فيه
وثانيها : ( إن الحال في المنقسم : منقسم ، وقد ذكرنا النقوض الثمانية في الحجة الأولى .
وثالثها : ( إن مقوي بعض القوة ، يجب أن يكون أقل من مقوي كلها ، فنقول : نسلم أنه لا بد من حصول التفاوت بين مقوي البعض وبين مقوي الكل . لكن لم قلتم : إنه لا يحصل التفاوت إلا بأن ينقطع مقوي البعض قبل انقطاع مقوي الكل ؟ [ ولم لا يجوز أن يظهر هذا التفاوت بسبب أن مقوي البعض ، أبطأ من مقوي الكل (٣)] وإن بقي كل واحد منهما أبد الآباد ، وعلى هذه الحالة ؟ .
وبالجملة : فنحن نسلم أنه لا بد من التفاوت . لكن لا نسلم أنه لا يحصل التفاوت ، إلا بانقطاع أثر البعض . فهم مطالبون بإثبات هذه المقدمة .
The fourth question: Suppose that the soul is capable of infinite actions. Then why do you say that the bodily power is not capable of infinite actions? His statement: Because every extended thing is divisible, and the state of what is divisible is divisible. And if that is so, then the strength of some of that power is less than the strength of all of it, and the lesser than the other is impossible? finite. So the strength of some power is finite, so the strength of all of it must be finite. Then we say: This argument includes premises: First: His statement: 'Every extended thing is divisible,' which is based on denying the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). And discussion of it has preceded. Second: 'The state of what is divisible is divisible,' and we have mentioned eight refutations (nuqud) in the first argument. Third: 'The strength of some power must be less than the strength of all of it.' We say: We grant that there must be a difference between the strength of some and the strength of all. But why do you say that the difference only occurs by the strength of some ceasing before the strength of all ceases? And why is it not permissible that this difference appears because the strength of some is slower than the strength of all (3), even if each of them remains forever, in this state? In summary: We grant that there must be a difference. But we do not grant that the difference only occurs by the cessation of the effect of some. They are required to prove this premise.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth question, which challenges the claim that bodily powers cannot perform infinite actions. The opponent argues: every extended thing is divisible, and the state (accident) in a divisible thing is divisible. Therefore, the strength (power) of a part of the power is less than the strength of the whole. The lesser than another must be finite; so part-power is finite, so whole-power is finite. The author refutes by analyzing the premises. First premise (every extended thing is divisible) relies on the denial of the indivisible atom (atomism), which has been discussed before. Second premise (the state in the divisible is divisible) has already been refuted by eight counterexamples in the first argument. Third premise (the strength of part is less than the whole) is granted, but the author questions why the difference must be due to one ceasing before the other. Perhaps the difference is due to slowness, not finite duration. The opponent cannot prove that the difference necessitates finitude; they are required to establish that premise.

Key Terms
متحيز
منقسم
الجوهر الفرد
مقوى
التفاوت
النقوض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The divisibility argument proves that any bodily power must be finite because its parts are finite.
Correction: The argument assumes that difference in strength must be due to cessation, but the author shows that difference could be due to slowness, allowing both parts to be infinite in duration.

Checkpoint
What does the author grant in the third premise of the opponent's argument?
A) That every extended thing is divisible
B) That the state in the divisible is divisible
C) That there must be a difference between the strength of part and whole
D) That the difference is due to cessation of the part

Key Takeaway
The author defeats the divisibility argument by showing that difference in strength does not require finitude; slowness can account for it even if both powers are infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that because you are weaker in some area than others, you are inherently limited, ignoring the possibility that you just need more time and practice? How does this lesson challenge your view of your own potential?
?
The opponent's argument seemed sound but had an unproven premise. Are there beliefs you hold that you have not critically examined for hidden assumptions? What is one belief you can test today?
?
The author grants difference but does not assume it implies finitude. In your life, do you equate difference with deficiency? How can you shift your mindset to see differences as slowness rather than impossibility?

The Fourth Proof: Rational Power Cannot Be in a Bodily Organ
Proves by disjunction that the rational power cannot reside in a bodily organ like heart or brain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the rational power cannot reside in a bodily organ, based on a disjunctive proof.

Core Idea
The rational power cannot be in a bodily organ because perception of its own instrument leads to impossible consequences.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from the classical kalam tradition, which uses a logical conditional to demonstrate that the rational power (al-quwwa al-ʿāqila) cannot be located in a bodily organ such as the heart or brain. The argument proceeds by showing that if it were, perception of that organ would either be perpetual or impossible, both of which are false. This refines our understanding of the immaterial nature of the intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical conditional structure: 'if ... then ...'.
The argument is concise; each part is important.
2
Identify the conditional and its consequent
The passage states: If the rational power were in a bodily organ, then it would either always perceive that organ or never perceive it. Identify these two disjuncts.
The consequent is a disjunction (either ... or ...).
3
Understand the definition of perception
Perception is defined as the obtaining of the quiddity of the known thing in the knower. This definition is crucial for refuting the possibilities.
Perception does not require bodily contact.
4
See why both disjuncts fail
The second disjunct (perception as a form in the power) is declared impossible. The first disjunct (perception as identity with the instrument) is also impossible by implication, making the consequent false.
Thus the antecedent (that the power is in a bodily organ) must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : قالوا : لو كانت القوة العاقلة حالة في آلة جسمانية من قلب أو دماغ ، لوجب أن تكون القوة العاقلة دائمة الإدراك لتلك الآلة ، أو ممتنعة الإدراك لها مطلقا . والتالي باطل . فالمقدم مثله باطل . بيان الشرطية : أن الإدراك عبارة عن حصول ماهية المعلوم في العالم، فلو كانت القوة العاقلة حالة في مادة جسمانية، لكان إدراك القوة العاقلة لتلك الآلة إما أن يكون عين وجود تلك الآلة ، وإما أن يكون عبارة عن حصول صورة مساوية لتلك الآلة في القوة العاقلة . وهذا القسم الثاني : محال
The Fourth Proof: They said: If the rational power were lodged in a bodily instrument like the heart or brain, then the rational power would necessarily be either perpetually aware of that instrument or absolutely incapable of perceiving it. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. Explanation of the conditional: Perception is the obtaining of the quiddity of the known thing in the knower. So if the rational power were lodged in bodily matter, then the perception of that instrument by the rational power would either be the very existence of that instrument, or it would be the obtaining of a form equivalent to that instrument in the rational power. The second division is impossible.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the rational power cannot reside in a bodily organ. The argument uses a logical conditional: If the rational power were in a bodily instrument, then it would either always perceive that instrument or never perceive it. Both possibilities are false, so the premise is false. The explanation: Perception is the presence of the known essence in the knower. If the rational power were in bodily matter, its perception of that instrument would be either identical to the instrument's existence (which is impossible because the subject and object would be identical) or a form of the instrument in the power (which is also impossible because a bodily form in a non-bodily power is contradictory). Thus the second division is impossible, and by implication the first is also impossible, so the consequent is false.

Key Terms
قوة عاقلة
آلة جسمانية
إدراك
ماهية المعلوم
صورة مساوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perception requires some kind of bodily contact or physical impression.
Correction: The passage defines perception as the presence of the known essence in the knower, which does not necessitate material contact. This definition is used to show that even if the power were in a bodily organ, its perception of that organ would not be a physical impression.

Checkpoint
According to the Fourth Proof, what would happen if the rational power were lodged in a bodily organ?
A) It would perceive the organ intermittently.
B) It would either always perceive the organ or never perceive it.
C) It would perceive the organ only during deliberation.
D) It would perceive the organ through a bodily sense.

Key Takeaway
The rational power's independence from bodily organs is proven by showing that its perception of its own instrument would lead to an impossible alternative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to be a thinking being. But do you ever treat your rational power as if it were merely a function of your brain, subject to chemical whims? What does this proof demand you affirm about your own soul?
?
If your rational power is not bound to a bodily organ, then your ability to know truth is not limited by your physical state. How does that change the way you approach moments of fatigue, illness, or distraction?
?
The proof shows that perception is not a physical imprint. When you read the Quran, do you seek to have the 'quiddity of the known' present in your soul, or do you settle for the sounds and letters? What would it take to truly make the meaning present in you?

Fourth Proof: Rational Soul Not in a Physical Organ
The author proves by conditional disjunction that the rational soul cannot reside in a physical organ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditional argument that the rational soul cannot reside in a physical organ, and why both possible outcomes of constant or never-perception are impossible.

Core Idea
The rational soul cannot be in a physical organ without entailing impossible perception patterns.

This lesson examines the fourth proof against the rational soul being lodged in a physical organ like the heart or brain. The argument proceeds by showing that if the soul were in such an organ, then perception of that organ would be either always present or always absent—both of which conflict with our actual experience. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Let the full argument sink in before analysing its parts.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the conditional statement
Find the 'if...then...' structure: 'If the rational power were in a physical organ, then it would be either constantly or never perceiving that organ.' This is the backbone of the proof.
The author calls this the 'shartiyya' (conditional).
3
Analyse the two options for perception
The proof splits perception into two possibilities: either it is the mere presence of the organ, or it is a form similar to the organ. Trace how each is shown impossible.
The second option leads to two similar forms in one matter, which is impossible.
4
Understand why the consequent is false
The proof says we know by necessity that sometimes we bring the heart/brain to mind and sometimes we don't. So the consequent (always perceiving or never perceiving) is false.
This falsity forces the antecedent (soul in organ) to be false.
5
Consider the objection and the author's reply
The author pre-empts an objection about the definition of perception and states that the form-based account is already refuted. Even if accepted, the form is a condition, but the earlier reasoning still holds.
The objection is not fully explained here but references earlier proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : قالوا : لو كانت القوة العاقلة حالة في آلة جسمانية من قلب أو دماغ ، لوجب أن تكون القوة العاقلة دائمة الإدراك لتلك الآلة ، أو ممتنعة الإدراك لها مطلقا . والتالي باطل فالمقدم مثله باطل . بيان الشرطية : أن الإدراك عبارة عن حصول ماهية المعلوم في العالم، فلو كانت القوة العاقلة حالة في مادة جسمانية، لكان إدراك القوة العاقلة لتلك الآلة إما أن يكون عين وجود تلك الآلة ، وإما أن يكون عبارة عن حصول صورة مساوية لتلك الآلة في القوة العاقلة . وهذا القسم الثاني : محال لأن تلك الصورة إذا حلت في القوة العاقلة الحالة في تلك الآلة ، فحينئذ يلزم كون تلك الصورة حالة في تلك الآلة ، فيلزم : اجتماع صورتين متماثلتين في تلك المادة ، والجمع بين المثلين: محال ، ولما بطل هذا القسم الأول ، وهو أن يقال : إن القوة العاقلة ، لو أدركت آلتها لكان إدراكها لتلك الآلة عبارة عن نفس حضور تلك الآلة عند القوة العاقلة . وهذا القدر من الحضور ، إن كان كافياً في حصول الإدراك وجب حصول الإدراك دائماً . وإن لم يكن كافياً ، امتنع حصول الإدراك في شيء من الأوقات . لأنه لو حصل في بعض الأوقات، لكان ذلك بسبب أمر زائد على مجرد حضور صورة الألة ، وذلك الزائد يجب أن يكون عبارة عن حصول صورة مساوية لها في القوة الناطقة ، بناء على القاعدة المشهورة من أن إدراك الشيء عبارة عن حضور صورة مساوية للمعقول في العاقل : وقد بينا أن ذلك محال . فثبت بهذا البيان : أن القوة العاقلة لو كانت حالة في جسم ، كقلب أو دماغ ، لوجب أن تكون مدركة لذلك الجسم دائماً ، أو تكون ممتنعة الإدراك دائمًا . أما بيان أن هذا التالي محال : فلانا نعلم بالضرورة : أنا في بعض الأوقات نستحضر صورة القلب والدماغ في عقولنا ، وفي بعض الأوقات تكون غافلين عن هذا الإستحضار . فثبت بما ذكرنا : صحة الشرطية ، وثبت : فساد التالي ، فيلزم فساد المقدم . والإعتراض : إنا قد ذكرنا الدلائل الكثيرة على أن حصول صورة مسلوبة في تمام الماهية للمعقول في جوهر العاقل محال ، فلا نعيد تلك الدلائل . ثم لئن سلمنا ذلك ، لكن حصول تلك الصورة ، يكون شرطاً لحصول الإدراك.
The fourth proof: They said: If the rational power were located in a physical organ, such as the heart or brain, then the rational power would necessarily be either perpetually perceiving that organ or absolutely prevented from perceiving it. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. Explanation of the conditional: Perception is the obtaining of the essence of the known thing in the knower. So if the rational power were located in a material substance, then the perception of that organ by the rational power would either be identical with the existence of that organ, or it would be the obtaining of a form similar to that organ in the rational power. The second division is impossible, because if that form resides in the rational power which is located in that organ, then it would necessitate that form being located in that organ, thus requiring the conjunction of two similar forms in that matter, and the conjunction of similar things is impossible. And when this division is invalidated, the first division remains: that if the rational power perceived its organ, its perception of that organ would be the very presence of that organ with the rational power. If this amount of presence is sufficient for the occurrence of perception, then perception would necessarily occur always. And if it is not sufficient, then the occurrence of perception would be impossible at any time. For if it occurred at some times, that would be due to something additional beyond mere presence of the form of the organ, and that additional thing must be the obtaining of a form similar to it in the rational power, based on the well-known principle that perception of a thing is the presence of a form similar to the intelligible in the knower. And we have shown that this is impossible. Thus it is established by this explanation that if the rational power were located in a body such as the heart or brain, it would necessarily be either always perceiving that body or permanently failing to perceive it. As for the demonstration that this consequent is impossible: because we know by necessity that at some times we bring to mind the form of the heart and brain in our minds, and at some times we are heedless of this recollection. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: the validity of the conditional, and the falsity of the consequent; therefore the falsity of the antecedent follows. The objection: We have indeed mentioned many proofs that the obtaining of a form completely identical in essence to the intelligible in the substance of the knower is impossible, so we will not repeat those proofs. Then even if we concede that, yet the obtaining of that form is a condition for the occurrence of perception.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that the rational power cannot reside in a physical organ such as the heart or brain. The proof is structured as a conditional: if the rational power were in a physical organ, then it would either always perceive that organ or never perceive it at all. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. The author then explains the conditional. Perception is the obtaining of the known thing's essence in the knower. If the rational power is in a material substance, then its perception of that organ would either be identical with the existence of that organ, or it would be the acquisition of a form similar to that organ in the rational power. The second option is impossible because that form would reside in the rational power, which is in the organ, leading to two similar forms in the same matter, which is impossible. The first option (that perception is the presence of the organ itself) is then examined. If that presence is sufficient for perception, then perception would be constant. If not sufficient, then perception would never occur. Thus the rational power would either always perceive the organ or never perceive it. But we know by necessity that we sometimes call to mind the form of the heart and brain, and sometimes we are oblivious. So the consequent is false, and thus the antecedent is false. Then the author addresses an objection: he has already provided many proofs that the acquisition of a form completely identical in essence to the intelligible in the substance of the knower is impossible, and he will not repeat them. Even if one accepts that, such a form is a condition for perception.

Key Terms
القوة العاقلة
آلة جسمانية
الإدراك
صورة مساوية
الجمع بين المثلين
الشرطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that perception always requires a form in the knower, so the rational soul could still be in a physical organ if perception is defined differently.
Correction: The proof actually considers both possibilities (presence and form). It shows that even if perception is defined as mere presence, it still leads to impossibility. Hence, no definition of perception saves the claim.

Checkpoint
What is the conditional statement in the fourth proof?
A) If the rational power is in a physical organ, then it perceives the organ constantly.
B) If the rational power is in a physical organ, then it never perceives the organ.
C) If the rational power is in a physical organ, then it either always perceives it or never perceives it.
D) If the rational power is not in a physical organ, then perception is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The rational soul cannot reside in a physical organ because doing so would force a pattern of perception (always or never) that contradicts our actual experience of intermittent awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your rational soul is not confined to your brain or heart, then what is your true self? Are you merely a collection of biological processes, or is there something in you that transcends matter? In what moments today did you act only out of physical desire, forgetting your immaterial essence?
?
This proof shows that your awareness of your own body (like the heart or brain) is intermittent. You are not always thinking of your body. So where is your identity when you are not aware of your body? If your body is not your seat, does that change your attachment to it? Will you cling less to comfort and more to the soul's demands?
?
You experience moments of clear thought and then heedlessness. If the soul were physical, that pattern would be impossible. So every time your mind wanders or returns to focus, you are witnessing a proof of your soul's immateriality. Are you living as if you believe that? When you neglect your afterlife, you act as if your soul were nothing more than neurons. What will you change today to align your life with this truth?

Second Objection: Mental Image Equals Object?
Author presents and refutes the equality objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mental image of a thing cannot be identical to the thing itself, and how this refutes the objection of two similars.

Core Idea
A mental image is not the thing itself—otherwise conceiving fire would burn the mind.

This passage addresses a key objection against the immateriality of the rational soul. The opponent argues that if the mental image were different from the object, then two similars would coexist. The author counters by showing that complete equality is absurd, using three clear examples. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's challenge, then three arguments, then a conclusion.
The passage uses 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث' to enumerate the arguments.
2
Identify the three proofs
Each proof shows a different absurd consequence of claiming complete equality between mental image and object. List them in your own words.
The proofs involve God, heaven/earth, and fire/snow.
3
Understand the conclusion
After refuting complete equality, the author explains that without equality, the original problem of two similars disappears.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : أن يقال : أتدعون أنا إذا عقلنا شيئاً ، فإن الصورة الحاضرة في العقل مساوية لذلك المعقول من جميع الوجوه والاعتبارات، أو لا توجبون حصول هذه المساواة من كل الوجوه ؟ والأول لا يقوله عاقل . وتقريره من وجوه : الأول : إنا إذا عقلنا الله تعالى وجب أن تكون الصورة الحاصلة في عقولنا : موجوداً واجب الوجود لذاته ، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل . الثاني : إنا إذا عقلنا السماء والأرض ، فهذه الصورة العقلية : كيفية قائمة بالنفس جزئية . وهذه الكيفية لا تحس ولا تلمس ، والعلم الضروري حاصل بأنها لا تساوي هذا الفلك في جميع أجزاء الماهية والصفات . الثالث : إنه لو كان الأمر كذلك ، فإذا عقلنا النار ، وجب أن تحصل النار في عقولنا . وإذا عقلنا الثلج ، وجب أن يحصل الثلج في عقولنا ، فتكون النار العقلية ، والثلج العقلي، مساوياً للنار والثلج الموجودين في الخارج ، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل . وأما إذا سلمتم : أن الصورة العقلية ، لا يجب أن تكون مساوية لذات المعقول من جميع الوجوه ، فحينئذ لا يلزم من حدوث صورة أخرى في القلب ، أو الدماغ في القوة العاقلة : اجتماع المثلين
The second question: [The opponent] says: Do you claim that when we conceive something, the form present in the intellect is equal to that conceived object in all aspects and considerations, or do you not necessitate the occurrence of this equality in every respect? The first is not said by any rational person. Its elaboration is in several aspects: First: If we conceive God the Exalted, then the form occurring in our intellects would necessarily be an existent necessary of existence by itself, and that is not said by any rational person. Second: If we conceive the heaven and the earth, then this intellectual form is a quality subsisting in the soul, particular. This quality is neither sensed nor touched, and necessary knowledge is obtained that it is not equal to this celestial sphere in all parts of essence and attributes. Third: If the matter were so, then if we conceive fire, fire would necessarily occur in our intellects; and if we conceive snow, snow would necessarily occur in our intellects. Thus the intellectual fire and intellectual snow would be equal to the fire and snow existing externally, and that is not said by any rational person. But if you concede that the intellectual form does not necessarily have to be equal to the essence of the conceived object in all respects, then it does not follow from the occurrence of another form in the heart or the brain in the rational faculty that two similars coexist.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second objection against the claim that the rational soul is a body. The objector asks whether the mental image of a thing is equal to that thing in all respects. The author argues that the first option (complete equality) is absurd, giving three proofs: 1) The concept of God would then be a necessary existent, which is blasphemous. 2) The mental image of the sky is a particular quality, not a physical object. 3) If the mental image were identical to the object, then conceiving fire would produce real fire in the mind. Since these are impossible, the objector must concede inequality, and thus the problem of two similars in the same locus does not arise.

Key Terms
الصورة العقلية
المعقول
اجتماع المثلين

?
The author says the concept of fire doesn't burn. What 'fire' in your life—anxiety, desire, resentment—do you treat as real and overwhelming, when in fact it is only a mental form? How can you remind yourself of this truth today?
